Governor-General

His Excellency the Right Honourable Sir Zelman Cowen, a Member of Her Majesty's Most Honourable Privy Council, Knight of the Order of Australia, Knight Grand Cross of the Most Distinguished Order of St Michael and St George, Knight Grand Cross of the Royal Victorian Order, Knight of the Most Venerable Order of the Hospital of St John of Jerusalem, one of Her Majesty's Counsel learned in the law, Governor-General of the Commonwealth of Australia and Commander-in-Chief of the Defence Force.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: SECOND PERIOD)
Advisory Council for Inter-government Relations Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.7 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Advisory Councilfor Inter-government Relations Act1976.
Airlines Agreement Act 1981 (Act No. 75 of 1981 )An Act to approve the execution of an agreement relating to air transport, and for purposes connected therewith.
Airlines Equipment Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 77 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Airlines Equipment Act 1958.
Airlines Equipment (Loan Guarantee) Act 1981 (Act No. 38 of 1981). An Act relating to guarantees of certain borrowings by a domestic airline.
Antarctic Marine Living Resources Conservation Act 1981 (Act -No. 30 of 1981 ) An Act relating to the conservation of marine living resources of the Antarctic and its surrounding seas.
Apple and Pear Export Underwriting Act 1981 (Act No. 14of 1981 )An Act relating to the underwriting of returns from the export from Australia of apples and pears.
Apple and Pear Stabilization Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 15 of 1981)An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Stabilization Act 1971, to repeal certain related Acts, and for other purposes.
Appropriation Act (No.3) 1980-81 (Act No. 55 of 1981 )An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund, additional to the sum appropriated by the Appropriation Act (No. I) 1980-81, for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1981.
Appropriation Act (No.4) 1980-81 (Act No. 56 of 1981)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund, additional to the sum appropriated by the Appropriation Act (No.2) 1980-81 ,for certain expenditure in respect of the year ending on 30 June 1961.
Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 16 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Act 1973.
Australian Capital Territory Gaming and Liquor Authority Act 1981 (Act No. 116of 1981)An Act to establish a Gaming and Liquor Authority for the Australian Capital Territory and for related purposes.
Australian Federal Police Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 22 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Australian Federal Police Act1979.
Australian National Airlines Repeal Act 1981 (Act No. 78 of 1981)An Act to repeal the Australian National Airlines Actl945 and for related purposes.
Australian National University Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 106 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian National University Act 1946.
Australian Tourist Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 27 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Australian Tourist Commission Act 1967.
Beef Industry (Incentive Payments) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 49 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Beef Industry (Incentive Payments) Act 1977.
Bounty (Non-adjustable Wrenches) Act 1981 (Act No. 18 of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain wrenches.
Bounty (Polyester-Cotton Yarn) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 17 of 1981 )An Act to amend the Bounty (Polyester-Cotton Yarn) Act 1978, and for related purposes.
Bounty (Printed Fabrics) Act 1981 (Act No. 102of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain printed fabrics.
Bounty (Textile Yarns) Act 1981 (Act No. 103 of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain textile yarns.
Broadcasting and Television Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 113 of 1981)An Act to amend the Broadcasting and Television Act 1942, and for related purposes.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Amendment Act 198) (Act No. 114 of 1981 )An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964 to extend it to certain licences.
Census and Statistics Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 48 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Census and Statistics Act 1905.
Christmas Island Agreement Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 107 of 1981)- An Act relating to the Christmas Island Agreement.
Coal Excise Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 19 of 1981)An Act to amend the Coal Excise Act 1949, and for other purposes.
Commonwealth Banks Act 1981 (Act No. 29of 1981).An Act to amend the Commonwealth Banks Act 1959, and for related purposes.
Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 26 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Act 1979.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION-continued

Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Act 1981 (Act No. 74 ofl981 ) An Act to implement certain changes in Commonwealth functions.

Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 62 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission Act1977.

Commonwealth Teaching Service Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.5 of 1981)An Act to amend the Commonwealth Teaching Service Act 1972, and for purposes connected therewith.

Companies Act 1981 (Act No. 89 of 1981 ) An Act to make provision for the government of the Australian Capital Territory in relation to the formation of companies, the regulation of companies formed in that Territory, the registration in that Territory of certain other bodies and certain other matters.
Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.2 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Act 1980.

Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 94 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Act 1980.

Companies (Acquisition of Shares-Fees) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 95 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares-Fees) Act 1980.

Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 4 of 1981)An Act to amend the Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1980.

Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Amendment Act (No. 2) of 1981 (Act No. 98ofl981)An Act to amend the Companies and Securit-ies (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1980.

Companies (Fees) Actl981 (Act No. 90of 1981 ) An Act relating to fees payable for the purposes of the Companies Actl98l.

Companies (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1981 (Act No. 92 of 1981 ) -

.

An Act to make certain amendments consequent upon the enactment of the Companies Act 1981 and for other

purposes.

Companies (Transitional Provisions) Act 1981 (Act No. 91 of 1981 )An Act to enact transitional provisions consequent upon the enactment of the Companies Act 1981.

Complaints (Australian Federal Police) Act 1981 (Act No. 21 of 1981)An Act relating to complaints made in respect of members of the Australian Federal Police, and for related purposes.

Conciliation and Arbitration Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 71 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Conciliation and Arbitration Act 1904.

Crown Debts (Priority) Act 1981 (Act No. 93 of 1981)- An Act relating to the priority of Crown debts.

Currency Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. II of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Currency Act 1965.

Customs Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 64of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901.

Customs Amendment (Securities) Act 1981 (Act No. 67 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Customs Actl901 in relation to securities.

Customs Amendment (Tenders) Act 1981 (Act No. 45 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 to provide for tenders for rights to import goods at concessional rates of duly, and for related purposes.

Customs Securities (Penalties) Act 1981 (A.:t No. 46 of 1981 )An Act to provide for penalties for refusal or failure to give certain securities with respect to the importation into Australia of dutiable goods.

Customs Tariff Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 68 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Customs Tariff Act1966.

Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 66of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Customs Tari.ff(Anti-Dumping) Act 1975.

Customs TarilfValidation Act 1981 (Act No. 87 of 1981 ) An Act to provide for the validation of certain collections of duty of Customs.

Customs Undertakings (Penalties) Act 1981 (Act No. 47 of 1981)An Act to provide for penalties for breaches of undertakings with respect to the importation into Australia of dutiable goods.

Dairying Research Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 12 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Dairying Research Act 1972, and for related purposes.

Designs Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 42 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Designs Act 1906, and for related purposes.

3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2973

Wednesday, 3 June 1981
Mr SPEAKER (Rt Hon. Sir Billy Snedden) took the chair at 2.15 p.m., and read prayers.
PETITIONS
The Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will be referred to the appropriate Ministers:
Tertiary Education
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. This petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
(i) the financial situation of students enrolled at Australian post-secondary institutions is already extremely difficult (as indicated in the Commonwealth Department of Education's 1979 survey of student incomes and expenditure) and any substantial increase in the financial commitments of students would gravely worsen their financial difficulties.
(ii) the imposition of post-secondary tuition fees of comparable levels to those imposed prior to 1971 would force many currently enrolled students to discontinue their studies and deter many persons willing and able at present to undertake post-secondary study from doing so.
(iii) those students and potential students from social groups currently under-represented and disadvantaged in the post-secondary education system would be most severely affected by the re-introduction of fees for post-secondary education.
Your petitioners therefore pray that:
I. post-secondary tuition fees not be re-introduced for any courses or for any category of students.
2. the visa charges upon private overseas students be removed immediately.
3. the Minister for Education immediately clarify the Government's attitude to the re-introduction of postsecolldary tuition fees.
And YOllr petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Dr Blewett, Dr Cass, Mr Cunningham, Mr Free, Mr Fry, Mr Howe, Mr Hurford, Dr Jenkins, Mr Barry Jones, Mrs Kelly, MI' Kent, Mr Mildren, Mr Milton, Mr Morrison, Mr Mountford and Mr Wallis.
Petitions received.
Tertiary Education
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. This petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
(i) the financial situation of students enrolled at Australian post-secondary institutions is already extremely difficult (as indicated in the Commonwealth Department of Education's 1979 survey of student incomes and expenditure) and any substantial increase in the financial commitments of students would gravely worsen their financial difficulties.
(ii) the imposition of post-secondary tuition fees of comparable levels to those imposed prior to 1974 would force many currently enrolled students to discontinue their studies and deter many persons willing and able at present to undertake post-secondary study from doing so.

(iii) those students and potential students from social groups currently under-represented and disadvantaged in the post-secondary education system would be most severely affected by the reintroduction of fees for post-secondary education.
Your petitioners therefore pray that:
(i) post-secondary tuition fees not be reintroduced for any courses or for any category ofstudents
(ii) the imposition of visa charges upon private overseas students be ceased and desisted from immediately.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr N. A. Brown and Mr Duffy.
Petitions received.
Tertiary Education
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in the Parliament assembled. This Petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
1. The proposal to introduce fees for higher and second degrees will mean that access to post-graduate and recurrent education will be limited to the rich; Australian research and ability to cope with technological change will suffer; this proposal must be seen as a step in the direction of the introduction of fees for all tertiary students.
2. the proposed introduction of a loans scheme for students will mean graduating with a heavy debt or being denied a tertiary education if loans are refused because a student is seen as a 'poor risk' (particularly affecting women and those doing non-vocational courses); only the rich will be able to get a tertiary education without course restrictions and the burden of indebtedness.
3. in regard to the proposal to restrict eligibility for TEAS and the foreshadowing of reviewing TEAS levels only in the light of the availability of loans, it should be noted that any further downgrading of TEAS, which is already inadequate, can only further limit access to education.
4. the proposed restriction of Adult Secondary Assistance to those needing qualifications for employment will severely restrict access to a full high school education and necessary bridging education to tertiary education for those who left school early.
5. the proposal not to fund 30 Colleges of Advanced Education if they are not rationalised is an attack on teacher education. This attack will create teacher shortages in the mid 1980s, restrict access to education now and endanger the future quality of teaching available to Australian children.
Your petitioners therefore pray that:
(i) post-secondary tuition fees not be introduced for any courses or for any category of students;
(ii) that the TEAS scheme be extended so as to more fully meet the real financial needs of students.
(iii) that a comprehensive loans scheme not be introduced. (iv) that there be no restriction of Adult Secondary
Assistance; (v) that the proposal not to fund 30 CAEs unless they are
rationalized be rescinded.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Beazley, Mr N. A. Brown, Mr Robert Brown, Mr Charles, Mrs Child, Mr Cunningham, Mrs Darling, Mr Free, Mr Holding, Mr Barry Jones, Mr Milton, Mr Morrison and Dr Theopltanous.
Petitions received.

Petitions

Trans-Australia Airlines
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble Petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
Many Australians depend almost exclusively upon Trans-Australia Airlines for the provision of essential air services.
The Federal Liberal Party policy objective of selling Trans-Australia Airlines within 4 years is contrary to the national interest.
Sale of Trans-Australia Airlines would inevitably result in:
(i) the creation of a private monopoly of major airline services in Australia;
(ii) higher domestic airfares.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will reject outright any proposal to sell Trans-Australia Airlines.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Charles, Mrs Child, Mr Hurford, Mr Jacobi, Mr Charles Jones, Mr Kent, Mr Mildren, Mr Morris, Mr Mountford and Mr Scott.
Petitions received.
Excise on Beer
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble Petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
1. The hotel industry in Australia is long-established and a valuable part of the community of this nation, providing places where the people may partake of the three basic needs of life; a place to eat, a place to drink, a place to sleep.
2. The hotel industry is accepted throughout the nation as occupying an important place in the social and economic activities of Australia.
3. The hotel industry is almost entirely within the private sector and makes a large contribution to the government in taxes of various kinds.
4. The hotel industry has a need to sustain an appropriate level of turnover and growth, to make profit, and to sustain and improve the level of employment.
5. The hotel industry of Australia is gravely concerned at published reports that the Government intends to increase excise charges on beer in the forthcoming Budget.
6. Sales of beer constitute the major source of income of most of the industry's 5,811 hotels in Australia.
7. The very steep rises in beer excise in 1975 and 1978 severely retarded growth of the Australian hotel industry.
8. Any further increases in excise on beer in the 1981 Budget would further retard the industry at a time when growth is essential to maintaining economic viability and the ability to serve the constantly growing needs of the community.
9. Such is its significance as a community requirement, beer occupies a place on the Consumer Price Index, and any alteration to its cost to the community would have an inflationary effect.
10. Any increase in excise would result in and force the hotel industry to pass on such increases to the consumer by way of higher prices for beer.
11. Higher excise would be only the starting point for an upward spiral in beer prices because of the presence of other

factors, such as State Licence Fee taxes, which are directly linked to excise rates.
12. The hotel industry of Australia already is gravely handicapped by heavy excise and other tax imposts, its growth is at a standstill; and, inevitably must decline if beer excise is increased.
Your Petitioners therefore humbly pray that Honourable Members of the House of Representatives:
1. Recognise the importance and place of the Australian hotelindustry in the social and economic life of the nation.
2. Accept that the viability of the hotel industry is essential to the maintenance ofemployment opportunities and stability of the industry is essential to enable it to continue to provide for the hotel needs of the nation in this period of growing population and increasing tourism.
And further, that Honourable Members of the House of Representatives resist and reject any move to increase excise on beer.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Adermann, Mr John Brown, Mr Fisher, Sir Phillip Lynch and Mr Thomson.
Petitions received.

Toxic Shock Syndrome
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned showeth:
That your petitioners are gravely concern~d by the threats to women's health and lives posed by Toxic Shock Syndrome, and urge the following steps to be taken as a matter of urgency:

Petitions

3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2975

(1) Tampons, sanitary napkins, vaginal deodorants and similar sanitary products to be re-classified under Therapeutic Agents (not under 'Cosmetics' as at present), in order to bring them under the scrutiny of the Australian Drug Evaluation Committee. We believe manufacture must be under the strictest hygienic conditions.
(2) After re-classification, we urge an immediate government-funded inquiry into all aspects of manufacture and content of these products. This must lead to: (i) contents of product and instructions for correct usage to be shown on packaging; (ii) removal of synthetic fibres, chemicals or ingredients that are potentiallyor actually harmful to users.
(3) Allocation of extra funding for ongoing research by the National Health and Medical Research Council into these products, results of this research to be made public to cnsure all health workers and potential users are fully informed. This campaign of public education would necessitate multilingual distribution of the information where appropriate.
Your petitioners, therefore, humbly request that Parliament take immediate and positive action to avert possible threats posed to women's health by implementation of the above measures.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Child.

Privacy and Personal Information
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
Paragraphs 107 and 108 of the Privacy and Personal Information discussion paper number 14, brought down by the Law Reform Commission in JUlle 1980, undermines the family unit and the authority of parents, which is the Christian basis of OUf society, by proposing that 'between 12 and 16 years, a child should be entitled to object to disclosure of information to a parent '.
It is your petitioners sincere belief that:
(a) an adolescent between the ages of 12 and 16 is not at a mature enough stage of life to decide whether or not the disclosure of information to a parent will be in the best interests of the adolescent.
(b) as a record keeper has no responsibility for the child, and as his moral standards may be different to those of a parent, he should not be permitted to come between child and parent.
(c) a parent has a unique position which may enable him/her to perceive a situation that the record keeper does not. The parent must therefore be aware of the problem if remedial action is to be taken, and has the right to guide the child in the direction he/she feels fit.
Your petitioners therefore pray:

2976 REPRESENTATIVES 3 June 1981

Petitions

That the Members of the House of Representatives will reject the recommendation of the Law Reform Commission and find some other means to protect children from the excessive abuse of some parents.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Dobie.

Proposed New Constitution
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That we believe that Australia's constitution is undemocratic and should be replaced by a democratic constitution, particularly as regards the new standard for regulation of Vitamin and Mineral Supplements.
We believe: 1. This legislation will curb our individual freedom to care
for our own health according to need, environment, climate, et cetera. 2. These substances have proven their efficacy in maintaining health rather than destroying it and should remain freely available. 3. Why curb these substances while other noxious and toxic substances are freely available to the obvious thorough detriment of our society-e.g. commercial alcohol and tobacco.
This new constitution should be drafted at a representative directly elected people's convention following extensive public debate, and then put to a referendum of the people. The petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Parliament, as a matter of urgency, will help to promote such public debate and will arrange for the holding of such a people's convention and referendum.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Good luck.

Defence Service Home Loans
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That all members of the Women's Services 1939-45 War qualify as 'Australian Soldier' under the Defence Service Homes Act for loans because on voluntarily joining the Australian Forces they took the oath to resist His Majesty's enemies making themselves available for combat duty in War Zones, and according to Acts and Regulations of the period the Military women had enlisted for service anywhere, some Naval women to sail in any waters, with all servicewomen available for service in Papua New Guinea.
As those members of the Royal Australian Navy, the Australian Imperial Force and the Royal Australian Air Force of 1939-45 War who were not sent to War Zones are granted loans, refusal to grant eligibility to all Ex-servicewomen of 1939-45 War is unjust and unfair.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that you amend the Defence Service Homes Act by repealing Sub-section 4 (2A) (b) (c) (d) of the Principal Act as amended by No. 137 of 1978, and grant eligibility to all members of the Women's Royal Australian Naval Service, the Australian Women's Army Service, the Women's Auxiliary Australian Air Force, the Australian Army Medical Women's Service, and the Voluntary Aid Detachment of 1939-45 War.

And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Jarman.

Plant Variety Rights
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled.
We, the undersigned citizens of the Commonwealth, do humbly pray that the Commonwealth Government:
I. Note that legislation establishing plant breeders' rights in other countries has had serious adverse effects, namely: (a) Virtual monopoly control of seed production has passed into the hands of a few large international corporations seeking to profit from the exclusive rights over plant genetic materials created by such legislation. (b) The varieties of seeds available have been restricted mainly to hybrids which will not reproduce truly and will not grow without the aid of artificial fertilisers and pesticides, thus maximising corporate profits without regard for the interests of growers and consumers. (c) The genetic diversity of crops has been eroded, rendering them vulnerable to disease and other environmental threats.
2. Recognise that maintenance of the genetic diversity of plant varieties is crucial to the continued well-being of the Australian nation, and take all necessary steps to preserve and promote such genetic diversity as a public resource and to prevent exclusive control over plant genetic materials from falling into private hands.
3. Defend the vital interests of Australian farmers and gardeners, independent Australian seed companies and their employees, and consumers of Australian farm and garden produce, by rejecting any proposal to legislate for the establishment of plant breeders' rights in Australia.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Les Johnson.
Petition received.

Sydney (Kingsford-Smith) Airport
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of we, the undersigned citizens of Australia, respectfully showeth:
That we oppose any expansion of the facilities of Kingsford-Smith Airport which entails the building of a new runway as it would have the following detrimental effects:
(1) The loss of one mile of waterfront including Lady Robinson's Beach and a huge part of Botany Bay;
(2) The loss of up to 1,230 houses and a drop in value of remaining neighbouring properties;
(3) The creation of more noise pollution in the area;
(4) The creation of more traffic congestion on streets leading to and from the airport;
(5) The forced diversion of Cook's River and further damage to the ecology of the area.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Federal Government will not consider satisfying the airport needs of Sydney by extending Kingsford-Smith Airport and that any decisions related thereto are not taken before there is an opportunity for adequate consultation with any community particularlyaffected.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Les Johnson.

Unemployment Benefit

The immediate and unconditional withdrawal of any proposals to deny Unemployment Benefits to unemployed people under 18 years old.

Interest Rates
The Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned respectfully showeth that:
(1) Interest rates, rather than falling by 2 per cent as promised by the Prime Minister in November, 1977, are now between 2 per cent and 5 per cent higher than when that promise was made, and are at historically higher levels.
(2) This prevents many potential home buyers from borrowing to purchase a house; causes severe hardship to many people who are paying off a housing loan, causing some to default and lose their home; prevents or restrains the establishment or expansion of many businesses; and is directly responsible for increasing numbers of bankruptcies.

COMMONWEALTH-STATE HOUSING AGREEMENT
Mr HOWE-My question is directed to the Minister for Housing and Construction. I seek clarification of his confusing answer to a question yesterday concerning the net value of Commonwealth payments to the States for housing. I ask the Minister: Is it a fact that total State repayments of advances and interest to the Commonwealth in 1980-81 amount to $200.3m? Is it also a fact that the base payment for the States and Territories for housing in 1981-82 and the subsequent four years is only $200m, of which $54m is for special groups in need? Is it a further fact that in 1981-82 New South Wales will receive $45.5m of loan funds for housing but will pay back $56m? Does that not mean that New South Wales will pay back more than it receives from the Commonwealth in 1981-82? Will the Minister confirm that next year Victoria and South Australia will also be paying back more than they receive for housing under the Commonwealth-State Housing Agreement?
Mr McVEIGH-The answer to the first two questions is yes. As to the other questions, surely the honourable member can understand this simple fact of life: Since 1945 the Commonwealth Government has made available to the States funds which have been topped up by State funds and surpluses accruing to the States because of concessional rates of interest on which loans have been made available. Last year in Australia $630m was made available for housing. Net profit accruing to the States was $142m. Payments. to the Commonwealth are not made by the States; they are made by the people who pay rent and repay loans granted for home purchase. In reply to the last part of the question, I say that the funds available for next year will be decided in the total Budget context.
COMMONWEALTH GRANTS COMMISSION REPORTS
Mr HARRIS-My question is directed to the Prime Minister. When are the Commonwealth Grants Commission reports due? Has the Victorian Premier-elect asked for an extra meeting between the States and the Commonwealth to discuss those reports, and has such a meeting been agreed to?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-There has been some doubt about the precise date on which we

2980 REPRESENTATIVES 3 June 1981

Questions Without Notice

will have available, not only to ourselves but also to the States, the Commonwealth Grants Commission report examining the relativities of Commonwealth payments to the respective States. As we are advised, it will be available some time in June. On present advice, we will probably have the report in time for people to look at it but not to examine it before the Premiers Conference on 19 June. I think it would probably be agreed that substantive decisions will not be able to be made at that meeting because, clearly, all State governments and the Commonwealth will want to examine the document very closely. At this point, nobody has any idea of the recommendations that might be contained in it, and whether or not they could pose particular complications for some States. As a result of that, Mr Thompson, the Premier-elect of Victoria, asked the Commonwealth whether it would be possible to schedule a special Premiers Conference to discuss these matters. I have told him that I think that is the only way to handle it and that such a conference will have to be held. We will need some flexibility about the date, but it would need to be some time during the winter.
PERMANENT BUILDING SOCIETY LOANS
Mr LES JOHNSON-I ask the Minister for Housing and Construction: Has the total value of housing loans provided by permanent building societies fallen from $91m in April 1980 to $57m in April this year? Has the number of building society loans fallen from 2,963 in April 1980 to 1,953 this year? Since permanent building societies provide one-third of all housing loans, does the Minister accept this decline as a serious deterioration in the availability of finance for private home buyers? Is this a major reason why home ownership has fallen to the lowest level for a decade?
Mr McVEIGH-The answer to the first and second parts of the question is yes. When we look at the amount of funds made available to permanent building societies, we see that again the figures are correct, and I congratulate the honourable gentleman for at least doing his homework. However, I question his figure for the percentage of funds to total housing made available by permanent building societies. From the information given to me at 2 o'clock, the figure was 28 per cent, not 33 per cent.
I indicate to the honourable member for Hughes that when we look at the totality of funds made available for housing we find that for the March quarter this year, compared with the March quarter last year, there was a slight drop of

3 per cent. However, we must remember that last year was a record year for loans made available to home owners-240,345--and of those people 106,000 were buying their first home.
On account of the responsible financial decisions made by my colleague the Treasurer, in December last year there was a return to the traditional lending and borrowing patterns in Australia. Because of a distortion in the money markets more money was made available in 1980 to permanent building societies than to banks. We have returned to the traditional ratio of 63 per cent for banks and 28 per cent for permanent building societies. We have to look at the total picture of finance made available for housing.
Again I question the accuracy of the honourable gentleman's figures. The total figures for home ownership in Australia show that this Government can claim a proud record. In 1949, 53 per cent of Australian people owned their own homes or were in the process of purchasing them. In August last year, from the most recent statistics made available by the Australian Bureau of Statistics, 71.9 per cent of Australian people owned their own homes. That is a record that this free enterprise Government can be proud of.
We are about home ownership for all Australian people. We do not subscribe to the proposition adopted by those who sit opposite. They should recall that one of their previous housing ministers said: 'We do not believe in home ownership. That will make a nation of capitalists'. I sat on the Opposition side of the chamber and listened to a former Minister for Housing say: 'We want to cut costs in Australian housing. We will make Australian people own smaller homes'. We do not subscribe to that proposition.
TELECOMMUNICATIONS DISPUTE Mr CADMAN--My question is addressed to the Minister for Communications. What is the current situation in regard to the Telecom Australia dispute? Are there any signs of an early settlement or will there be continuing reduction and disruption of services?
Mr SINCLAIR-Discussions are still continuing. Just before I came into the House, I endeavoured to determine what the prospects were for the breakdown of services. With respect to the international network, I understand that people trying to plug into the Sydney Overseas Telecommunications Commission network switch are finding it a little difficult getting through the trunk arrangements, but, with the exception of a few countries, the international subscriber dialling service is still functioning effectively. Where it is not

Questions Without Notice

3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2981

operating, it is possible through reference to a trunk operator to obtain connection to most countries.
However, within Australia there has been a breakdown of a number of trunk systems and that is of major concern to the Government. The Government is very conscious that within the conciliation and arbitration procedures there is a fair and equitable opportunity for the representatives of the two unions to pursue their claims. It is totally opposed to the implementation of bans. A number of personnel have been stood down as a result.
The Government is obviously very anxious that services should be resumed at the earliest possible moment. However, it does not believe that in conditions where the service continues to deterioriate it can remain completely at arm's length from the dispute. Therefore, 1 have initiated a number of approaches to Telecom management to try to ascertain the exact state of play. I hope that out of the discussions before the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission it is possible for the dispute to be settled. If not, the Government will of course have to consider what measures it needs to take to ensure a resolution of the dispute even if those steps lead to having to close down Telecom.

TELECOMMUNICATIONS DISPUTE Mrs KELLY-Is the Minister for Communications aware of the survey undertaken by Telecom Australia management and the Australian Telecommunications Employees Association of comparably qualified tradesmen in various areas of public employment and accepted by Telecom management as a representative survey? Does the Minister agree that the survey shows Telecom employees as being amongst the very lowest paid of those employed by the 25 employers in the survey and that, for instance, tradesmen employed by the South East Queensland Electricity Board are paid 16 per cent or $34 a week more than tradesmen with the same qualifications employed by Telecom? Does the Minister agree with the management of Telecom that this is not an acceptable position for Telecom and its employees in this industry which is characterised by high technology and unquestioned financial capacity and which is of critical importance to the Australian community?
Mr SINCLAIR-J am very indebted to the honourable member for Canberra for having demonstrated how generous the tax reimbursementarrangements entered into by this Government have been to the State governments. Indeed, it is true that particularly the New South Wales Government, but to a degree the Victorian Government, for example through the Victorian power authority, and the other State governments have entered into arrangements outside the wage indexation guidelines which have created enormous problems for the 80 per cent of employees who are employed under the private sector in this nation. The honourable lady referred to a specific questionnaire relating to a number of parallel wages paid within selected areas of Public Service employment. Had that survey ranged across the whole breadth of employment, taking into

account private sector as well as public sector employers, she would find that there are many areas in the private sector where the wage disparity with Telecom looms in favour of the Telecom employees. The concern we have is for the whole nation and not just for those who work within the public sector.
KING'S HALL: REMOVAL OF DEMONSTJUTORS
Mr COLEMAN-My question is directed to the Minister for Administrative Services as the Minister responsible for the Australian Federal Police. Will he inform the House whether the police used brutality in removing demonstrators from King's Hall yesterday afternoon?
Mr NEWMAN--I do not think I have to remind all honourable members of the very unruly and, I must say, unnecessary demonstration that took place at the entrance to Parliament House yesterday. To answer directly the honourable member's question, let me say that I believe that the police acted with considerable restraint in the face of a very vigorous, hostile crowd that physically set out to attack the police. I reject utterly some allegations that have been made by members and senators of the Opposition that the police acted brutally or with undue hostility to the crowd. I have taken the trouble to'--
Mr Barry Jones-Undue hostility?
Mr NEWMAN--Without any hostility to the crowd. I make no qualification at all. I have taken the trouble to look at some of the video tapes of the incident. I have spoken to reporters who were there. They watched the police ask the demonstrators to leave King's Hall and then physically try to remove them.
Those tapes show many incidents that demonstrate what I was saying about the hostile nature of that crowd. One policeman was having his hair pulled, and was being pushed and pulled headlong down the steps of Parliament House. If they had got him there, there would have been a possibility of quite serious injury. A young woman police officer was being punched in the chest and crushed against the doors of Parliament House by the crowd.
The police were acting under the orders of their superior officer on this occasion. They were asked to act with physical restraint. They were acting under the principles of common law. I say once again that I reject the allegations made by some honourable members opposite about the actions of the police. I go further, and commend the police for the most considerate way in which they

dealt with these people under undue provocation which mean~ physical injury.
TELECOMMUNICATIONS DISPUTE
Mr HAWKE-My question is directed to the Minister for Communications. Firstly, did the Acting Chairman of Telecom Australia, Mr May, write to the Minister on 16 April seeking approval to negotiate with the Australian Telecommunications Employees Association in respect of the union's wage claims? Secondly, is it a fact that by the time the matter was called on before Commissioner Clarkson on25 May Telecom management had received no reply from the Minister? Thirdly, I ask whether it is a fact that in these circumstances Telecom, having acknowledged that Telecom employees have a legitimate claim for a significant wage increase, put on record before the Commissioner the following words:
The recent decision of the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission on wage indexation guidelines does not provide a ready basis for advancing such a claim.
Finally, as Telecom management and the unions believe that a significant wage increase should be negotiated and as they, together with the arbitration commissioner, believe that this cannot be achieved appropriately under the guidelines, why does the Minister persist in a course of action which is against the public interest and which Telecom management believes is against the interests of Telecom?
Mr SINCLAIR-The answer to the first part of the honourable gentleman's question is yes. The answer to the second part in respect of contact with the Acting Chairman of Telecom is that verbal discussions were held with both the Acting Chairman and members of the executive of Telecom regarding the details of the basis on which negotiations could proceed. Thirdly, with respect to the whole of the basis of public interest which the honourable gentleman seeks to assert, I point out to him, as I did in answer to the honourable member for Canberra, that it is imperative that the interests of the whole Australian community be taken into account.
In this present dispute, while there was a review analysing comparable wage levels between members of some sectors of the Public Service and Telecom, that review did not embrace the private sector. It did not embrace wage levels paid to people in similar wage and salary classifications in areas where people were called on to do similar jobs and where the wage levels do not show the wage disparity which appears in the review undertaken by Telecom and its employees. Fourthly, it is true that within the conciliation and arbitration system there is a capacity to refer to an anomalies

procedure through which there is an ability for the genuine public interest and the interests of the employees of Telecom to be properly taken into account. It is in that forum that the Governm mt believes wage negotiations should occur and it is within that forum that the Government has been encouraging both Telecom and its employees to resolve the present dispute.
SHEEP SHEARING
Mr FISHER-The Minister for Science' and Technology would be aware that a search for a safe, reliable and effective chemical for the harvesting of wool has been carried on throughout the world for decades, particularly in the United States of America and the Soviet Union. Is the Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation involved in such research into finding an alternative to the manual shearing of sheep? If so, what progress has been made?
Mr THOMSON-The Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation has been engaged in research on biological defleecing for some time. This has been sponsored by the Australian Wool Corporation. It has been a most interesting and successful piece of research so far. The research has been aimed at finding a substance which, when injected, will cause a break in the fleece. The fleece can then be pulled off either manually or automatically.
Mr Young-Round the paddock.
Mr THOMSON-I hope the honourable member for Port Adelaide will be interested in this great example of Australian science.
Mr SPEAKER-The honourable member for Port Adelaide will remain silent. The Minister will proceed with his answer and ignore the interjection from the honourable member for Port Adelaide.
Mr THOMSON-CSIRO has discovered a protein which causes shedding of the fleece in about 48 hours. The protein is a substance called epidermal growth factor which, very fortunately, occurs naturally in body tissues in small quantities. The advantage of this natural occurrence is that there is unlikely to be any drug residue problems or harmful side effects. The next step in this research is field evaluation and development of management techniques which will take some time. I hope that honourable members will join me in congratulating CSIRO on what appears to be a breakthrough in what has been world-wide research.

UNITED STATES: NUCLEAR WEAPONS Mr SCOTT-My question is addressed to the
Prime Minister. I refer to extensive reports that the United States has introduced nuclear weapons to Japan which is contrary to law and without informing or consulting the Japanese Government which is contrary to an oral agreement to consult in advance. I also refer to the official response by the United States to questions on this issue which are that it neither confirms nor denies the presence of nuclear weapons in any circumstances. Has this issue been discussed with the United States Administration? What assurances has the Prime Minister been given that similar non-binding oral undertakings given to Australia in relation to B52s will not also be evaded?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-Japanese and United States relations have not been discussed between Australia and the United States because we happen not to be responsible for the policies of Japan or responsible for relations between Japan and the United States. We are responsible for relations between Australia and the United States. In matters of concern and interest to this Government and to the Australian people I have no doubt that the United States's word can be relied on.
FRENCH NUCLEAR TESTING Mr SHIPTON-Has the Minister for Foreign Affairs seen reports that the French Government intends to continue underground nuclear testing in the Pacific? Is this true? Does the Australian Government agree with the French Government policy? Does this mean that French policy in the Pacific has not changed under the new President of that country? Finally, will countries in the South Pacific be concerned?
Mr STREET-I have seen a statement attributed to sources in the French ministry of defence which appears to indicate that the French Government does plan to resume underground nuclear testing in the South Pacific. If this in fact happens, I greatly regret it. It would be all the more disappointing coming so soon after an announcement made in Paris a few days ago, on 29 May, that testing would be suspended pending a review of the French nuclear weapons program. The' Australian Government has consistently made clear its opposition to France's nuclear testing program in the South Pacific. Of course, we have devoted considerable efforts in working actively for a ban on the testing of all nuclear weapons.

COMMONWEALTH HEADS OF GOVERNMENT MEETING
Mr ARMITAGE-I refer the Prime Minister to his remarks in the House last week in which he strongly defended his decision to send five Ministers and six back benchers to deliver personal invitations to 40 Commonwealth political leaders to attend the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting in Melbourne in September. I ask: Has the Prime Minister's attention been drawn to wide editorial criticism of the Government's decision in the Australian Press? In particular, has he noted the comments in an editorial in today's Sydney Morning Herald which describes the decision as an affront and the Prime Minister's public explanation of it as eyewash? Finally, in view of the Government's professed determination to impose austerity in government spending, will the Prime Minister now abandon his expensive and unnecessary scheme and leave the matters of conference invitations and agenda discussions to the Commonwealth Secretariat and our own diplomatic representatives in the various countries?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-I have advised the House on more than one occasion of the reasons for the Government's decisions. Those decisions stand. But, for the benefit of the honourable gentleman, let me repeat the arguments and the reasons. Over the last decade when the Canadian Government or the United Kingdom Government was responsible for holding the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting it adopted the practice of sending people to some Commonwealth countries for the very reasons that we have adopted-to seek the views of other countries about the nature of the agenda, the sensitive issues that might have to be handled and what progress the Commonwealth can make in contributing to a resolution of significant issues. The one difference on this occasion is that instead of sending emissaries to some Commonwealth countries-a practice which we believe was not in the best interests of the unity of the Commonwealth, in which all members, large and small, have a significant part to play-we are sending people to all Commonwealth countries. This is part of the process of building into the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting an agenda which has had an input from all Heads of Government from all Commonwealth countries, wherever they may be.
Earlier, I advised the House that at the end of July I will be having discussions in the United Kingdom with the Secretary-General, Mr Ramphal, who very strongly supports this process of consultation before the conference begins. On this basis the program will continue. I do not think

Opposition members interjecting Mr SPEAKER-Order! The honourable members for Melbourne, Hawker, Hotham and Hindmarsh have interjected continually throughout Question Time. I ask all four honourable members to remain silent; otherwise I will have to deal with them. Mr ANTHONY-The oil from Bass Strait comes from what are known as old wells and new wells. I mentioned that the old wells attract 50 per cent of the world parity price. Any new fields, developed since September 1975, attract the world parity price. The previous Labor Government decided that those new wells would attract the world parity price, so it ill becomes the Leader of the Opposition now to complain of the returns that those companies might be getting for oil because it is partly Labor's policy that is giving them that return.
FARM OVERDRAFTS Mr HICKS-I ask the Treasurer whetper, in view of the very high interest rates operating for overdrafts and farm loans, now in the vicinity of 16 per cent, he will as a matter of urgency consult the New South Wales Government to see whether it will immediately lift or completely eliminate the iniquitous 1t per cent levy it places on high interest rate loans. Mr HOWARD-The loan instrument duty which is imposed by the New South Wales Government has been in operation in that State for a number of years. It is a revenue measure which I think is duplicated in most, if not all, the other States of Australia. Whilst I share very much the concern of the honourable member for Riverina about the level of interest rates charged on overdrafts around Australia, and of course noting his particular interest in the rural communities of Australia, I would have thought that the New South Wales Government could make a more constructive contribution towards moderating interest rates, not only in that State but also throughout the rest of Australia-a moderation that might not involve the forgoing of a significant source of revenue for that State. I refer in particular to the capacity of the New South Wales Government to moderate significantly the borrowing appetite of its authorities and to cooperate with the Commonwealth Government in bringing about a sustainable and proper reduction in the size of the total public sector borrowing requirement.

TAXATION: MINERS' HOUSING Mr HUMPHREYS-My question, which I direct to the Treasurer, refers to the crippling strikes on the central Queensland coal fields in 1978 and again last year in response to the Government's decision to tax the housing provided to miners. Does the Treasurer recall that in order to settle this dispute he promised in a statement on 13 October last year to amend the Income Tax Act so that only one-tenth of the market rental value of the miners' accommodation would be subject to tax? Does the Treasurer also recall saying in his statement that until the law was changed the miners would continue to be assessed on the basis of the existing law? Does the Treasurer intend to introduce these promised amendments into the Parliament this session? If not, does this mean that if the law is 110t changed by 30 June the Commissioner of Taxation will continue to tax the Queensland miners on the value of their housing, thus precipitating another wasteful and prolonged dispute on the coal fields? Mr HOWARD-I recall both that strike and the terms of settlement and the statement that I made in October oflast year. Mr Dawkins-Which one? Mr HOWARD-The one to which the honourable member's colleaguereferred. The legislation will not be introduced during this session. I can inform the honourable gentleman that I had already anticipated the possibility of this question arising. I have discussed the matter with the Commissioner of Taxation, who is responsible for the administration of the Act. On the basis of that discussion, I can say to the honourable gentleman that it is. plainly unthinkable that the law would be administered in a way contrary to the clearly stated intention and understandings embodied in that statement in October.
MOTOR VEHICLES: LIQUEFIED PETROLEUM GAS
Mr GOODLUCK-Is the Deputy Prime Minister aware that motor vehicles can and do run economically and efficiently on liquefied petroleum gas? In view of the stated objective of the Government to encourage alternatives to the petrol-driven motor vehicle, will the Government consider, firstly, converting all Commonwealth Government vehicles to LPG; seoondly, introducing incentives to local motor vehicle manufacturers to offer as an option a dual-powered vehicle-petrol and LPG; thirdly,. calling for an inquiry into the reasons why the efficient Datsun LPG-only vehicle failed and was condemnedso

Sinai Peacekeeping Force

3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2987

unfairly in the market place; and fourthly, encouraging more LPG filling stations throughout Australia, supported by constructive publicity?
Mr ANTHONY-I am not a salesman for Datsun; nor can I explain why that company's liquefied petroleum gas conversion car was not a success. However, I can make some inquiries and advise the honourable member. Certainly it is the Government's policy to encourage LPG as an alternative type of fuel. We have been encouraging people to convert. We have been using Commonwealth vehicles as an example. I know that in some of the capital cities a considerable number of vehicles in the Commonwealth fleet have been converted to LPG. I think all these vehicles are capable of running on either LPG or gasolene. One of the encouraging features about LPG is the recently reduced international price. I note that the price from Saudi Arabia and some of the other Gulf states has decreased by about $50 a tonne by the recent announcements at the meeting of the Organisation of Petroleum Exporting Countries. If this decrease reflects on to the Australian market it means that LPG prices will come down. Certainly, that in itself ought to be an added encouragement for people to convert to LPG.

SINAI PEACEKEEPING FORCE
Discussion ofMatter of Public Importance
Mr SPEAKER-I have received a letter from the honourable member for Corio (Mr Scholes) proposing that a definite matter of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion,
n~mely:
The inability of the Australian Defence Forces to respond to the open ended commitment to a Sinai force and at the same time develop basic Australian defence requirements.

I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their places.
More than the number ofmembers required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places-
Mr SCHOLES (Corio) (3.13)-It is not my intention to deal with the foreign policy or political implications of the commitment of an Australian defence force outside Australia. My concern is the effect that such commitments have on the Defence Force, the development of Australian defence policy and the directions of planning for the defence of Australia which, to say the least, is currently inadequate and is rendered even more inadequate by ad hoc commitments which do not necessarily reflect the Government's planning for the Defence Force, nor the capacity of the Defence Force to respond in such instances.
It is known that during 1978-79, when a force was requested to serve in the proposed United Nations force in Namibia, the Defence Committee and the Defence Force leaders made it clear to the Government that the capacity to provide a force of the size and type which was being suggested did not exist. Ultimately, a force of 250 non-combatants was agreed upon, with 50 security troops to protect that force. If accepted now, this commitment would place a serious strain on the capabilities of the Australian Army. This commitment was strongly argued against, because of its effects on the Defence Force, by senior defence personnel and members of the Australian Defence Committee. The current offer is not in response to a United Nations commitment and, according to correspondence exchanged between the Australian Government and the United States recently, it does not reflect a necessity. The Government has made it clear in statements that it believes the capacity to supervise the withdrawal in the Sinai exists within the two powers party to the agreement, which are not at this stage seen to be under any threat, or likely to be under any threat from a power other than one of those in agreement to the withdrawal. The force proposed is not a United Nations force, as was suggested. It is to be a United States formed multinational force made up of unknown components. Australia has been requested to make a commitment to provide forces for two years, or, as would be more acceptable to the United States, three years. The minimum period is two years. The countries acceptable to the two parties and of whom a request has so far been made are Argentina, Australia, Belgium, Canada, Equador, Fiji, Italy, the Netherlands, Singapore, Mexico, Nepal, New Zealand and Uruguay. A substantial number of those countries will not accede to the request

This is a commitment by the Government to a reduction in that capacity.

Sinai Peacekeeping Force

Mr MORRISON (St George) (3.41)-1 was interested to note in the comments made by the Minister for Administrative Services (Mr Newman), who at the moment is Acting Minister for Defence, a backpedalling on the commitment for a force to the Sinai. For the first time he has introduced a number of very serious qualifications. He certainly does not reflect the enthusiasm shown by his colleagues, the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street) and the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser). 1 believe that this serves to underline that the defence forces are not enamoured of the prospect of a deployment to the Sinai force. That very concept in the manner in which it has been presented, particularly by the Minister for Foreign Affairs, runs counter to the present planning and the philosophy of Australia's defence.
Let me take up one very important point which is reflected in the matter of public importance before the House. The commitment to a Sinai force is an open-ended commitment. I use these terms advisedly. The Minister for Foreign Affairs in his statement said that it would be difficult for the Australian Government to consider an openended commitment but, throughout the speech that he made to this House, he sought to convey the impression that this was only of a temporary nature.
Let us look at the realities. There is no cutoff date or fixed term in the Camp David Accords for the force. The commitment is indefinite. According to the Minister for Foreign Affairs, the commitment will be maintained as long as either party believes it to be necessary. It could go on for years. I wish to make it very clear that the terms for the presence of a force in the Sinai are indisputably open-handed.
What then would be the commitment for the individual participants? What would be Australia's commitment? Again, let us look at the realities. We all know that there is a widespread and understandable reluctance by countries throughout the world to get involved. The responsibility of maintaining the continuity of that Sinai peacekeeping force will be in the hands of only a very small group of countries. That very small group of countries will be left to carry the baby. The Minister will be aware of the discussions that have been going on. The United States of America has made it clear that the commitments of participants to the Sinai force will be for at least two years and, more likely, three years. The Minister will be aware also that the United States has told the Australian Government quite clearly that the forces of participating countries could well be asked to remain beyond that period.

The commitment is open-ended but this Government is very likely not to resist United States pressure for Australian participants to stay on and on. The Minister has spoken of our capacity to put a force into the Sinai but he has not spoken about our capacity to maintain a force of one battalion year after year, which is the likely consequence of a commitment to the Sinai force. This can serve only to distort the planning for the defence of Australia. It will become irreparably distorted.
Australia is not, as we well know, a superpower and it has limited resources. These resources are most properly devoted to our main defence responsibility-the defence of Australia. 1 thought this proposition had been accepted on a bipartisan basis. It is a singularly sensible statement arising, as it does, from the philosophy and the administration of the Australian Labor Party when in office in the years 1972-75. There is a very distinct and delineated area of Australia's primary strategic concern. I wish to quote from the White Paper on defence issued by this Government in November 1976 which put forward a number of propositions that were carried over from the Labor Party's administration of defence. The White Paper states:
For practical purposes the requirement and scope of Australia's defence activity are limited essentially to the areas closer to home. These are our adjacent maritime areas; the south-west Pacific countries and territories; Papua New Guinea, Indonesia; and the South-East Asia region.
It continues:
Events in distant areas such as Africa, the Middle East arid North East Asia (assuming there were international sanctions for Australia's involvement) are beyond the reach of effective defence activity by Australia.
That sums up the standing of Australian Defence Force thinking, not only in 1976 but also through the period 1972-75 and, I trust, now.
I want to turn to the role of the Sinai force to which this Government is seeking to commit Australia. On any reading there is an Alice in Wonderland haze about the whole concept of the Sinai force. Does anyone really believe that a put together force of 2,000 lightly armed personnel is anything more than a token, the worst form of plate glass diplomacy? A small force of that nature, if smashed, will bring on to the scene the major insurance policy holders. The concern of the Australian Labor Party is that Australians should not be sacrificed as one of the splinters from that plate glass diplomacy.
We all know that the force will be concentrated in two areas-in the north at Rafau on the Mediterranean and in the south at Sharm El Sheikh on

Sinai Peacekeeping Force

3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2993

the Red Sea. These areas are separated by something of the order of 250 miles of the Sinai Desert. Not even the Israeli Government would regard the proposed Sinai force as anything more than a flimsy security blanket unless-this is the important qualification-United States forces were directly involved.
One could well be excused for believing that the repeated references to a UN force in the Camp David Accords are a sham. Israel, for a variety of reasons-undoubtedly it thinks they are good reasons-is insistent that the United States should guarantee its security and, to that end, wants the United States directly involved in the force. As we all know, paragraph 8 of Article VJ of Annex I of the Camp David Accords states that the United Nations force and observers will be drawn from nations other than members of the Security Council. This automatically excludes the big powers, including, of course, the United States. However, we have all noted that President Carter's letter of 26 March 1979 provides the let out by agreeing to provide an acceptable alternative multinational force if a United Nations force did not eventuate. In a recent question addressed to the Minister for Foreign Affairs, I asked:
As both Israel and Egypt have a direct and significant interest in ensuring that the Camp David Accords are successfully implemented, will the Minister urge both governments to assume responsibility for managing the withdrawal of Israeli forces without the necessity for a multinational force?
We should realise that already there has been a major withdrawal from the Sinai area. Israeli troops have withdrawn from over two-thirds of that area. This has all been handled by a Sinai field mission of some 200 civilians. A simple proposition which I think we should put, and which we should urge the Minister to put to the United States, is: Why cannot that Sinai field mission continue to handle the withdrawal processes? It has certainly been successful up to now. I see no reason why it cannot be successful in the future.
The important point is that the Camp David Accords are agreements between two governments. Both of those governments have a vested interest in ensuring the success of those Accords. They have a responsibility to themselves, to peace in the Middle East and to the rest of the world to ensure that their conduct under the Accords is such that there is no necessity for a peace force in the Sinai. There is certainly no necessity, as we on this side of the House see it, for Australian participation in a non-United Nations force in the Sinai area.
