BROADCASTING AND TELEVISION AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Bill presented by Mr Sinclair, and read a first time.
Second Reading Mr SINCLAIR (New England-Minister for Communications) (4.5)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The purpose of this Bill is to amend the Broadcasting and Television Act 1942 to provide for changes covering:
(a) supplementary licences for radio stations; (b) public interest criteria; (c) ownership or control provisions;

2996 REPRESENTATIVES 3 June 1981

Broadcasting and Television Amendment Bill

(d) the Australian Broadcasting Commission's merchandising and contractual activities; and
(e) other minor amendments.
The House will l'ecall that in a statement on 9 October 1980 my predecessor, the Hon. A. A. Staley, foreshadowed legislation to introduce supplementary licences as a means of increasing the range of broadcasting services available in nonmetropolitan areas. The Government recognised that in those areas where an additional fully competitive commercial station was not a viable proposition an alternative needed to be found which widened the choice of services. The supplementary licence concept was conceived as a means of providing additional radio and television services, while protecting the commercial viability of existing services in the area and preserving the principle oflocalism in broadcasting.
The Bill provides for the introduction of supplementary licences for commercial FM radio in areas outside Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane, Adelaide and Perth. While commercial FM services have begun operation in mainland metropolitan areas, no commercial FM services are available in regional areas. Many such areas cannot support fully competitive FM services, and accordingly the only means of providing these services in those areas is through the supplementary licence concept. This will enable an existing commercial AM operator to provide an FM service in any area not able to support a fully competitive service.
Honourable members will note that the Bill does not provide for supplementary television licences. The Government has decided not to proceed at this time with supplementary television licences, following extensive discussions with the television industry and following representations by regional television operators that in many cases supplementary television licences would not be commercially viable without a subscription television component. Before any introduction of subscription television services is contemplated, a number of issues will need to be considered, not the least of which is the desirability of such services and the ground rules within which they should operate. Furthermore, the Government believes it WOUld, in any case, be anomalous to introduce subscription television into regional areas without also considering such services for metropolitan areas. Accordingly, the Government has referred the matter of SUbscription television to the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal for consideration in conjunction with its inquiry into cable television. No decision regarding subscription television will be made until the Tribunal has

presented its report, which is expected in March 1982.
The Government is conscious of the fact that in a limited number of areas supplementary television licences may be viable without a subscription television component. However, the Government is not prepared to make separate provision specifically for those areas at this time. It believes that any introduction of supplementary television licences to increase commercial services should be considered in a wider context. The Government is anxious that large regional cities and the smaller capitals such as Canberra should have the opportunity for increased services at the earliest practicable opportunity. It is anticipated that Government will be making further decisions on the subject following receipt of the Tribunal's report. In particular, representations have been received from the CTC7 group in Canberra and a station in Hobart in the expectation that two supplementary licences would be provided for their use in those two centres. The Government has considered theil' representations. However, as with the other major sub-metropolitan area of Newcastle, where again the population base and the nature of the commercial television coverage is such as perhaps to justify more than one supplementary licence, it is felt that the introduction of specific provisions to accommodate their peculiar circumstances could not properly proceed in isolation from consideration of the matters that will be under debate in this Parliament following receipt of the report of the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal on the cable television and subscription television inquiry that I mentioned a little while ago.
The House will also recall that on 9 April 1981 I announced to the Parliament the broad thrust of the conclusions reached by the Government after its review of aspects of the Act. I foreshadowed amendments covering:
1. The machinery and procedural aspects of sections 90 to 92KA of the Act; and
2. inadvertent breaches of the Act by shareholders in companies acquiring interests in radio and television broadcasting licences through share trading on the stock exchange.
Necessarily that statement did not express with the same precision as legislation the changes proposed to the Act. Honourable members will note that in some respects the Bill differs from the proposals then outlined. The changes follow from further consultation with the industry. However,

Broadcasting and Television Amendment Bill

3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2997

that statement did outline in general terms a package of proposals with two major themes which certainly remain relevant.
Firstly, the amendments will maintain the basic structure of decision making that has been in operation since 1977. The amendments will provide the means for more effective administration of the broadcasting system in accordance with government policy. The lack of reference points for the Tribunal in deciding whether a particular licensing decision would be in the public interest has led to some uncertainty in Tribunal proceedings. The incorporation of legislative guidelines for public interest will provide a clearer statement of government policy in relation to the major discretionary powers of the Tribunal.
Secondly, the amendments are designed to ensure that the ownership or control provisions will remain effective and appropriate to the commercial environment of the 1980s. Restrictions on the free transfer of interests should be no greater than are necessary to achieve a reasonable balance between the desirability of preventing uncontrolled trading in licences and the need for a vigorous share market. It has become apparent that the present share transaction procedures, which, of course, require the Tribunal's approval prior to any purchase, may operate to prevent a large diversified industrial corporation, for example, from lawfully purchasing shares on the open market in another such corporation, if the second corporation has an indirect interest in a radio or television station of as little as 6 per cent of issued capital. The Government regards such a result as inappropriate for an Act which is designed only to regulate radio and television, and unnecessary to achieve the aims of the legislation. This applies even more strongly in the case of third party breaches of the Act. It is only in exceptional circumstances in our system of justice that a person should be immediately liable for an offence as a result of some other person's actions. In this instance, the circumstances certainly do not warrant such a result. In the Government's view, any person being put into inadvertent breach of the Act should not face possible prosecution, without first being allowed at least six months to comply with the ownership limitations.
On 9 April last, my colleagues the Attorney General (Senator Durack) and the Minister for Industrial Relations (Mr Viner), representing the Attorney-General, tabled in Parliament a report by the Administrative Review Council on the inquiry procedures of the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal. In my statement to the House on the same day I said that there would not be any decision as to specific recommendations until the

public and the broadcasting industry had the opportunity to comment. I have, through my Department, recently contacted interested individuals and organisations asking for their comments on the report by the end of July. Therefore, I expect the Government to be in a position to consider specific recommendations of the report later in the year.
I also point out that during the preparation of the Bill and particularly following my announcement to the House on 9 April there has been considerable public debate. In particular, details of the proposals have been discussed extensively with a wide range of broadcasting and television groups and all those who have made contact with my office. The idea, of course, was to ensure that in the application of the ownership or control amendments those who were immediately affected should have some opportunity of expressing the consequences as they saw them. Certainly, overall the objective was to assess the impact of suggested changes before the details of the Bill now to be presented to the Parliament were finalised.
In summary, the intention of the present amendments is to streamline procedures, avoid delays in the Tribunal's consideration of transactions, minimise the necessary number of inquiries and provide clearer guidance in relation to the Tribunal's major discretionary powers. I turn now to the detail of the proposed amendments.
Supplementary Licences for Radio Stations
The Bill will enable commercial AM licensees, in areas other than Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane, Adelaide and Perth, to apply for a supplementary licence to operate an FM radio service in their areas. Provision has also been made to allow licensees, in areas where there is more than one AM station, if they so choose, to form a consortium to apply for one supplementary licence on a joint basis. Because of the different propagation characteristics of FM as opposed to AM signals, provision is also made for the Minister to invite an applicant to apply for associated commercial translator licences. The intent behind this provision is to ensure that an area currently served by a commercial AM station and its associated translator stations will as far as possible be able to be served by a supplementary FM station together with its associated translator stations.
Applicants will make their applications to the Minister in accordance with a form approved by him. Applications can be made by commercial AM operators only to provide a supplementary

2998 REPRESENTATIVES 3 June 1981

Broadcasting and Television Amendment Bill

FM service in their own areas. Unlike other licence applications, where applicants apply in response to an invitation by the Minister, applicants for supplementary licences will be able to apply at any time. The Minister will, however, be able to refer the application to the Tribunal or to reject the application on grounds relating to technical matters or to the planning or development of broadcasting and television services. This provision arises from the Minister's statutory responsibilities for the planning and development of these services. There is, after all, only a limited spectrum and it is essential that there is a capacity to regulate the available area within it.
In considering applications for supplementary licences the Tribunal will follow similar procedures to those applying to other licences. For example, in considering an application for a supplementary licence, the Tribunal will be required to take account of the criteria applying to the grant of other licences. However, an additional provision will enable the Tribunal to refuse to grant a supplementary licence in any area which can sustain a fully competitive commercial broadcasting station without endangering the viability of existing services. If the Tribunal finds that an additional commercial station would be viable then it will be required to inform the Minister of its determination and the reasons for it.
Supplementary licences will be granted for up to five years and renewed for up to three years. The Tribunal shall, in considering any renewal of a supplementary licence not less than eight years after the initial grant of a supplementary licence, determine whether an additional commercial station is reasonably likely to be viable in that area. If it finds that such a station would be viable, it shall recommend to the Minister that he invite applications for a commercial licence and shall give the supplementary licensee three years' notice of its intention not to renew the supplementary licence. A licensee, but not a consortium, will be able in such a case to opt to retain the broadcasting station operated under his supplementary licence and instead forgo the station operated under his commercial licence. This provision is intended to cover a broadcaster wishing to retain his supplementary FM station rather than his commercial AM station. In this case the supplementary licence will convert to a normal commercial licence.
I need to comment oil some aspects of supplementary licences which have been the subject of some recent concern by the broadcasting industry. A decision to seek a supplementary licence is

entirely a decision for the licensee in the area concerned. The ministerial discretion to dismiss applications can be on only technical grounds or for planning and development reasons, for example, where no frequencies are available or where the calling of applications for a fully competitive service is contemplated. I emphasise that the ministerial discretion must be interpreted within the scope, purposes and objects of the Act, which are all laid down and are available for everybody to see and understand. The minimum duration of a supplementary licence is 11 years with no consideration of non-renewal before eight years. The procedures for renewal impose what I believe is reasonable protection for the licensee on the one hand and the public on the other. The Government believes this provides an adequate period for licensees to recoup their investment.
Finally, it is not correct to suggest that there are no appeals against decisions by the Tribunal which apply to other classes of licence. The appeal provisions in section 119A apply equally to supplementary licences as to other classes of licences, the only difference being that there is no appeal against non-renewal ability after eight years. Special provisions have been inserted to allow the supplementary licence to continue in operation in circumstances where the Tribunal has decided not to renew the licence but for one reason or another competitive licences have not been issued.
The Bill includes a number of special provisions relating to localism and arrangements with other radio stations. Specifically, supplementary licensees will need to seek Tribunal approval for arrangements entered into with other stations for the regular broadcasting of:
(i) the programs of a related commercial AM station or other commercial stations;
(ii) the programs of the Australian Broadcasting Commission, the Special Broadcasting Service or public stations.
This provision will provide some degree of Tribunal oversight over the simulcastingof programs-that is, the simultaneous transmission in one area of the same radio program by two or more stations-or the networking of radio programs from other areas. Other provisions intended to preserve the principle of localism are those relating to:
(i) the minimum amount of locally produced or originated programs as provided for by licence conditions; and
(ii) the encouragement of local advertising.

There is already a considerable backlog of applications to be considered relating to the existing range of broadcasting and television services. The undertaking concerning the provision of an adequate and comprehensive service in pursuance of the licence provides a focus for public input at licensing inquiries. 3000 REPRESENTATIVES 3 June 1981

Broadcasting and Television Amendment Bill

Sections 90J and 92F of the principal Actclauses 27 and 37 of the Bill-are being amended to provide for the notification to the Tribunal of any intended transaction and the lodgment of an application for approval of either an intended transaction, or a completed transaction. Failure to lodge a notice or application prior to a transaction will be an offence on each day until a notice or application is lodged. However, such an offence will not preclude ultimate approval by the Tribunal of a transaction provided an application is lodged within 28 days. These amendments will allow the Tribunal to receive applications for approval of share transactions after, as well as before, transactions have taken place.
New sections 90JA and 92FAA-clauses 28 and 38 of the Bill-make provision for the approval of share and debenture transactions. In summary, these sections provide:
(i) for the Tribunal to approve an application in respect of share transactions after, as well as before, a transaction has taken place;
(ii) that the Tribunal may disapprove an application made before or after a transaction provided that it first holds an inquiry into the intended transaction; that inquiry may at the Tribunal's discretion be:
(a) a separate inquiry into the transaction; or
(b) an inquiry into both the transaction and the renewal of the licence;
(iii) that any inquiry may be required by the purchaser, in his application, to commence within three months after the application and if the inquiry does not commence within three months, the transaction is deemed to have been approved; and
(iv) that the Tribunal shall take all reasonable steps to complete an inquiry within three months of commencing the inquiry.
Where a transaction has taken place without prior approval the Tribunal may give directions to prevent any action it considers will have an adverse effect on the ability of a company holding the licence to comply with the conditions of the licence or on the operations, management or the selection or provision of programs by the licensee company. These powers are conferred by clause 40 in new section 92M. Clauses 28 and 38 of the Bill also provide the criteria which must be taken into account by the Tribunal in considering whether to approve a transaction. In particular, the clauses provide the grounds on which the Tribunal may

refuse an application for the approval of a transaction, in whole or in part where an application is lodged in accordance with section 90J or 92F.
These are: (i) in the case of a purchaser acquiring or increasing a major interest in the licence, the Tribunal would be empowered to refuse consent to the transfer of that licence to the applicant; This provision recognises that a major share transaction affecting a licensee and a transfer of the licence are, in practical terms, similar and the Tribunal would consider the same public interest criteria and other criteria as are provided for in clause 19, that is, section 89A dealing with transfers;
(ii) in the case of a purchaser acquiring any interest in the licence, where the licence has been granted within the previous two years, this provision is intended to preserve the integrity of the licence grant process, by preventing an unsuccessful applicant for a licence from taking over the company of the successful applicant.
(iii) in the case of a purchaser acquiring a minor interest, or any other applicant acquiring an indirect interest through the purchaser, where the applicant does not comply with public interest criteria, this provision applies the criteria in a more appropriate way to minor and indirect interests than if these were considered under point (i) above and take into account:
(a) the effect the transaction will have on the capacity of the holder of the licence to comply with the conditions of the licence and the written undertakings given to the Tribunal in relation to program services;
(b) whether the applicant is a fit and proper person to hold a licence and, as far as is relevant, has the necessary financial, technical and management capacities to carry out the requirements of a licence; and
(c) the degree of concentration of influence either directly or indirectly, on commercial radio and television stations, in a nonmetropolitan area.
These streamlined procedures and guidelines will provide adequate controls on the ownership of radio and television. The Tribunal's approval will be necessary for the acquisition or increase of a

Broadcasting and Television Amendment Bill

3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3001

prescribed interest, and the Tribunal will retain a discretion to consider an acquisition in the context of a public inquiry. At the same time the share market will be freed from unnecessary restrictions, but adequate checks will remain to discourage trading in licences per se. I should like to make one further point. The Government has decided that the new provisions relating to approval of share transactions will apply only to transactions taking place after commencement of this legislation. Clause 50 gives effect to this decision.
LOAN INTEREST AND REGISTERED LENDERS
Under the existing Act, loan interests in a television station may result in a lender acquiring a prescribed interest in a licence. In some cases this will restrict opportunities to obtain tinance, except by bank overdraft, to which exemption already applies, when in practice the investor might only be able to influence a licence where a licensee defaults on a loan. New section 91 cclause 31 of the Bill-empowers the Tribunal to register approved lenders and so exclude them from the prescribed interest provisions of the Act provided the Tribunal is satisfied that a person will not be in a position to exercise, directly or indirectly, a significant influence on any company holding a licence. In circumstances where a registered lender used a loan interest to exercise undue control over a licensee the Tribunal could deregister the lender. The lender would then be subject to the prescribed interest provisions applying to loan interests.
VARIAnON OF LIMITS ON PERMISSIBLE HOLDINGS
New section 91D-c1ause 31 or the Bill-addresses the problem of breaches of section 92 of the Act by third parties acquiring excess prescribed interests as a result of share transactions by other persons. Experience has shown that an investor can be put in breach of the Act through the purchase of shares by another party in which the investor is a shareholder, because of the tracing provisions of the Act. Problems of third party breaches of the Act have arisen particularly with institutional investors because of their large and diversified portfolio shareholdings.
New section 91D provides that any person holding an interest of from 5 per cent up to 10 per cent-on any test-in a television licence without its being considered as a prescribed interest, may apply to the Tribunal for a certificate permitting him to hold that interest. However, the benefits of the section will be extended only to persons acquiring an interest in a television licence operating

in an area where there is at least one other commercial television station-that is, the five mainland State capitals-and then only if those persons can satisfy the Tribunal that the interest in question will not give rise to a person exercising, either directly or indirectly, a significant influence on the licensee company. Clause 31 of the Billincorporating both section 91 and section 91D--will commence on a date to be proclaimed. Some preliminary work by the Tribunal is necessary to enable introduction of these provisions. However, the Government will be looking to proclaim this provision as soon as practicable.
FOREIGN SHAREHOLDINGS
The existing sections 90G and 92D of the Act restrict ownership of shares, but not voting rights, in licensee companies by any individual nonresident to 15 per cent, and by the aggregate of non-resident shareholders to 20 per cent, of issued share capital. The interpretation of the provision has presented difficulties because of the lack of definition of 'resident' and the meaning of 'control'-that is, whether it is commercial control, up to 50 per cent of votes, or control as defined in the Act; that is, 15 per cent of votes and shares. These sections have been redrafted to provide a redefinition of restrictions on foreign shareholdings to provide that only Australian citizens and companies in which Australian citizens hold more than 50 per cent of the votes and/or shares, may be 'in a position to control' a licensee company. Clause 53 is a transitional provision for non-resident shareholdings, enabling residents who are not Australian citizens to retain any interests they hold at commencement which comply with the existing provisions of the Act.
PROVISIONS RELATING TO ENFORCEMENT AND PENALTY
PROVISIONS
The changes proposed by the Bill to the ownership or control provisions will produce a system which gives considerably more flexibility to licensees and persons acquiring interests in licences. The Government expects strict compliance with the new provisions. To this end existing sanctions and penalties have been strengthened substantially. Clause 40 provides for the Tribunal to give directions for the purpose of securing disposal of excess interests if it is satisfied that a person has breached the provisions of Division 2 or 3 of Part IV of the Act by the acquisition of shares, debenture or loan interests. Directions given under clause 40 will be enforceable in the Federal Court of Australia. Additionally, the Bill provides in clauses 40 and 46 that non-compliance

3002 REPRESENTATIVES 3 June 1981

Broadcasting and Television Amendment Bill

with directions will attract substantially higher penalti~ than those presently in force.
AUSTRALIAN BROADCASTING COMMISSION MECHANDISING AND
CONTRACTUAL ACTIVITIES Clauses 7 and 8 of the Bill amend merchandising and contractual provisions relating to the operations of the Australian Broadcasting Commission. These provisions will enable the ABC to make, promote, circulate or sell non-literary matter associated with its programs. Clause 8 allows the ABC to enter into contracts for the sale of its programs without ministerial approval. This delegation is essential for the quick negotiation of contracts.
OTHER MINOR AMENDMENTS The Bill contains a number of other provisions which are either consequential to the changes outlined or are minor changes which update and standardise provisions to accord with current legislative practice. In 1965 when the ownership provisions were last amended special provision was made for persons holding interests in excess of the new limitations, such that these interests could be retained as long as they were not increased or restructured. The effect of this 'freezing' provision or the 'grandfather' clause as it was known, which today applies only to a few companies, has been to inhibit their ability to adjust their corporate structure to the present commercial environment. These companies will require time to restructure their interests. I take this opportunity to foreshadow a Government amendment which I will be proposing to move during the Committee stage of the debate on the Bill which will make appropriate transitional arrangements for these companies in relation to the new share transaction procedures. The proposed changes I have outlined to the ownership or control provisions will be the first major alterations for 16 years, yet there have been significant developments in the industry over that time. These changes, together with the introduction of supplementary licences for radio stations, will provide a sound basis for the continued development of broadcasting in the coming decade without undue constraint on commercial activity. I commend the Bill to the House. Debate (on motion by Mr Dawkins) adjourned.
BROADCASTING STATIONS LICENCE FEES AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Bill presented by Mr Sinclair, and read a first time.

Second Reading Mr SINCLAIR (New England-Minister for Communications) (4.35)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
This Bill makes consequential amendments to the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964 to extend that Act to supplementary licences for radio stations. References in the Act to commercial broadcasting stations, licences and licensees are applied to supplementary broadcasting stations, licences and licensees. Provisions have also been inserted to deal with licence fees payable by a consortium of commercial broadcasting licensees holding a supplementary licence. The Bill provides that the supplementary broadcasting station will be regarded as a separate operation for the purposes of licence fees assessments. The effect of these amendments is to place supplementary licensees on the same basis as commerciallicensees in respect of the imposition and calculation of licence fees. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr Dawkins) adjourned.
OVERSEAS TELECOMMUNICATIONS AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Bill presented by Mr Sinclair, and read a first time.
Second Reading Mr SINCLAIR (New England-Minister for Communications) (4.36)-lmove:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The principal purpose of this Bill is to amend the Overseas Telecommunications Act 1946 to allow the Overseas Telecommunications Commission (Australia) to form or participate in the formation of a company that may be established to own and manage the national communications satellite system. On 18 September 1980, the then Minister for Post and Telecommunications informed the House that tenders were to be called for the supply of a communications satellite'system, and that the Government was considering the possibility of establishing a company to own and manage that $ystem, the public sector to hold 51 per cent of the share capital in such a company and the private sector to hold 49 per cent of the shares. A committee of officials was requested to report on the interest of the private sector in participating in such a company in which OTC would have a major interest on behalf of the Government, the company to operate' as a commercial tax-paying ertterprise. As an interim measure, OTC had been designated as the authority to own

Overseas Telecommunications Amendment Bill

3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3003

and manage the communications satellite system. For this purpose, licences under the Wireless Telegraphy Act 1905 had been issued to OTC to provide telecommunications services by way of a satellite system.
The request for tenders for the satellite communications system was issued in October last year. The request for tenders is a comprehensive set of documents inviting tenders for the supply of the space segment of the satellite system, the major city earth stations and minor earth stations to access the satellite, and earth stations required by Telecom Australia for telephony services as well as to receive only earth stations for regional and remote area national broadcasting services. Also included in the request for tender was the supply of earth terminals for the Department of Transport's air safety and navigational services. Twenty tenders were received for the space and earth segments of the satellite communications system and evaluation of tenders is now taking place.
In introducing this Bill I make it clear that the Government has not taken decisions on what the final ownership arrangements will be. The report of officials on their discussions with the private sector on participating in a company to own and manage the satellite communications system is now being considered by the Government and I hope to be able to announce the outcome of these considerations in the neal' future. Honourable members will appreciate that if the Government decides to proceed with the concept of establishing a company with public and private sector involvement, there will be a need for further discussion and negotiation on the detailed arrangements which might apply before the company, as a legal entity, is established. The provisions of the Bill in this regard should therefore be seen as a contingency measure only. Whether there will be a need for recourse to the provisions of the Bill will depend on Government decisions regarding the organisation or authority which will be the ultimate owner of the satellite communications system and the timing for implementing those decisions. For this reason the Bill provides that action by OTe to form or participate in such a company requires the approval of the Minister.
Other objectives of the Bill are to enable OTe to provide technical services, on a commercial basis, to such a company, and to enable OTC to provide consultative services to the governments

of neighbouring countries on the use of the satellite system for their own purposes. Again the Minister's approval is required before OTe can take action along those lines. The provision for OTC to provide consultative services of this nature to neighbouring countries reflects the Government's policy of assisting less developed nations in the region to develop their own telecommunications services. Provision has been made in the design of the system for the satellite to provide telecommunications services for the Government of Papua New Guinea. This is an optional feature in the system design, and the Papua New Guinea Government has yet to make decisions regarding its use of the Australian satellite system for its own needs.
For technical reasons, it has not been possible to provide in the first generation satellite system for services to be available to other countries in the South West Pacific region. The Government has offered, however, to participate in a study of the telecommunications needs of those countries, and a formal invitation has now been received for Australia to take part in such a study. The results of that study will be taken into account when planning later generations of communications satellites.
The Overseas Telecommunications Commission has had considerable experience in the provision of telecommunications services by satellites. It now provides tracking, telemetry, command and monitoring facilities for the Intelsat satellites in orbital locations over the Pacific and Indian Oceans. It will provide similar services for satellites to be launched by the European Space Agency. The OTe also provides the major facilities at Moree, Ceduna and Carnarvon for receiving and transmitting by satellite of international communications. Since the taking of the decision to invest OTC with the interim responsibility for developing the satellite communications system, that authority has built up its technical resources on satellite communications. The skills and experiences of OTC officers in satellite communications systems will be of major benefit in pursuing the Government'8 policy of extending modern telecommunications services to all people in Australia.
In seeking the support of honourable members to this Bill 1 would emphasise that the development of the nationaI communications satellite system is a major project. The evaluation of tenders will be one of the most critical aspects. It has to be undertaken within a time frame which would allow contracts for the supply of the system to be entered into in early 1982, so that the space and

3004 REPRESENTATIVES 3 June 1981

Bounty (Printed Fabrics) Bill

earth segments of the system are available to coincide with reservations which have been made with the National Aeronautics and Space Administration, or NASA, and the European Space Agency to place the satellites in their Ofbital positions. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by MI' Scholes) adjourned.
BOUNTY (PRINTED FABRICS) BILL 1981
Bill presented by Mr Moore, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr MOORE (Ryan-Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs) (4.43)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The purpose of this Bill is to give effect to the Government's decision to pay a bounty on the printing in Australia of certain lightweight woven fabrics weighing less than 120 grams per square metre. The decision to adopt this form of assistance follows the Government's consideration of the recommendation by the Industries Assistance Commission contained in its report No. 240 of 30 April 1980 entitled 'Textiles, Clothing and Footwear'. This bounty proposal is part of the Government's broader program for the textiles, clothing and footwear industries, intended to encourage the development of a more efficient industry structure. It is expected also to provide substantial benefits to user industries such as clothing manufacturers. To this end, the proposal is in agreement with the broad objectives of the Industries Assistance Commission's recommendation.
The Bill provides for the bounty to be payable at the rate of 70 per cent of the additional value added to the fabric by the printer. The value added will be based on the factory cost of tl>e bountiable printed fabric less the costs of materials used in the printing process. The scheme is to operate for a period of seven years from I January 1982 and bounty payments for the tlrst full year are estimated to be $2.5rn.
The bounty assistance is designed to offset the effect on printers of another Government decision to grant import entry of certain fabrics at mini.. mum rates of duty and free of quota that is, those printed fabrics weighing less than 120 grams per square metre, and destined for garment use. Bounty is payable on fabric printed at registered premises during the bounty period, if the fabric is used or is sold for use in making up into garments in a registered clothing factory. Clothing factories will be treated as being registered for the purposes of the bounty scheme if registered under a State

law or, in the case of internal Territories which do not have laws suitable for these purposes, such places used for the production of garments and approved by the Comptroller-General of Customs.
This approach is consistent with exclusions from the proposed tariff concessions of certain lightweight woven fabrics which I mentioned earlier. The exclusions will protect the interests of manufacturers of other lightweight cloth, such as pocketings, linings and curtains. At the same time, the Bill makes provision for an extension by regulation of the uses to which bountiable fabric may be put, if the Government decides that such an extension is desirable. The Government expects that the assistance proposed by this Bill will enable the local industry to compete adequately against printed fabrics of a similar nature which will be able to be imported at minimum rates of duty and free of quota after 1 January 1982. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr Scholes) adjourned.
BOUNTY (TEXTILE YARNS) BILL 1981
Bill presented by Mr Moore, and read a first time.

Mr MOORE (Ryan-Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs) (4.48)-1 move:

The purpose of this Bill is to give effect to the Government's decision to provide bounty assistance to the production in Australia of certain yarns. The decision to adopt this form of assistance follows the Government's consideration of the recommendations by the Industries Assistance Commission contained in its report No. 240 of 30 April 1980 entitled 'Textiles, Clothing and Footwear'.
This bounty proposal is part of the Government's broader program designed to encourage the development of a more efficient industry structure for the textiles, clothing and footwear industries. It will also provide substantial benefits for industries using bountiable yarns in manufacturing textiles or textile products. User industries will benefit from reduced costs of material inputs and greater flexibility of sourcing. To this end the proposal is in agreement with the broad objectives of the Industries Assistance Commission's recommendation. The Bill provides for the bounty to be payable to producers of most yarns at rates calculated as specified percentages of the value added to the yarns, on a factory cost basis, in their production. The yarns eligible for the bounty are

Airline services are one of the few profitable areas of our public transport system. The objective of the Australian Labor Party Opposition is to ensure that the people of Australia have maximum access to safe, convenient airline services at a minimum cost to the users. There can be no justification, in the provision of airline services under the airline agreement, for the operators of those services, Ansett Airlines and Trans-Australia Airlines, to earn profits commensurate with trading operations in the general commercial environment. That was a point made very strongly by the recent Holcroft Committee of Inquiry into Domestic Air Fares in its recent report. The fact is that

the public transport system of which the airline

Debate (on motion by Mr Morris) adjourned. service is a major part is there to provide a service

3006 REPRESENTATIVES 3 June 1981

Airlines Agreement Bill

to the community. It is not there to earn rewards for risk-takers. In fact, under the provisions of the airline agreement there is little risk involved because the system is a cost-plus system. Only the profits have not been spelled out in specific terms.
.Before discussing provisions of the Airlines Agreement Bill let me quickly recall the origins of the two-airline policy. Honourable members will remember that it was introduced in the early 1950s to prop up the failing private enterprise operator, Australian National Airways. It was amended subsequently in 1957 to meet the desires of its successor, Ansett Airlines. The policy has entrenched a rigid regulatory control of domestic airline services and has been supported by successive conservative governments. The great contradiction in the behaviour of honourable members sitting opposite is that despite their constant claims as to the efficiency and efficacy of free enterprise and how it can be done better by the private sector, the two-airline policy, by its very nature, is the antithesis of free enterprise. It provides to two private multi-millionaire operators guaranteed protected access to half the capacity of major airline services in this country. On the basis on which they are protected from any competition, there can be no danger of a competitor pushing them out from their share of the traffic or of endangering their commercial position. As I mentioned, they are protected by a cost-plus pricing system.
The present proprietors of Ansett Airlines seized control of Ansett Transport Industries Ltd in a gigantic stock exchange coup in late 1979. They paid heavily inflated prices for AT!. The new proprietors then had to set about finding ways of increasing the financial yield for Ansett Airlines. They did not have great difficulty because they had a compliant government. They had a captive government then and they have a captive government now. It stands ready to do their bidding. Today we saw the introduction of the Murdoch amendments in respect of the Broadcasting and Television Act. The two-airline agreement was to be re-negotiated and changed to assist further the multi-millionaire proprietors of Ansett Airlines.
The new agreement makes no reference to a limitation on profits despite the exclusive right of the parties to provide major domestic airline services. It is designed to help achieve that higher financial yield now required by Ansett Transport Industries to fund the inflated prices and to help pay for the tax free profit that Mr Robert Holmes a' Court gained from the sale of his shares to the

present proprietors. As I have pointed out, because the fare structure is based on a cost-plus system and the agreement requires the operation of only two operators over the entire trunk route network, Ansett cannot fail. There is no risk or danger from competitive forces over the entire trunk route network. Again, that is a point which was made by Mr Holcroft. In effect, the two airline policy is a licence to print money. That is the point that has been made by editorial writers around this country on a number of occasions. The editorial writer of the Australian Financial Review of7 December 1979, at the time when the coup was in progress, was correct when he wrote:
The major asset of ATI is the entrenched rights and privileges it possesses under the two-airline policy.
Television licences are public property which may not be freely bought and sold. So is the position of ATI within the two-airline policy.
Government policy does not have to remain unchanged on either.
It certainly has not. The present multi-millionaire proprietors of ATI bought into that company and organised that stock exchange coup on their own initiative. Let their risks be on their own heads, but let not their risks be an imposition upon the air travellers of this country under the masquerade that they are providing a public transport service, a better service and that they would not have entered into contracts to purchase new aircraft if they did not have knowledge of the new agreement which was negotiated and which was not made available to this Parliament. The travelling public has no obligation to ensure an inflated rate of return on the investment the proprietors of Ansett have made in their airline.
The incredible subservience of this government to the Ansett proprietors is shown by the surrender of its sovereign power to determine aviation policy. It is an incredible performance. I could not find anything in parliamentary history which showed a sovereign government handing over its power finally to determine policy in the appropriate area to a private organisation which, in this case, is two multi-millionaires. The granting in clause 2 of the agreement of a 90-day power of veto to Ansett and TAA is without precedent in parliamentary history, but it also indicates the level of distrust. This is very important because this legislation has come before the Parliament after several months of dissention, disarray, backroom brawling and attempts to overthrow the leadership within Government. Out of all that disarray has come this second version of a two-airline agreement within 12 months. The distrust that is indicated in clause 2 of the new agreement goes to

3008 REPRESENTATIVES 3 June 1981

Airlines Agreement Bill

with the provisions of the Victorian Companies Act. Under the agreement the Minister is to be presented with accounting information specifically, with airline operations in total, not specific, terms. Why could the Schedule not provide for accounting information from Ansett and TAA set out in a standardised format so that a meaningful analysis and comparison between the two operators could be made? There is a great need for financial information on each individual aviation section of Ansett. The Airline Equipment (Loan Guarantee) Act requires this information to be furnished each year by Ansett. All the Minister has to do is extend this information to be available to be tabled in Parliament. If TAA has to furnish similar financial information in a standardised format then members of this Parliament and members of the public will be aware of what is going on in this highly protected industry.
In the 24 years since the 1957 airline agreement Ansett has not had to produce a detailed set of accounts for examination by anybody. The Department of Transport may have had access to those accounts. I do not believe that in recent years the Department has exercised its prerogative to require that information. If it did the information was not provided to the Parliament. In the recent Holcroft committee inquiry the proprietors of Ansett were not only able to dictate to the inquiry how it should conduct its affairs but also were able to elude providing necessary detailed financial information on the airline's activities. That information is absolutely necessary. If other operators in the industry are required to produce information so should Ansett. But it is necessary so that the parliament and the people can assess the efficiency and the financial results of the airlines on a comparative basis as part of the offset for the heavy level of protection that is provided to Ansett and TAA.
Clause 15 of the agreement deals with the role of Qantas Airways Ltd, TAA and Ansett. In the 1980 agreement this clause, despite recent Press coverage, still retains for Ansett a power of veto over direct Qantas participation in the provision of domestic air services. I have noted in recent weeks stories which gave the impression that the clause had been modified. It has been modified to require the Government to consult with the board of Qantas. But the modification has not altered the fact that Ansett, as one of the parties to the agreement, has a power of veto over whether Qantas should directly participate in the provision of domestic air services across Australia. Again the consumer is discriminated against for the benefit of domestic operators.

The contrast to be made is that recently, when there was a strike within the domestic aviation industry and the Government wanted to do something about it, it was quickly able to overcome the facilitation problems the Minister for Transport is so fond of quoting as the reason the capacity of Qantas across Australia cannot be used. In that strike situation, where it suited the Government's own political purposes, it moved in quickly to utilise Qantas capacity.
Clause 15 (2) sets out that any change in Government policy in the future in the respective roles of TAA, Ansett and Qantas will need only the approval of the parties to the agreement. That confirms what I have just mentioned in respect of Ansett having the final veto. The consultation process for Qantas is a little bit of window dressing.
Another undesirable feature of the agreement is its term of five years, plus three years notice of termination, which is in effect an eight-year minimum period. Its result will be to deny to Australians the benefits of new generation aircraft, adjustments that may be required flowing from changing market patterns, population shifts and tourist development. I will deal with these aspects in more detail in the Committee stage of the Bill.
Because of time limitations I turn briefly now to the Independent Air Fares Committee Bill. This legislation is a partial acceptance of the Australian Labor Party's policy canvassed at the last two elections for the establishment of an independent air fare tribunal, to which I referred on those occasions as the Prices Justification Tribunal or a similar body. Such a tribunal would be given the power to assess and determine air fares. Despite the deficiencies of this Bill, it is a step in the right direction. Those deficiencies can be remedied in the Committee stage with the support of some honourable members opposite. One major deficiency in this Bill and the Airlines Agreement Bill is the absence of a finite and precise financial objective for the airlines which would be available for consideration by the Independent Air Fares Committee. The Preamble to the agreement to which I referred earlier refers, in association with paragraph (a), to the need to ensure 'the efficient and economic operation of air passenger services within Australia'. As I said earlier, they are vague words, public service jargon, with the meaning being in the mind of the utterer. Clause 11 of the Independent Air Fares Committee Bill lists the items to which the Committee shall have regard in conducting major air fare reviews. Clause 15 (11) (d) contains almost the same words as clause A of the preamble to which I have referred; the same vagueness and the same generality.

Airlines Agreement Bill

3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3009

However, clause 15 (11) (g), which relates to operating on a competitive basis, is almost in conflict with clause 15 (11) (d) because the two objects cannot be compatible. On the one hand, the legislation involves operating on a competitive basis and, on the other, it involves ensuring economic and efficient operation. Clause 15 (11) (d) of the legislation would be improved if the financial objectives were specific. From my assessment and interpretation clauses 3 and 5 of the air fares committee legislation create serious problems for East-West Airlines Ltd, the major regional operator, in that that company's fare over a trunk route sector must be treated by the Committee as a discount fare. The fact that its fares will be treated in that manner imposes upon the company an obligation to supply information to which it would not have access, and an obligation to make forecasts or produce evidence that certain events may not happen. Again, that is information to which the company would not have access and which it would not be able to provide. We will examine that matter in more detail when we come to the Committee stage of the Bill.
Whilst the legislation picks up a number of the Holcroft committee's recommendations, it is regrettable that it has not adopted those in respect of profit limitation, full public disclosure and, very importantly, reconciliation of airline accounts with the overall activities of the organistions concerned. Some Government members like to criticise the non-airline activities of Trans-Australia Airlines from time to time without following the argument right through or without providing full information. After all, they are members of the Government and it is their responsibility to ensure that full information is provided. It is not the responsibility of the public; they are the Government. In the case of Ansett Transport Industries Ltd, I have often had the complaint from surface transport operators that there are ways and means that can be followed by Ansett Airlines of Australia and ATI in the preparation of accounts, in inter-company transfers and inter-company baJances in the establishment or conduct of operations, airline and non-airline, which conceal the true movements of funds between those companies and which place the airline company always in the position in which, when it is purchasing new aircraft, it needs the benefit of Government guarantees to obtain loans abroad. Mr Holcroft, a major figure in the private sector of the transport industry; Professor Hogan, an economist; and Dr Bell, who is an economist in the investment field, were skilled and able to assess and determine the importance of proper reconciliation. Unfortunately, a provision to deal

with such a practice is not contained in the Bill. Similarly, the Bill does not provide for public dissemination of comparative information on the efficiency of the financial and technical results of both airlines.
I am restricting my time because of a request from the other side. I have outlined the Opposition's general approach to the three Bills before the House. In view of those time restrictions I will reserve my further remarks for the Committee stage of the Bills.
Mr SHACK (Tangney) (5.14)-The three Bills the House is debating cognately this afternoon-the Airlines Agreement Bill, the Independent Air Fares Committee Bill and the Airlines Equipment Amendment Bill-together with the Australian National Airlines Repeal Bill which will be debated in this chamber later today, are the result of over two years of extensive consideration of domestic aviation policy by the Government, by private members of parliament, by the airlines themselves and, importantly, also by the pUblic. Some of the decisions that have been announced by the Government to date, and to which I will refer shortly, ensure that this consideration will be an ongoing process; a process to evaluate the appropriateness of the two-airline policy, which is to be perpetuated by the Airlines Agreement Bill, and perhaps to examine some other options such as a greater or lesser degree of deregulation in the future. As the honourable member for Shortland (Mr Morris) mentioned, there is a limitation of time for this debate. I know that several of my colleagues would like to speak in the Committee stage. I propose to keep my most detailed comments for that time.
I now want to speak broadly, but briefly, on what I think has been achieved over the last couple of years. Over the last two to two and a half years consideration can be perceived as beginning with the Domestic Air Transport Policy Review Committee Report, which came out in 1978. That was an important report because it came out in June 1978, when the Airlines Agreement Act of 1973 terminated. After December 1977 the opportunity existed to provide for termination of previous airlines agreements with five years notice. I believe it was an important report. It came to numerous conclusions designed to improve the regulatory environment. It was on the basis of this report that the Government proceeded to negotiate with the airlines to come up with the airlines agreement which was signed towards the latter part of last year. That agreement was designed to incorporate the improvements and changes that were recommended in the report.

However, the committee concluded it was not possible to make Trans-Australia Airlines fully commercial while the Government was a majority shareholder.
What we are doing now, in my view, is taking the first most important step. We have to take the next most important step before we arrive at a final decision as to the future of TAA, that is, to investigate the structure of TAA. To look at TANs superannuation liabilities and other liabilities, its asset value and so forth. We can then arrive at an answer to the question which at present we cannot answer, that is: What is TAA worth? We have heard a lot of hysteria from the Australian Labor Party on this matter, which is central to its socialist ideology. I believe that if the Australian Labor Party shook off its blinkers and looked at the matter it also would come to the conclusion 1 presently hold that a more competitive TAA will be achieved if there is direct public shareholding. 1 believe that the regulated environment would be all the better for that.
The fourth initiative that has been announced by the Government is the intention to establish a deregulation inquiry, and again I refer to what Holcroft said. In simple terms, he said: '1 determine faults in the regulated environment and I list by way of recommendations ways in which I think you can improve it. But even if you do everything I say you should do to improve the regulated environment, you will end up only with second best.' In my view, the Holcroft committee's most important conclusion was that we should be proceeding down the road towards progressive deregulation. The Minister in his 2 April statement to this Parliament, when dealing with the Airlines Equipment (Loan Guarantees) Bill, announced the intention to set up the inquiry. Again I say that the two most important aspects yet to be determined are the terms of reference for that inquiry and the personnel who will conduct it. I can see no reason why those matters should not be decided later this year when Parliament returns for the Budget session. As the Minister announced, the inquiry will be asked to report within five years. If the inquiry is set up properly, with dinkum terms of reference and with people who

Fifthly, ] come to the proposed two-airline agreement itself. The agreement takes into account the Government's decisions on the independent air fares committee and on TAA. Before dealing with that in a little greater detail, I would like to summarise the stage we have reached. In my mind, there has been definite progress. There have been air fare reductions on long haul routes and we have seen a foreshadowing of further changes in the relativities between fares for long and short routes. It is intended to establish an independent committee separate from the Department of Transport to determine future fares, to monitor the cost methodology, and to provide that public accountability that is so necessary in a regulated environment. A new two airline agreement has been negotiated to take into account the decisions of the committee and the decisions on TAA. It is intended to establish an inquiry to investigate whether deregulation is a viable objective for our future domestic aviation needs.
These are all significant undertakings and statements of intent which, in my view, mark important and welcome shifts in Government policy. Honourable members will recall that only a little while ago that policy did not really accept that the fare relativities between long and short haul routes were inequitable or unjust. Only a little while ago that policy was firmly committeed to the reins of regulatory control being held exclusively by the Department of Transport, which was committed to the continuance of TAA as a statutory corporation. It was based on a seemingly unshakable commitment to the long term maintenance of the two-airline system. [ believe that we have progressed, that we are taking steps in the right direction, and that further progress can be expected as we move down the path that has now been chosen.
In my last five minutes I want to turn to the two-airline agreement. I oppose the two-airline policy. I have opposed it in the Parliament for as long as I have been here, and my opposition is well known outside the Parliament. I accept that the proposed agreement contains improvements over

3012 REPRESENTATIVES 3 June 1981

Airlines Agreement Bill

previous agreements-improvements with respect to freight, the air fares committee, and public accountability. But for every improvement we can find complaints and criticisms, such as the legitimate concerns of East-West Airlines Ltd, the concern that Qantas Airways Ltd will not be able to play its proper role, and so on. I remain opposed to the two-airline policy as such because I believe it denies entrepreneurs freedom to enter the market, it denies the Australian travelling public significant carri~r choice, and it offers the public little realistic price value choice in terms of fares. I accept that the policy does have some benefits, such as flight frequency and a very high standard of in-flight service, but I do not think that they outweigh the disadvantages, and they are certainly benefits that are provided at an inflated cost.
In the past I have called the two-airline agreement a piece of economic madness which is costing Australia dearly. I believe the present system is based on an inflated cost due to operational inefficiency. It is my view that a deregulated system would open up the system to competitive market forces and would see all features-price, frequency and service-based on and responsive to consumer demand. I have to accept the possibility that I may be completely wrong. My prejudice against the two-airline policy and in favour of progressive deregulation may be completely wrong. In that light, I congratulate the Government for opting for the only responsible course available to it, that is, to investigate the situation. The deregulation inquiry is a first-class initiative. I welcome it with all the support I can muster. For the first time in 20-odd years we will look at other possibilities and make a decision as to whether they can be applied to Australia. We will look at the American experience, not only from the perspective of the airlines but also from the perspective of those people we too readily forget-the passengers. The deregulation inquiry will report within five years.
Given that fact, what bothers me most about this legislative package is the life of the new agreement. I believe we should put ourselves in a position whereby we will make a decision on the deregulation inquiry at the end of five years. The airlines agreement as it is presently drafted and the Bill which gives effect to that agreement places no requirement on anybody to do anything after five years. We cannot do anything before five years, but after five years we do not have to do anything either. If honourable members doubt that I ask them to look closely at the terms of clause 22(4) of the agreement. Over the last few days we have heard a lot about whether five plus

three makes eight, whether three from eight equals five and so forth. I want to try to reduce the whole issue to this fundamental point. As clause 22(4) is presently drafted, it provides for a five year minimum plus an indefinite period during which we mayor may not give notice of something. It then has a three-year period of termination. Effectively we have a minimum of eight years but a maximum that is indeterminate. I have advocated strongly in the past that we should have a five-year maxamum. I will save my comments on that matter for the Committee debate because I am running out of time. I foreshadow that I will argue that we should opt for a five plus three situation but that we should make the minimum of eight years as provided for in the agreement a maximum of eight years. Amendments to be moved in my name have been circulated. I will leave my further remarks for the Committee stage of the Bill.

That concerned the suggestion on the permanent use of Qantas on east-west fiights-
put forward by the honourable member is certainly a feasible one in terms of administration. The Minister may defend himself, if he wishes to, by pointing out that there are enough caveats and loopholes for him to whip a horse and cart through. Therefore, Western Australians cannot, I believe, look forward with the degree of confidence they ought to a rapid gaining of access to Qantas flights on the east-west trip.
I remind honourable members that various estimates made of the value to the Western Australian travelling public of the use of Qantas seats have put the reduction of fares as high as 50 per cent.
