We cannot guarantee in any aspect of this legislation that the situation will be alleviated in the future.
The Governments adjustments to air fares were a joke. But they were accepted by gullible members opposite from Western Australia as the forerunner of more cuts. All the groundwork was laid for a rapid reallocation of costs by the Holcroft committee. A considerable amount of work was done by committee members on that matter. As I said, the groundwork was laid for a rapid decision. But under the Bill there is no obligation on the committee to report in under three years. I will refer to the Minister's speech on 1 April and his comments on whether there would be further air fare reductions. To a very considerable extent these can only be based on a proper appreciation of the need to reallocate costs. The Minister stated:
However, the inquiry recognised a considerable amount of detailed work would still be necessary to derive and verify the most appropriate fair setting formula based on current costs.
He went on:
The Government will arrange immediately further substantial discussions with the airlines so this task can be completed by July 1981.
The honourable member for Tangney expressed hope that when the next air fare rise came about such adjustments would be made. The simple fact of the matter is there is now established a body given responsibility for reallocating costs. That will provide a perfect excuse for this Government-and it has not very often needed perfect excuses in the past to do what it wants-to evade having to do that cost reallocation so that we in Western Australia can expect, three years from now perhaps-no doubt promises will be made before the next election by the Liberal Party advertisers doing a soft shoe shuffle-the fare reductions that we might get in the west. Of course, that may well come to naught after the next election. That question has been made 'public proof' with the terms of the Committee's reference. As I would suggest, in the end nothing will come of it. Therefore we still have the situation where the air fares to Canberra from Sydney is $112, from Melbourne $145, from Adelaide $232, from Brisbane $214 but from Perth $504.

Airlines Agreement Bill

3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3015

Over the years quite effective pressure groups have been established in Western Australia to demand air fare justice. Nothing in the Bill gives such groups automatic access to the committee they have striven so hard to create. At cost allocation reviews such groups will appear at the Committee's pleasure. If the groups are not made parties to the agreement they, like the rest of the public, have no automatic right of appearance; nor do they have the right to know what transpires at Committee hearings. Part of the hearings-a part vital to a Committee decisionmay be held in private. On top of that, interest groups have no right of access to major and minor air fare reviews.
As the honourable member for Tangney pointed out the Committee must be trustworthy. The Holcroft inquiry pointed to the dangers of regulators identifying with the interests of the regulated and not the interests of the public, on whose behalf they should be acting. This applies to the Government in the case of the Holcroft inquiry. It will apply to the Committee in the case of its composition. We have not been given any guarantees in the Minister's second reading speech that the Government will appoint to this Committee the type of person who will not purely be a lackey of the Independent Air Fares Committee interests. The importance of doing so I think was well outlined on page 238 of volume 1 of the Holcroft committee report. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard that page of recommendations in order to be able to conclude my remarks and to give other honourable members time to speak.
Leave granted.
The document read as jollows-
15.9 The problems of accountability in an industry or activity that is treated for regulatory purposes as in some sense part of the public sector are especially testing. The concept of accountability is bound to embrace the activities of the regulatory agency as well as the performance of the regulated participants. For example, accountability requirements imposed upon the regulated industry may reveal shortcomings which also reflect upon the workings of the regulatory agency. In such circumstance the regulatory agency may develop as much of an interest as the regulated participants in limiting the information available on past and present performance.
15.10 A positive concern with accountability must involve the dissemination of information on the workings of the regulated industry and the regulatory agency in such a manner as to allow third parties, especially the users of services of the regulated industry, to make judgments about the efficiency and effectiveness of the regulatory agency as well as of the regulated industry.
15.11 It may be objected that the regulated industry, while having to meet the requirements of the regulatory agency, also has to meet other obligations in the marketplace with all the reporting requirements that are laid down for private sector firms. However, this objection is misplaced if the provisions for an industry specially regulated by the mechanisms of the

public sector also involve withdrawing some of the normal checks applied to market performance. 15.12 Air passenger transport activity bears witness to the complexity of arrangements associated with regulation. For example, the two major participants, TAA and Ansett, are not engaged solely in this activity so there is no clear basis to?
Mr BEAZLEY-I thank the House. Neither the membership nor the public accountability of this Committee conforms to the standards that the Holcroft committee laid down. The failure to conform to the standards that the Holcroft committee laid down, I believe, means that in the future there will be considerable difficulties in ensuring that the travelling public is treated justly by the two-airline agreement. There is to be no consumer representative on the Independent Air Fares Committee. Public access and public accountability are highly restricted. The Opposition will move amendments that will provide honourable members opposite with a chance again to assert their principles on both those accounts. I believe that the Opposition's amendments when they are moved must be supported. The honourable member for Tangney talked of the progress of sweetness and light on this matter, but it appears that quite bloody and bruising battles have taken place opposite. Honourable members have gone on record via leaks and direct public statements. Their statements are all on record; they all match amendments that the Opposition is moving. We look forward with very great confidence to a few thumping victories this evening.
Mr CHARLES JONES (Newcastle) (5.53)-The Bills before the House in this cognate debate are the Airlines Agreement Bill, the Independent Air Fares Committee Bill and the Airlines Equipment Amendment Bill. The introduction of these pieces of legislation into the Parliament has certainly brought about considerable conflict within the ranks of the Government members who have all been pushing their own lobby's point of view. The result is that the Cabinet and the Government have been in one hell of a mess ever since the airlines agreement was first brought to negotiation. In a country the size of Australia, with a population as large as it is, with a limited number of air travellers, there are some advantages in having a genuine two-airline agreement and an arrangement which would put the airline system into a truly competitive position and which at the same time would give the people of this country a first-class airline system at fair and reasonable prices.
Ever since the original airline agreement was first negotiated and brought into legislation by the Menzies Government in 1952 the result has been one thing only. On that occasion the legislation was designed to protect Ansett Airlines of

3016 REPRESENTATIVES 3 June 1981

Airlines Agreement Bill

Australia from Trans-Australia Airlines or any other competitor which might move into the field. I draw the attention of the House to the case only a few years ago when IPEC endeavoured to get into the airline business, into the lurk, into the monopoly operation; in order to get a licence to print money. That is what the airline agreement has developed into-a licence for the private operators to print money. In such a situation there is a need for a renegotiation of the airline agreement.
A limitation of five years notice of termination under the old agreement has been mentioned. The position under the old agreement was that it was almost impossible to terminate the agreement. The present agreement has five years to run. With three years notice of termination the position could well be that at the end of this Parliament in 1983 we could have an Australian Labor Party government. In 1985 the five years would have run its course. The Labor Party could give notice of termination of the agreement at that point. When the three years notice expires there could be a change of government. So, we then have a change of policy.
This airlines agreement should be a sunset agreement. If we have a five-year agreement then at the end of five years that agreement should terminate. There should not be any notice of termination of the agreement. When the agreement is approaching the end of its five-year term it is up to the government of the day and the parties to the agreement to sit down to talk about it, to negotiate a new agreement that would then become operative, if it is agreed to by the Parliament. This should be the practice instead of this humbug, this means whereby Ansett can get out of having the agreement renegotiated. The Minister for Transport (Mr Hunt) in his speech drew attention to the fact that in 1974 I, as Minister for Transport, wanted to give the people of Western Australia another service. Thereby TAA would have been in competition with MacRobertson Miller Airline Services which, after all, is only another name for Ansett Transport Industries Ltd. The Labor Government was going to fit TAA with DC9s. It was going to give the people the option of two fares-the first class fare and the economy class fare. At that time all the people using the MacRobertson Miller airline had to pay the first class fare. There was no economy fare. Our proposal involved a 25 per cent reduction in fares for the people. How long did it take to get that decision that was taken by the Labor Government put into effect? It was some three and a half years. By the use of the terms of the agreement and the High Court of Australia and

all the rest of it the people were prevented from getting this opportunity to have lower fares. The Minister in his second reading speech said:
In its submission to the public hearings of the Holcraft Inquiry into Domestic Air Fares in Port Hedland. TAA claimed that between November 1977, when the DC9 services commenced. and August 1980, 41,168 passengers had used the DC9 services. These passengers had paid some $1.5m less for their travel than if they had usedF28 services. a saving of over $25 for each passenger.
That is what the first two-airline agreement meant to the people of Western Australia. The members of the Liberal Party in Western Australia and the members of the Liberal and Country parties in this Government were instrumental in forcing the people of Western Australia to pay the extra charges on the airlines operations in Western Australia. The proposal could have resulted in cheaper fares for those people. What happened occurred just because the Government wanted to pay for services rendered to it by Ansett. Ansett has been protected for years by the Liberal and Country Party governments of this country. Now we have the set-up with Murdoch and Sir Peter Abeles in Ansett Transport Indus" tries Ltd. Here once again is the pay-off. Just as with the pay-off earlier today with-the amendments of the Broadcasting and Television Act, this pay-off is for services rendered to the Government by Ansett and by Murdoch in the 1975, 1977 and 1980 elections.
Sitting suspended from 6 to 8 p.m.
Mr CHARLES JONES-The Airlines Agreement Bill and the two associated Bills are very important pieces of legislation. One of the most unfortunate aspects of this debate is the limited time the Parliament will give to considering these major Bills. Without repeating what I said prior to the suspension of the sitting, I wish to refer to a couple of matters. The first relates to the set-up of Ansett Transport Industries Ltd. a company that covers a multitude of undertakings. No real consideration has been given to the composition of this company, as to whether it is a transport company or a media company, or whether it will be involved in television operations as a result of the legislation introduced earlier today. That legislation is part of a pay-off to Murdoch for services rendered to this Government in the three elections held in 1975, 1977 and 1980. Honourable members know that what I am saying is true.
I draw the attention of honourable members to the fact that Ansett Transport Industries operates in the airline business not only as Ansett Airlines of Australia but also as Ansett Airlines of New Souih Wales and MacRobertson Miller. One can go through the various States and see the names

Airlines Agreement Bill

3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3017

under which Ansett Transport Industries operates. The company also owns a number of tourist resorts and touring buses throughout Australia. The Wellington Hotel, which is just down the road from Parliament House, is owned by Ansett Transport Industries. The company deals in interstate bus and freight operations, and it also operates as a furniture removalist. This is all part and parcel of its operation. When Sir Reginal Ansett was Chairman he took the company into television. As a result, we now have a situation where this agreement will have a major effect on all the undertakings in which Ansett Transport Industries is involved.
I remind honourable members that Ansett had a very substantial shareholding in Associated Securities Ltd. Ansett employees were encouraged to take out debentures and secured and unsecured notes. Finally ASL folded up after Ansett Transport Industries had been given a guarantee by this Parliament for the borrowing of money from the Exim Bank of the United States of America, thus enabling the company to borrow money at much lower rates of interest than would normally apply and to use the cash flow that the airline industry provides for a company to try to keep ASL alive. Unfortunately, these matters have not been taken into consideration by this Parliament. I believe it is wrong to rush this legislation through the Parliament. Consideration should be given to referring the legislation to a parliamentary select committee or to one of the Parliament's legislation committees, rather than pushing it through as we are doing at present.
One of the clauses in the Airlines Agreement Bill places us in an intolerable situation. The clause seeks to allow one of the private operators to decide whether Qantas Airways Ltd will be permitted to operate on domestic routes. One of the two operators, Ansett or TAA, can veto Qantas's operations. Do we intend to reverse the position and allow Qantas to veto the operation of Ansett and TAA on the international routes? These matters have not been explained in detail. I am concerned about the Press reports and statements we have received from East-West Airlines. I have great regard and respect for that airline because I think it has done an excellent job. The former Chairman, Don Shand, was a great man in aviation, and I am certain that the Minister for Transport, who is seated at the table, would not dispute that point of view, having lived fairly close to him. I had great respect for Don Shand and the airline he operated, and I have the same respect for the present General Manager, Mr Riley. These people are all concerned about what is happening.

Budget Rent-a-Car Systems Pty Ltd, which admittedly is not owned by Bob Ansett but by the Bank of New South Wales, is putting forward the point of view that the agreement should not be passed by the Parliament at this stage without its being given further serious consideration. It is for these reasons that I say there is a need for this legislation to be considered by a select committee or by one of the Parliament's legislation committees. I am surprised at the hide of the signatories to the agreement, but I think I can guess where it came from. It came from Ansett. The name of Sir Reginald Ansett has been maligned, but unfortunately the airline carries his name. He is not the guilty man; the guilty men are Sir Peter Abeles and Murdoch, but the fact is that we still have to refer to the airline as Ansett. I now turn to clause 10 (3) of the Schedule to the Airlines Agreement Bill, which states:
The Commonwealth agrees that it will not increase the amount of tax (including customs and excise duties) payable in respect of each litre of aviation fuel in any period of twelve months by a greater amount or rate than the corresponding amount or rate of any increases in tax in that period imposed on motor spirit.
It is like the hide of them to require the Australian Parliament to give them that assurance, that is, that the Commonwealth Government will not increase aviation fuel taxation or excise. Does the electorate have any assurance from the Government that it will not increase fuel tax? Does it have any assurance that the Government will not increase income tax? The Government gave the assurance to Ansett. It has tied it to some other remotely related form of taxation in a tax for roads. I will never forget what Harold Holt said back in about 1962 when he was the Treasurer. I asked him a question about fuel tax and he said that petrol was only another commodity that the Parliament reserved the right to tax and that the Parliament should not assent to agreements such as the one to which I have referred namely, the agreement contained in clause 10 (3). That clause should be deleted from the agreement, and I am disappointed to find that the Minister is prepared to agree to such a clause being included in the agreement. I believe that parallel scheduling by the two airlines, Ansett and TAA, is a waste of capacity. Day after day two aeroplanes fly simultaneously between major centres, in many cases with only half the capacity of the aeroplane occupied, where one aeroplane could do the whole job. Unfortunately, this situation applies under the agreement as far as parallel scheduling is concerned. The airlines stick rigidly to the principle. Neither will give an inch.
In the limited time available to me I wish to make reference to the Independent Air Fares

3018 REPRESENTATIVES ,3 June 1981

Airlines Agreement Bill

Committee which it is proposed to set up. The Committee is to 'comprise a part time chairman and tWo part time members. I would like some indiCation from the Minister, if he should reply to the debate, as to whom he is considering for those positions. What sort of people is he looking at? Without mentioning names, of course, the appointment of a judge has been mentioned. If he was a full time judge, he would not receive any special allowance as the Chairman of the Independent Air Fares Committee. I am more interested in establishing who the three people will be. What will be their connection with the aviation industry?
I draw the attention of honourable members to the constitutional powers of this Parliament to set up an interstate commission. Legislation to establish such a commission went through the Parliament in October 1975. That legislation has never been proclaimed by this Government. Admittedly, avia~ion was excluded from the amendments which we were forced to accept. Of course, that was all done at that time. Let us be clear and frank about that. It was done because of the pressure that Ansett Transport Industries was able to wield on the Liberal-National Country Party Opposition. At that time the Australian Labor Party, unfortunately, did not have the numbers in the Senate as was later proven on 11 November 1975. We could not get the legislation for the establishment of the interstate commission through in the form we wanted it through. I had to accept the amendments in this House rather than let the amendments be introduced in the other place. The Liberal-National Country Party Opposition at that time, under pressure from Ansett, was successful in forcing us to accept the deletion of any reference to aviation.
It would be quite easy for this Government to bring that commission back into being. I believe it would be of great value as far as transport is concerned. Nobody really knows the cost of carrying a tonne of freight, whether it be by road transport or by rail transport. We do have some idea of the cost of a tonne of freight carried by ships because shipping gets very little subsidisation compared with the other two modes of transport. So the Government should be looking at this aspect rather than setting up a separate identity to deal with just one facet of transport, namely, the fares to be charged on the main trunk route operations. I ask the Minister to give some serious consideration to this matter. Once again I think this matter should be referred to a select committee. It is one thing that that committee could have a very good look at. It could also look at providing the means for correctly assessing the rates that will be

charged by the airlines. While people are only appointed to part time positions their connections are questionable. Pressures can be applied to them in relation to the fares to be charged. I am not satisfied with the means whereby these people will be appointed. I think it is a matter that should be considered in much greater depth than we have considered it at this time. Having in mind the desires of the House to go into Committee I conclude my remarks.
Question resolved in the affirmative.

Mr SHACK-I seek your guidance, Mr Deputy Chairman. I have circulated an amendment relating to clause 2. As I have said, that is the first clause that can be amended. I move:
Clause 2, page 1, line 8, omit sub-clause (2), substitute the following sub-clause:
'(2) Section 3 shall come into operation at the expiration of the period of 90 days referred to in sub-clause 2 (3) of the agreement referred to in section 5'.
The whole thrust of my contribution to this debate is that the Parliament should provide for an end point to the new airlines agreement. Equally, I believe, it should provide for a definite end point for the existing legislation. As the Bill is drafted at the moment, clause 2 (2) states:
Section j shall come into operation on a day to be fixed by Proclamation.
Proclamation is an uncertain time. I see it as the role of this Parliament to place a definite end point on when section 3 shall come into operation. 3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3019

2 (2), as presently drafted, states that section 3 shall come into operation on a day to be fixed by proclamation. My amendment, if passed, would mean that the repeal clause would come into effect at the expiration of the period of 90 days which is provided for in the agreement. Subclauses 1 (3) (a), (b) and (c) of the Schedule which is, in fact, the agreement, state that the remainder of the agreement shall come into operation on the day of occurrence of the last of the following events: The first event is an Act of the Parliament providing for the establishment of the Independent Air Fares Committee; the second event is the amendments to clause 8 related to the Customs (Prohibited Imports) Regulations and the Airlines Equipment Act; and the third event is the 90-day period which, in everyday speech, is the opt-out clause which is provided for in clause 2 (3) of the Schedule. The new agreement will come into existence after the expiration of 90 days. 1 believe that is also the time when all existing agreements should be repealed. That is my purpose in moving the amendment.

Mr MORRIS (Shortland) (8.32)-1 move the following amendment standing in my name:
Page 3, after clause 8, add the following new clause: Cessation of operation of Act '9. (l) This Act, unless sooner repealed, shall cease to be in force at the expiration of 5 years after the date of commencement of this Act. (2) For the purposes of section 8 of the Acts Interpretation Act 190I, when this Act ceases to be in force by virtue of sub-section (1), it shall thereupon be deemed to have been repealed by an Act other than this Act.'
The purpose of the amendment is to limit the life of the agreement to five years, which is the minimum life under the existing agreement. In the view of the Opposition, the Government has no mandate to extend the life of the agreement, whether by one year to six, three years to eight or five years to ten. It has been revealed in recent times that a secret deal was concluded between the proprietor of Ansett Airlines of Australia, Sir Peter Abeles, and the Government to double the life of the agreement to lO years. That deal was made prior to 17 March last year, the date on which Ansett, with a great deal of fanfare at a national Press conference, announced its decision to purchase its new Boeing fleet. The doubling of the agreement to 10 years was carefully concealed from the Parliament and from the public by the Government, by the Ansett firm and by Sir Peter Abeles, despite the expensive fanfare associated with the announcement of the purchase of the new Boeing fleet. I refer specifically to the speech of the Minister for Transport (Mr Hunt) made in this House on the last night of the last Parliament. On page 2, he stated:
We have now reached a stage where discussions on principles with the airlines have been finalised and drafting of the new agreement is nearly completed.
That was the evening of 18 September last year. Some 16 hours later it was announced that the Minister, Ansett Airlines and the Australian

National Airlines Commission had sign.ed a new agreement. But it was carefully concealed that the new agreement provided for a doubling of its life to 10 years. I quote also from the Minister's Press release of 19 September last year recording that event. Paragraph 3, in reference to the new two airlines agreement, states:
The terms of the new agreement are along the lines of the principles outlined in the second reading speech of the Australian National Airlines Act introduced into the House of Representatives on 18 September 1980.
The Minister was careful to make no reference in his speech or in that Press statement to the doubling of the life of the agreement or to the veto power being provided to Ansett to prevent the use of Qantas Airways Ltd capacity in the provision of direct domestic services across Australia. There was a conspiracy of silence to delude this Parliament and to delude the Australian electorate because the Government was then going out on an election campaign. Not one word was uttered, except for a report that appeared in the Australian Financial Review of 22 September. That report referred to a 10-year agreement. It is reported that the Minister refused to confirm the accuracy or otherwise of that lO-year agreement. So he knew there were questions raised about it but he refused to confirm it. He denied that information to Parliament, as did the airline itself. Sir Peter Abeles, his colleagues and this Government have engaged in a conspiracy of silence to deny essential information to the Parliament and to the public. What of the term of 10 years? I quote from no lesser personage than the Secretary to the Department of Transport, the Minister's own top adviser. A few weeks after this deal was done, on 15 April 1980 in an address to the Committee for the Economic Development of Australia, that gentleman said:
In listening to me, you might bear in mind that in a fast changing world a span of 10 years is a long period over which to make predictions.
In other words, he was saying it was difficult in planning the transport scene and in developing transport policy to make any kind of firm predictions on that time scale. A five-year period, as proposed in the amendment the Opposition has put to the chamber, would enable reviews of the agreement to be carried out in, say, the third year or the fourth year. It would enable the effectiveness of the new arrangements to be assessed and an assessment of market changes to be made. It would enable Parliament and the government of the day to appraise the features of the new generation short haul aircraft. It would enable the Government and the Department of Transport to evaluate changes in the tourist industry, including changes in demand. It would enable government

Why was the matter kept secret? It was kept secret because the Fair Internal Air Fares Organisation in Western Australia had proposed to mount a candidate at the Senate election in late 1980. This deal was done in March 1980. I do not know which Government back benchers knew about it. I understand some Government back benchers from Western Australia knew about it but they did not have the courage to tell their constituents, nor did they have the courage to tell the Parliament or the people of what had been consummated between Ansett Airlines and this Government. The principals of the Fair Internal Air Fares campaign disclosed subsequently that they learned of the new term of the agreement some three days prior to the election and they knew that a number of Western Australian back benchers also knew about it. But the campaign committee thought that at that time, so close to the election, there was little point in revealing the information to the public. So we have the reasons for the conspiracy of silence and the participants in it. This legislation has now been brought in. What does Sir Peter Abeles say? On 27 May, having engaged in an exercise to conceal critical information from this Parliament and from the electorate, he then had the temerity to make a statement to the Australian Financial Review. On 27 May its report stated:

port from the men of integrity on that side of the chamber, the men who put public interest first, it is the Opposition's intention there will be a review in year three and year four of the five-year term in which we would carry out the kind of analyses and assessments that I have indicated. Recognising that immediate changes cannot be made to existing arrangements because of their complexity, then those changes could be discussed and debated and means established to determine how new equipment could be brought in, how air fare structures could be changed if necessary, and how greater flexibility and better service could be provided to the Australian people. I commend the amendment to the men of integrity opposite who put public interest first.

Mr MORRIS (Shortland) (8.45)-The comments of the honourable member for Tangney (Mr Shack) sought to give the impression that the legal advice he has in respect of the amendment I have moved is the correct advice. The fact is that the Schedule and the Bill have been very cleverly drafted, and the Minister acknowledges that.
Mr Hunt-No, I did not.
Mr MORRIS-I thought that the Minister did. The expert legal advice given to me is that the form of the amendment I have moved, given the contents of the Bill and the manner in which the agreement has been drafted and contained in the Schedule, would be effective if carried. It is no good the honourable member trying to hide behind the prop that his advice or the advice to his colleagues is that our amendment would not be good law. The simple fact is that the Government was extremely clever in the manner in which it drafted the legislation. Clause 22 (1) of the agreement provides that the agreement shall continue in force unless and until determined in accordance with its provisions. The Standing Orders prevent the moving of an amendment to a Schedule contained in a Bill. If we consider those facts we recognise something of the cleverness of the Government.

As I mentioned earlier, from the beginning this has been a very clever and secret deal. Firstly, the public was denied knowledge of what was happening. The deal had been done. Secondly, the Bill was drafted in such a way as to be amendment-proof. Irrespective of what the honourable member for Tangney has just said, I repeat that the legal advice I have taken from experts similar in standing to those who advised the honourable member, is that the amendment in the form we have proposed would be good law and would be effective in terms of the Standing Orders and in terms of the laws of this nation.
In conclusion, the five-year period we are pro-. posing is the minimum currency of the existing agreement. Nobody on the Government side, nobody in the airline industry-not even Sir Peter or Mr Murdoch-has said that the five-year notice in the existing agreement is not sufficient time. Not one word has been uttered. There has been general acceptance of that time. All we are asking of honourable members opposite-those men of integrity and those who put public interest first-is that they confirm the policy they have been pursuing for the past five years under existing legislation. If they want to see the Australian tourist industry develop, if they want to see Australians get a better deal in airline services, if they want to see cheaper fares and greater public accountability, and the right of the government of the day to control airline policy, they will support the amendment I have moved.

Mr CHARLES JONES-We are debating an amendment. Thank you, Mr Deputy Chairman, for your assistance. Whilst we are dealing with an amendment to insert a new clause 9 in the Bill, in actual fact we are talking about clause 22 (1), (2), (3) and (4) of the Schedule to the Bill, that is the agreement. It is ridiculous that the present two-airline agreement has a five-year period and then five years notice of termination. AlII can say is that in another two years and one month the five years notice of termination will be included.
This Government should have the intestinal fortitude to do what a Labor Government would do and terminate the agreement, forcing the parties to come to the negotiating table to work out

some sort of agreement acceptable to them and to the Parliament. The agreement is not worth anything unless the Parliament passes this Bill. Therefore, I say to those honourable members who I know are in favour of the five-year agreement to give it a go and let us see where we get to. That is the situation. I ask honourable members to give it serious consideration.

MrSHACK (Tangney) (9.10)-I move:

(2) Page 3, after clause 8 add the following new clause: Cessation of operation of Act '9. (1) This Act, unless sooner repealed, shall cease to be ill force at the expiration of 8 years after the day on which the agreement referred to in section 5 comes into force and effect. (2) For the purposes of section 8 of the Acts Interpretation Act 1901, when this Act ceases to be in force by virtue of sub-section (1), it shall thereupon be deemed to have been repealed by an Act other than this Act.'.

In the second reading debate I spoke about the amendment to some extent. Suffice is to say that if the amendment were agreed to it would see the insertion of a new clause 9. I believe this to be a legally responsible amendment. It complements the agreement in that the minimum eight years as provided for in the agreement would, under this amendment, become a maximum of eight years. It coincides with the timing of the deregulation inquiry in that the Government has announced that the inquiry will report within five years. The Government of the day will then be in a position to give notice after five years and consideration of the findings of the deregulation inquiry. If the Government chose to go down the deregulatory path there would be a three-year transitionary period, after which time the agreement in to to would expire at the end of eight years.

Mr MORRIS (Shortland) (9.12)-The amendment moved by the honourable member for Tangney (Mr Shack) is in the view of the Opposition a second best proposition. It suffers from the same doubts, on legal advice, as did the amendment that 1 moved on behalf of the Opposition. It is not correct to say that this amendment would be effective and that the other one would not have been. As I said, we regard it as second best. The Opposition will be supporting the amendment.

Mr HYDE (Moore) (9.13)-1 wish to make only one point; namely, that 1 do not believe the

amendment suffers from the same legal doubts. The eight years were guaranteed to the companies under the agreement that they had signed. There is opportunity for the government of the day to fill that agreement to the letter with the acceptance of this amendment moved by the honourable member for Tangney (Mr Shack). This would not have been possible with the amendment moved by the honourable member for Shortland (Mr Morris),
Mr HUNT (Gwydir-Minister for Transport) (9.14)-The Government rejects the amendment. The Government has resolved to establish an independent inquiry into the advisability or otherwise of deregulation. This committee of inquiry is to report within five years. If the committee should report in favour of deregulation and the government of the day resolves to adopt those recommendations the government would have the opportunity of serving notice, at the end of five years of operation of the agreement, to terminate that agreement three years later. This would enable an orderly transition from a regulated two airlines arrangement to a deregulated environment.
There is no doubt that at least three years would be required to enable the Government and the airlines to make the necessary infrastructure arrangements at airports and elsewhere to implement a deregulated system. It would enable airlines and other interested groups to gear themselves up to take advantage of a deregulated system. On the other hand, the sunset clause in the agreement would terminate the agreement at eight years regardless of the report of the inquiry. It could well result in the government of the day, say a Labor government, not exercising its right to give notice to terminate the agreement, thus leaving the airline industry in considerable uncertainty until the airline agreement concludes at the end of eight years. Such an agreement would result in aviation policy being dropped into a vacuum at the end of eight years. Such a policy would lead to a chaotic situation and could well result in the ultimate nationalisation of the airlines system which is clearly the objective of the Opposition.
I believe the difference between the Government's approach and the approach involved in the amendment is the difference between order and chaos. This amendment would not be in the best interests of the travelling public. In view of the decisions taken by the Government, and leaving aside the Airlines Agreement Bill before the chamber but including the decisions relating to Trans-Australia Airlines and the decisions to

Airlines Agreement Bill

3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3027

establish an inquiry into deregulation, it is undoubtedly a logical and rational approach to support a five-year agreement with a provision for three years notice to terminate the agreement following any decision that a future government might take resulting from the deregulation inquiry.
Moreover, the legal advice I have obtained from the Attorney-General (Senator Durack) is that if the amendment were accepted by the Government, or, carried by the Parliament, both the Airlines Agreements Act and the agreement itself would be thrown into doubt and confusion. It would, therefore, be ridiculous for the Parliament to accept those circumstances. I am advised that the amendment would also have the effect of repealing the Customs prohibited import regulations thus destroying the basis of the twoairlines policy. The Government, therefore, cannot accept such a proposition or the amendment.
Mr BEAZLEY (Swan) (9.17)-1 support the amendment that was moved by the honourable member for Tangney (Mr Shack). In doing so I make a couple of points about it. The Minister for Transport (Mr Hunt) has waxed moral on the issue of the chaos that the airlines would be thrown into if this amendment were passed. The Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser), however, made quite clear over the weekend what the intentions of the Government were with the Schedule and the way in which the Government devised the Schedule, with particular relation to this eight-year clause. He pointed out that the purpose of the legislation, and particularly this clause, was to make it Labor election proof. In other words, the purpose of the Government was not orderly airline policy. The purpose of the Government was, by whatever means possible, to frustrate the Australian Labor Party eight years in advance. I think that is a disgraceful position to be presenting to the Australian public, even if it was disclosed at a closed session of the Liberal Party and was subsequently leaked. If that is the Government's motive it deserves all that it gets on this item.
I do not believe that the advice of the Attorney General (Senator Durack) is any more valid than the advice that has been given by the various legal sources of both the honourable member for Tangney and the honourable member for Shortland (Mr Morris). If the advice of the Attorney General turns out to be correct, it will be correct because of the way in which the Government has gone about negotiating the agreement. Its intentions are to frustrate the capacity of the Parliament to operate in a normal way when dealing with an agreement such as this. But it will not happen because the legal advice that we have

received, which is the same legal advice that the honourable member for Tangney has received, is good and therefore, the amendment should pass.

Mr CADMAN (Mitchell) (9.19)-1 will be brief. There are many great advantages in what is being proposed by the Government. Whilst J understand the attractions of the proposition put by the honourable member for Tangney (Mr Shack) I am afraid that 1 disagree with the thrust of what he is doing in light of the pressure of time and the circumstances. There has been much gained. He admitted this in his speech at the second reading stage. The proposal that there be a five-year period plus a three-year notice period, as the honourable member has moved, would tend to deny the opportunity for open discussion concerning the setting of air fares. It would also tend to prejudice the deregulation inquiry. The full evaluation of the report of the Holcroft Independent Public Inquiry into Domestic Air Fares, I believe, would also be put in jeopardy if this amendment were to be carried.

I say to my colleagues who tend to support this amendment that, as Shane Paltridge did, we have broken new ground in the airline system in Australia today. I think that we have put in place a proposition that will allow all people, whether they live in the east or the west of this country, to look forward to opportunities they have not had previously. If those opportunities were denied to them, as indeed the Opposition would deny them, it would be a great shame. I indicate in passing that the Opposition completely opposed the idea of differential equipment and the opportunity of breaking up the tight and tied system. The Opposition has spoken against it and has opposed that proposition. We on this side of the chamber have moved forward to advantage the people of Australia. J think that from this point we can see advance, and there will be opportunities for further advance. It would be a great shame if those opportunities were lost.

Mrs DARLING (Lilley) (9.43)-Briefly, I would like to support what the honourable member for Maranoa (Mr Ian Cameron) has just said. I share his concern regarding clause 12. In Queensland it could force Bush Pilots Airways out of business. The Airlines Equipment Amendment Bill gives the Minister sole power to determine the number of future aircraft and flights to be operated by every airline. This number having been arbitrarily decided, the airline is not permitted to operate additional flights if passenger demand suddenly increases. This clause relates to airlines being forced out of a service. If larger airlines decide to opt out of servicing country routes-as the agreement allows them to do if they find them unprofitable-it is reasonable to assume that passenger demand for an airline such as Bush Pilots Airways will increase. Under this agreement Bush Pilots Airways will be locked in. It would be compelled to refuse the business under threat of heavy penalties, if Ansett Airlines of Australia or Trans-Australia Airlines should withdraw and Bush Pilots Airways have to meet that demand.
Mr Bradfield-That is not right.
Mrs DARLING-That is right. Under the agreement, Bush Pilots Airways will be locked in.
n will be compelled to refuse business under
threat of heavy penalties, according to this agreement, once a certain number of flights is agreed upon. I do not know whether the honourable member realises this. The same ludicrous situation will occur if demand drops. It may be required by this legislation to operate, as near as is practicable, a set number of approved flights despite the drop in demand. It could even be obliged to run empty flights to satisfy the needs of the agreement. This situation could definitely crop up in Queensland if

clause 12 is agreed to. The larger airlines, if they find it unprofitable, coud drop those routes. They would not be serviced. Bush Pilots Airways will have to pick up those routes and start servicing them and then it will be locked in by this agreement. It is a ludicrous situation which will be very bad for Queensland.
I recommend to honourable members that they consider the points which have been put forward. This clause should be dropped from the agreement to the benefit not only of Queensland but also of other areas. If honourable 'members opposite are really concerned about free enterprise they will move in this area and stop the squashing of free enterprise. At present they are only boosting big monopolies, be they private or public enterprise, to the detriment of small airlines. It is up to honourable members opposite to show where they stand and support their colleagues and my neighbours from Queensland.
Mr KATTER (Kennedy) (9.45)-1 am particularly concerned with clause 12. The honourable member for Maranoa (Mf Ian Call1eron) and I are concerned about those people who sustain industries that bring many billions of dollars into this country. Particularly vital to them is just how often an aircraft flies into their remote areas, the sort of aircraft that flies in and what they will be charged for those services. The difficulty with clause 12 is that if an airline is not showing a profit over a period of six months it can withdraw from the service after a further period of three months. We just did not sit back and accept this. There has been a series of long discussions-- .
Mr Hunt-We have had meetings. Mr KATTER-We have had meetings. On Monday the Minister for Transport (Mr Hunt) convened a meeting which was attended by Frank Ball, the Australian General Manager of Trans-Australia Airlines, Frank Pascoe, one of the senior officials of Ansett Airlines of Australia, the honourable member for Maranoa, my~elf, and a few others. The crux of that discussion was our understanding that Ansett and TAA have a unique privilege. They have the sole rights to run on the major routes in Australia. I well remember it being said some time ago by the then Minister for Civil Aviation that if those airlines were to continue to have that protection and that sort of privilege they were expected to serve the rural air routes. The Holcroft report has been adopted and we have to try to make the best of it. As a result of very lengthy discussions on Monday the General Manager of TAA issued the following statement which I very much want to go in the record:

Airlines Agreement Bill

3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3031

1. TAA will honour its undertaking with the Queensland Government to maintain F27s now operating until April 1982 or in accordance with the provisions of the new two-airline agreement, whichever is the later.
The second point he made was in the following terms:
Before that time TAA will: (a) Negotiate with Bush Pilots and other operators to conduct commuter operations over routes which TAA can no longer economically operate in accordance with IAFC guidelines; (b) Assess the viability of the establishment-
This is a very important point which came as a result of some pretty hot argument on Monday afternoon-
of regional centres serviced by commuters radiating from those regional centres with F27 services operated by TAA.
Finally, he stated:
The Government will continue to assist in negotiations between TAA and other operators, including Bush Pilots and East-West, to ensure that air services in western Queensland are maintained at a satisfactory level.
The point J want to make is one on which we will have to stand firm. I do not know what muscle we will have when it comes to a final showdown. In my part of Queensland we are sustaining three industries that bring billions of dollars into this country. We did not tell God to put all the minerals 400, 500 or 600 miles away from the coast. We did not tell God to give sheep foot rot. I notice that a lot of honourable members opposite do not believe in God. They should make their own arrangements and deal with their bloke, but I will deal with my fellow. We did not ask that sheep along the coast get foot-rot. We did not ask that lousy pastures be put along the coastal areas and for us to have the great trace metal areas out in the back blocks. This is no joke, although it might be a joke to honourable members opposite. These great industries are many hundreds of miles from the coast. If we do not have damned airline services, what do we have? Whitlam came along and dragged away every subsidy we had. He kicked us in the guts and brought us to our knees out there. That is the sort of thing we had to take. He did not give a damn about anyone out there.
Dr Theophanous-Mr Chairman, I wish to raise a point of order. We are discussing the Airlines Agreement Bill, not the protection or otherwise given by a previous government to the honourable member's area.
The CHAIRMAN. Order! There is no point of order. I understand that the honourable member for Kennedy is addressing clause 12.

That is why we are concerned about clause 12. Mr DAWKINS (Fremantle) (9.53)-One can only admire the spirited and uncharacteristic display of independence on the part of the National Country Party. It is regrettable that it all comes too late and there is nothing anyone can do about the situation. If members of the National Country Party are really concerned about the airlines agreement which tonight is being ratified by this Parliament, they should have, at the very minimum, voted for the last amendment proposed. They have voted for an agreement which can go on for ever and ever. If they were concerned about any aspect of the clauses in the Schedule they would have-had they known what they were doing, presumably they would have-voted to give a limited life to this agreement. They would have voted to provide for the circumstances in which the Parliament would have had to enter into deliberately and ratify another agreement at the expiry of this current agreement in eight years time. The votes of the honourable member for Kennedy (Mr Katter) and the honourable member for Maranoa (Mr Ian Cameron) would have been enough to turn this question around, to rewrite aviation history in this country not only for people in the metropolitan areas but also for people who those honourable members purport to represent. They are nothing but phoneys or dopes

3032 REPRESENTATIVES 3 June 1981

Airlines Agreement Bill

because the whole point is that in advance of eight years time they and the rest of us would have had the opportunity to rewrite the agreement. But they have given this Government, or any government, and more particularly the airlines, an agreement with an infinite life. Unless a parliament or government at some later time takes the initiative to rewrite this agreement it will go on forever and the aspects of clause 12 with which the honourable members disagree can go on not only for their lifetime but for their children's lifetime and their children's children's lifetime. The problem with them is that they do not even understand what they are doing. The honourable member for Maranoa said he would call for a division. What on earth will he call for a division on?
Mr Ian Cameron-Mr Chairman, I have not mentioned a division.
Mr DAWKINS-The honourable member has just denied it, but earlier he said he would call for a division. There is nothing to call for a division on. We have just discussed the eight clauses of the Airlines Agreement Bill. It might be of enlighten~ ment to the honourable member for Maranoa to know that he is not free to meddle with the clauses of the Schedule; he either had to take the opportunity offered to him a moment ago to vote for giving this agreement a definite life, or do nothing. It is a great shame that his spirited display of independence was not a little more in evidence just a few moments ago. I repeat that he and the honourable member for Kennedy had the opportunity to rewrite aviation history in this country but they displayed their arrant gutlessness in not defying their Government and taking that course of action.
We are used to slick justifications from the National Country Party. A moment ago the Minister for Transport (Mr Hunt), in his explanation of the reasons we could not have an eight-year term, said we have a committee that needs five years to report. I remind people of the situation a few months ago when we had another inquiry, the Holcroft Independent Public Inquiry into Domestic Air Fares, which was supposed to have a year to report and yet all of a sudden the urgency of the matter, the requirement to prevent chaos in the airline industry, required that that inquiry should report within five months; should terminate its inquiry in order for the Government to carry out certain undertakings that it gave before the election to its powerful friends in the airline industry. We are used to these slick justifications. No more than five months was necessary before the election; now we need five years for an inquiry into whether there should be deregulation.

I wish to tell members of the National Country Party another thing. Tonight they have crunched headlong into the movement in the Liberal Party which supports small government and deregulation. They have just woken up for the first time that there are some dangers and problems involved in deregulation. If we go flying straight into deregulation some people are bound to miss out. I just ask you--
Mr Katter-Mr Chairman, I raise a point of order. The honourable member for Fremantle is directing his remarks at me. I find them completely insulting. I would not vote with him if I were dying in the middle of the Nullarbor Plain.
The CHAIRMAN. Order! The honourable member for Kennedy will resume his seat. I remind the honourable member for Fremantle to address his remarks through the Chair.
Mr DAWKINS-The honourable member is prepared to do anything for his constituents except vote in their interests. He would not move away from the Government benches for anything in the world. His constituents have to realise, once and for all, that he will not take any action which advances their interests if it means deserting the Government which he has supported in such a cherished fashion for so long. There are some problems in deregulation. I ask honourable members to contemplate this: If the National Country Party is up in arms about the horrors, the portents, of deregulation, what are the chances of this inquiry likely to be?

In Committee Clauses 1 and 2-by leave-taken together, and agreed to. Clause 3 (Interpretation). Mrs KELLY (Canberra) (10.17)-1 wish to speak in opposition to clause 3 sub-clauses (5) and (6) on behalf of the Opposition. These subclauses illustrate that this whole package of legislation is framed on the basis of backing up and supporting Ansett Airlines of Australia. In faCt, one could be forgiven for thinking Ansett's own legal people had drafted this legislation. That is so clearly illustrated in these two special sub-clauses. I will go into them in some detail. They highlight the totally hypocritical approach of this Government. On the one hand the Government parties say that they are the parties of free enterprise and that they like market forces to operate. Yet when it comes to airline policy the situation is totally different. These sub-clauses act as a deterrent to East-West Airlines Ltd, in particular, and any third airline operating fairly in the market place. I will highlight that claim in my discussion on these two sub-clauses. First of all, I think the existing fare structure operating in Australia is to the detriment of consumers in Australia, that is, the users of air services in Australia. They have not been taken into consideration at all in the drafting of this legislation. The situation for my constituents in Canberra is one example. Recently we had an increase of 37 per cent in the fare for the leg between Canberra and Sydney. That is absolutely appalling when one considers that we have a degree of isolation in Canberra. Government members interjectingMrs KELLY-How do you get here if you do not come in on aeroplanes? I do not see you driving, walking or hitchhiking. This rapid increase in air fares-37 per cent for my constituentsin Canberra-has had a very detrimental effect on the tourist industry. It will continue to do so. The Minister for the Capital Territory (Mr Hodgman) nods his head. What is he prepared to do? Will he vote with me on this matter? This legislation should give a third airline the opportunity of providing for my constituents in the Australian Capital Territory a cheaper air fare. That is why I am opposing these twosub-c1auses today.

Independent Air Fares Committee Bill

3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3035

Twill illustrate a point to honourable members. When the air fare increase was announced a further airline put a submission to the Department of Transport outlining a proposition to provide an air fare cheaper by $10 each way than the existing Ansett and Trans-Australia Airlines fare. That was the proposal. The operators claim they can do that with the F27 and a one class service. The legislation before us today will work to inhibit their doing that and providing this fairer system for my constituents in the Australian Capital Territory and others like them throughout
Australia.
I want to confine my comments specifically to sub-clauses (5) and (6) of clause 3. The effect of clause 3 (5) is that any air fare proposed by an operator other than Trans-Australia Airlines and Ansett Airlines of Australia for a trunk route shall not be an economy fare or first class fare for the purpose of the Bill. The consequence is that no operator other than TAA or Ansett has a right to have its trunk route fares reviewed under clause 15, the major air fare review clause, or clause 16, the minor air fare review clause. Such operators can have their trunk route fares reviewed only if the Committee of its own motion decides to conduct a review under clause 15 (2). The opportunity for a minor air fare review is removed. This follows from the definition in clause 3 (5) of economy fare and the fact that clause 15 and clause 16 refer only to economy fares. Under clause 3 (6) any trunk route fare of an operator other than TAA or Ansett which is not the same as the TAA or Ansett fare is treated as a discount fare. The consequence is that unless the other operator charges the same fares as TAA or Ansett approved fares the operator then must satisfy the Committee. Such an operator has to prove, firstly, that the proposed fare is likely to increase the operator's profitability; secondly, that the proposed fare is unlikely to result in the economy fares of any operator over trunk routes being increased; and thirdly, that the conditions are reasonable and not discriminatory between persons who comply with the conditions in clause 17 (4).
Although on the face of it these criteria may seem reasonable, legal advice which I have had, and which I know has been available to other of the minor operators in Australia, is to the effect that it would be unreasonably difficult for an operator to satisfy the Committee on the first two requirements. The requirements of clause 17 (4) are unduly onerous in their application to discount fares. They are even more unreasonable and unnecessary for regional airline trunk route fares. All trunk route fares should be assessed on the same basis as TAA and Ansett fares under clause

15 (ll) and clause 16 (8). These clauses provide TAA and Ansett with more than adequate protection.

This Bill can be amended easily by the deletion of clause 3 (5) and clause 3 (6). I wish that more National Country Party members were in the chamber to hear this because they are the people who should be voting with the Opposition on this amendment. This amendment will have the effect
that the trunk route fares of regional operators such as East-West Airlines and Bush Pilot Airways are considered on a proper basis. All that the Opposition is asking for is that such operators get a fair go. If this Government really believes in supporting private enterprise and free market forces it should give those operators a chance.

Mr MORRIS (Shortland) (10.24)-Mr Chairman, it is the intent of the Opposition to oppose the inclusion of sub-clauses (5) and (6) of clause
3. I have just been discussing the matter with the Clerk and it appears that because of the haste with which we are considering these amendments
and because of the pressure since last evening we have not prepared written notice of that intention. Could notice be prepared to enabk that process to be followed?

The CHAIRMAN. If the honourable member so wishes he can move to omit sub-clauses (5) and (6) from clause 3.

Mr MORRIS-I move:

Clause 3, page 4, omit sub-clauses (5) and (6).

Clause 4 (Constitution of Committee).

Mr MORRIS (Shortland) (10.32)-Clause 4 prescribes the constitution of the Independent Air Fares Committee. It is to be made up of three members. Amendment No.1 standing in my name reads:

Clause 4, page 5, after sub-clause (2) insert the following sub-clause:
'(2A) At least one member shall be a person who is a member or officer of a body having functions in relation to consumer protection. '

The purpose of the amendment is to ensure that there is direct consumer representation upon the Committee so that there is a direct link from some 11 million persons who use domestic passenger air services each year to the deliberations of the Committee and there is a channel and means by which the problems experienced by members of the public in the utilisation of air services and the views as to the adequacy or appropriateness of the types of fares offered to the community can be processed

through to the Committee. At the same time, having direct consumer representation on the Committee would ensure that there is a link both ways-between the Committee's activities and the public and then between the public in its expression of views and the Committee. Not wishing to delay the chamber at this stage, for those reasons I move:
Clause 4, page 5, after sub-clause (2) insert the following sub-clause:
'(2A) At least one member shall be a person who is a member or officer of a body having functions in relation to consumer protection.'
Mr HUNT (Gwydir-Minister for Transport) (10.33)-The Government notes the view of the Opposition in regard to this matter. The Government intends to leave the clause in its present form. I assure the chamber that we will obtain the best people possible whatever the qualification. There is not the slightest doubt that the quality of the people who are chosen for this task is very important to the functions of the Independent Air Fares Committee. The Committee has a very important task in ensuring that it sets the fares according to the guidelines that have been adopted which are consistent with the Holcroft Independent Public Inquiry into Domestic Air Fares recommendations of setting a nationally consistent cost related fare. Every effort will be made to ensure that we will obtain really top people who will take into account the consumer requirements.
Amendment negatived.
Clause agreed to.

There is really no need for the amendment because obviously the Committee will have to establish a satisfactory base on which to do its future work.

For the information of honourable members, subsequent amendments Nos 7 to 13 relate to the

Independent Air Fares Committee Bill

3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3039

matter contained in the amendment I have just moved. The effect of the amendment is to ensure that major fare reviews be conducted by way of public hearing. Under the provisions of the Bill, cost allocation reviews will be conducted in public, with the Independent Air Fares Committee having a discretion to determine which part of the proceedings ought to be conducted in private and to determine which evidence should be released, with a disclaimer that evidence, or material may be submitted or submissions made that would breach trade secrets or be of a commercially confidential nature. That is a matter to be tested later in the operation of the Committee, and here in the Parliament, I hope.
The purpose of the amendment I have moved is to ensure that the major fare reviews be conducted publicly. One of the very contentious points of the whole two-airline policy was brought out very clearly by Holcroft in his report, and it is a point that I have made over and over to the Parliament in the last three or four years. Where we have a situation in which two major operators have exclusive access to the provision of a public transport service, and where they operate under legislative protection on a cost-plus basis that ensures they have to be profitable, and under legislation, as in the Schedule to the Airlines Agreement Bill, which prescribes that there be only two operators over the entire trunk route network, it is a nonsense to suggest that they suffer from a risk of disclosure of information. Let me record in Hansard an extract from page 175 of the Holcroft report. Paragraph 10.23 states:
The Inquiry has observed a number of features of the administration of air fare pricing which it believes should be altered as quickly as possible. By far the most important of these is the lack of public disclosure of financial and performance data by the airlines.
The matter of public disclosure is, in the Inquiry's view, of overriding importance in the regulation of air fare pricing. A moment's reflection will recognise the irrationality of creating a protected joint monopoly, with a cost plus pricing system, and then affording the members of that joint monopoly secrecy in relation to costing and pricing.

of such a stance, apparently without asserting its right to be properly informed as surrogate for the pUblic.

I could not put it in better words than it has been put in the Holcroft report. For this reason we believe it is the responsibility of the two airlines to provide information to the Committee in the conduct of major fare reviews, so that those two airlines give full public accountability to the public
and to the Parliament that gives them legislative protection in the conduct of air fares. For those reasons we believe the major fare reviews should be conducted in public. The effect of my amendment is to bring about that result. I commend the amendment.

MI' HUNT (Gwydir-Minister for Transport) (10.53)---The Government does not agree with this amendment. To have a public inquiry every time there was a major fare review would result in
extended inquiries. It is very necessary to have a public inquiry for the cost allocation review to set the formula. Once the formula has been set it is
necessary for the Committee then to adjust all future applications for fare increases according to that formula. Needless to say, there would be very lengthy delays in reponses to applications if there
were to be a public hearing every time there was an application for a fare increase. When one takes account of the scope of responsibility-it covers not only the trunk operators, but also the regional operators and the commuter operators-the Committee would hold, on average, one inquiry each working day. An enormous bureaucracy would be needed to handle such a work load. We do not believe it is necessary.

That the amendment (Mr Morris's) be agreed to.

Mr MORRIS (Shortland) (11.0)-1 move:

(5) Clause 15, page 14, after paragraph (11) (a) insert the following paragraph:
'(aa) the need to ensure that the profit of Ansett and the Commission, before interest and tax, excluding extraordinary items, as a proportion of revenue does not exceed 6 per cent, given a continuation of the present levels of interest and income taxation in Australia;'.

Clause 15 deals with the conduct of a major airfare review. Sub-clause (11) deals with the matters to which that the Independent Air Fares Committee shall have regard in determining the amount of economy air fares. Paragraph (e) states:

the need to ensure that air services are provided over trunk routes by 2 economically viable passenger operators:

Whatever that means I do not know. The meaning is in the mind of the utterer. Paragraph (d) prescribes:

the need to ensure that air services are operated on an efficient and economic basis.
Paragraph (g) prescribes:
the need to ensure that air services that are provided over trunk routes are operated as far as possible on a competitive basis.
That paragraph makes a nonsense of the previous one to which I have referred. If the fares are to be the same, if the matters to be taken into consideration are the same, and if there is a vagueness about establishing that air services are to be operated on an efficient and economic basis, then the only real competition can be the colour of the curtains over the galley of the aircraft or the colour of the uniforms of the hostesses on board.
We are proposing that, in addition to those vague items, there be included a 'precise measurement of profit objective for the two airlines concerned. The amendment that 1 have proposed again is taken directly from the Holcroft committee report. I must point out that the members of that committee are men with a very wide experience in the commercial world, with a wide experience of the type of return that is available to the various industries within Australia, whether in investment, manufacturing or transport. Mr Holcroft is from the transport field, Dr Bell is from the investment field and Professor Hogan is an economist from Sydney University. I will not go into the report and the detail of the matters they considered, the kind of ratios they compared and the comparisons that they made of the different measures used to establish what is a satisfactory return up on funds employed upon capital or upon revenue, but those three gentlemen came to the conclusion that, in the light of all the evidence available, the most appropriate measure would be to write into future legislation a requirement relating to profit before interest and tax. Paragraph 16.82 sub-paragraph (3) of the Holcroft report states:
Profit before interest and tax, excluding extraordinary items, should as a proportion of revenue not exceed 6.0 per cent, given a continuation of present levels of interest rates and income taxation in Australia.
Sub-paragraph (4) states:
The rate of return and the rate of profit before interest and tax should be reviewed whenever there is a significant shift in the criteria by which they were previonsly established.
I will not take the time of the Committee going through the detail of the background to that recommendation. I have read the recommendation and I am satisfied that it is an appropriate recommendation. I leave it to honourable members to refer to the report.
Before 1sit down I wish to repeat the point that I have made before. When two operators are

Independent Air Fares Committee Bill

3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3041

given a protected and privileged position in the provision of a public transport service-a guaranteed profit through a cost plus system-in the public interest, that arrangement must carry with it a heavy responsibility for public accountability. Neither of the major operators is exposed to the degree of risk that exists in other commercial ventures in the economy. They are protected. There can be only two operators; there cannot be a third operator in the field. They are not entitled to the kind of return that could be justified in a venture or a commercial operation with a higher risk component. Airline services should be predicated upon the assessment that they are part of the public transport service. For those reasons the Opposition moves the amendment which I have outlined to the chamber. 1 commend that amendment to honourable members.
Mr BEAZLEY (Swan) (11.7)-1 want to reinforce some of the remarks made by the Opposition spokesman for transport, the honourable member for Shortland (Mr Morris) and point out that in fact the Holcroft committee considered the question of profit at very considerable length. In fact it saw it as fundamental to its whole examination of the air fare structure. The figure of 6 per cent was arrived at after extremely detailed consideration. A second feature upon which it is worth commenting and upon which the Opposition spokesman did not comment is that because of the experience of the Holcroft committee and the general tenor in which the negotiations for the two-airline agreement were conducted, it is possible that we will have some trouble ensuring full disclosure of the accounting procedures of the two airlines that will be party to this agreement. I refer not so much Trans-Australia Airlines, which was relatively blameless in its performance before the Holcroft committee, but to the other airline.
The point I wish to make about this 6 per cent profit margin is that as well as being appropriately calculated by persons capable of making such a calculation and as well as being right, given the regulated nature of the airlines and ensuring that there is no exploitation of the travelling public, it would also have the very distinct advantage of concentrating the minds of the accountants of the two airlines wonderfully and ensuring that the figures which they come up with are accurate and the sorts of figures that we could subject to some form of public scrutiny. Thus [ hope, we arrive at a situation in which the airline travelling public is not exploited to the extent to which it has been to this point.
Mr HUNT (Gwydir-Minister for Transport) (lLlO)-In brief, the Government does not accept this amendment. The airlines have made

an $800m investment in new generation widebodied aircraft, which will be of tremendous benefit to the travelling public generally. They are a more efficient and less noisy aircraft, and will help to stabilise the fares of passengers because of their efficiency, so long as we can maintain a sufficiently high utilisation level. Work done indicates that if the 6 per cent figure were established there is no way that either airline could operate at a profit. [n fact, they would operate at a loss. Of course, Holcroft made the point that the figure could be varied, so the question of the making of a profit will be a matter for the Independent Air Fares Committee to decide, having regard to its guidelines. The figure mentioned in the Holcroft report certainly was not one of the soundest aspects of that report.
