AUSTRALIAN NATIONAL AIRLINES REPEAL BILL 1981
Second Reading Debate resumed from 28 May, on motion by MrHunt:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr MORRIS (Shortland) (11.17)-The Australian National Airlines Repeal Bill deals with the conversion of Trans-Australia Airlines from a statutory authority to a public company. Obviously it is the first step by the Fraser Government in the sale of all or part of the airline. When the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) stated as reported in the Melbourne Herald on 3 April, that shares would not be sold to Ansett Airlines of Australia, he clearly did not state to whom shares would be sold. If it was proposed that the shares were not to be sold then he would not have said they would not be sold to Ansett; he would have said they would not be sold to anybody. The Deputy Prime Minister and Minister for Trade and Resources (Mr Anthony) agrees with the view of the Australian Labor Party and the majority of Australians that the sale of TAA would lead inevitably to the formation of a private monopoly in major domestic airline services, higher air fares for travellers and the abolition of country air services. We heard from two Queensland members of the National Country Party earlier this evening, the honourable member for Kennedy (Mf Katter) and the honourable member

for Maranoa (Mr Ian Cameron), of their concern about the possible abolition of country air services in your State, Mr Deputy Speaker.
Let us look at the history of Trans-Australia Airlines. It was established at a time when aviation was in its infancy. There was a very firm belief in this country, held by the then Leader of the Opposition, Mr Menzies, and also by the United Kingdom Government, that the provision of transport services should be carried out by a public utility. It was the decision of the government of the day in 1946 that the most effective way to provide a national system of safe, regular airline services was with one major airline. The total investment in that airline, Trans-Australia Airlines, was some $14.8m in 1946. Not one dollar has been put into that airline since. It has developed and grown upon the basis of the service provided, on the reserves it has developed and on the profits it has made. We know that the two-airline policy devised by the Menzies Government in 1952 was aimed at protecting Australian National Airways-later Ansett Airlines of Australiafrom the growth of TAA. In other words, it was devised to protect the private enterprise operator from the rigours of competition from another operator-as I said earlier, that is the very antithesis of what private and free enterprise is supposed to be all about.
Despite the handicaps and the obstacles of the two-airline policy and despite the impediments and obstructions placed in the way of Trans-Australia Airlines by successive conservative governments, Trans-Australia Airlines went on to become a leader in market innovation, to gain the support of the majority of air travellers and to hold that support year after year, establishing itself as a national symbol of endeavour, of spirit and of identity as a successful public enterprise, as a successful Australian enterprise and as a symbol of achievement by the Australian people. It has been supported by generations of Australians and generations of Australians have contributed to the development of that airline. Since 1946 the airline has carried some 71 million passengers, it has paid $35m in dividends to Consolidated Revenue, it has paid government charges and taxes of $423m and it has established a national route network of 63,583 unduplicated kilometres servicing some 53 towns and cities.
Let me put to rest the claim often made by opponents of TAA because they see the airline as a danger, that TAA does not pay the same taxes. Let me state, and the Minister will confirm this, I am sure, that TAA pays the same taxes and government charges as any public company. It is interesting to note the value put on TAA by those

I would like to quote what the Minister for Transport said on 18 September 1980 when introducing the Australian National Airlines Amendment Bill (No.2) 1980. I described this Bill as an act of grovelling by the Government before the proprietors of Ansett. I want to refresh the memories of honourable members opposite about what the Minister said. He stated:
Paramount amongst these changes was the need to place TAA on a more commercial basis. Since the Government aims to create a more competitive environment for the two airlines, it is amending the ANA Act to ensure that TAA operates on a similar basis-
I underline those words-
to a privately owned airline. At the same time the Government is anxious to ensure that the ANA Commission as a statutory authority is not advantaged over its competitors. This approach is consistent with the Government's overall policy that publicly owned business undertakings should, as far as possible, be neither advantaged nor disadvantaged as compared with their competitors from the private sector.
Is the Minister to tell us this evening that that was a falsehood, that what he told us on 18 September last was not true, or substantially was not true? The challenge is there for the Minister. Let me compare those words of 18 September with the

Minister's words on 25 May. The Minister in his second reading speech on the Australian National Airlines Repeal Bill 1981 stated:
I now acknowledge the contribution of the Australian National Airlines Commission to the development of aviation since its establishment in 1946. TAA is an airline with a great reputation. This is due to its management and staff, the people who have made and are making it a great airline.
That is a pretty paltry kind of epitaph when the Government wants to send an airline down the drain. I thought that the airline would have deserved something better than four lines. The Minister went on to say:
-I firmly believe that by making it a public company and freeing it of many bureaucratic controls it will go from strength to strength.
Which version is correct? Is it the first speech or the second speech? I believe the first one is correct. I have looked at those amendments. The Minister was acting on the basis of fact. He had information and he spelt out in the Bill the proposed amendments, the purpose of the amendments and the reasons they were being made. The second is simply a propaganda document brought up to quieten those poor fellows from Western Australia who have been conned about the deregulation inquiry and who have now been further conned by the words 'We will make TAA into a public company. That should satisfy them. We will have a look at selling it'. The task that faces the Minister is to detail specifically the many bureaucratic controls that were not evident to him or his Department when the legislation of 1980 was submitted to the House.
The major issue at stake in this whole exercise and in the thinking of the free enterprise fanatics opposite is the profit stripping of profitable public enterprises. The Government is concerned about selling off only profitable public enterprises, not the loss incurring public enterprises that are regarded as necessary services, such as railways, schools and hospitals or any other handouts to give as tax deductions. It is concerned with selling only those services which Australians of several generations have established. This airline has been brought to a stage of development where it can be profitable. Now that the risks have been borne by past generations and taxpayers over the last 35 years, the Government says: 'Now it is profitable let us give it to our friends, the wealthy investors and stock exchange speculators'. The Government has no mandate for that action. Some Government members may feel that they would like to do that, but let me make it crystal clear to them that they have no mandate. They have a mandate to do the opposite. They said in the election campaign and have said over and over in the last few years that TAA was not for sale. This

Minister for Transport, the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and the previous Minister for Transport are on the record as saying that TAA would not be sold.

Selling TAA shares to the public would be regarded as a provocation by any future socialist government .

It is not the policy of the Australian Labor Party to nationalise the airlines system. It is the policy of the Labor Party to ensure that TAA remains a 100 per cent government owned enterprise. It is the political reality that at the moment at least two major airlines operate in this country. I do not see any way that that number can be reduced unless the Government proceeds with the sale of TAA. That would produce a private monopoly in the provision of major air services.

MI' JULL (Bowman) (11.43)-The hour is getting late and I will confine my comments to very brief statements indeed. I listened with interest to the honourable member for Shortland (Mr Morris). I think we have to realise that this legislation fits in as part of the whole package of legislation which the Government has brought into the House tonight. I sincerely believe the package can lead to a fairer and more equitable pricing system for air fares in Australia and ultimately cheaper air fares in Australia. It would be interesting to ponder what my philosophical commitment would have been in 1946 to the establishment of Trans-Australia Airlines. Perhaps I would have found some support for it at that stage. That is 30-odd years ago and the whole of the domestic as well as the international aviation scene has changed very much indeed.

the moment it is almost impossible to find out through the access we have in our everyday operations here in Parliament what really has been going on with TAA, its superannuation scheme, its restrictions on expenditure and its relationship with the Department of Transport. All these matters are adding up.This is really one of the wisest moves that could be made for the sake of TAA, whichever way it might go in the future.
As I said the Bill is part of a total package lining domestic aviation up for the 1980s. The days of 1946 and the early 1950s are long gone. If we can look at the legislation in that respect and with the positive feeling that TAA will be owned by all the people of Australia, then we have to make sure they get value for money and that their investment is sound. This is the only way we can do it. I have every confidence that the Minister will fol-

We have to be careful to avoid the situation that has developed in a number of overseas countries. I will cite just two examples. One relates to New Zealand and its Government owned domestic airline, and the other relates to Britain where British European Airways virtually acted as the country's domestic carrier. I am scared that if we do not have a full investigation of TAA and a proper look at its financial setup we will have such a situation. I am not denying that TAA has been operating on a shoestring for many years.

Mr LEO McLEAY (Grayndler) (11.48)-[n my view the Australian National Airlines Repeal Bill is one of the most iniquitous Bills brought into this House by the present Fraser Government. In some respects it is a sale of the Australian people's birth right. As the honourable member for Bowman (Mr Jull) said, Trans-Australia Airlines was established in 1946 by the Chifley Labor Government. Since that time TAA has carried over 71 million passengers and has paid $35m in dividends to consolidated revenue. It has also paid taxes and government charges to the Commonwealth amounting to $423m. That is not bad for a company that a number of Government members are saying is on its last legs. Indeed, in the last financial year TAA showed an improvement in its passenger growth of 7.1 per cent. It carried more freight than Ansett. Australian National Airlines Repeal Bill

When looking at the Bill one should ask why the Government is to sell an enterprise that in 1979-80 made $2.9m profit for the Australian taxpayer. In looking at the evidence before us and even the Press statements made on the subject, it is quite clear why the present Liberal-National Country Party Government-even over the National Country Party's head-wishes to sell TAA or set it up in such a fashion that the Government, at some later date, can sell it off. The Minister for Transport (Mr Hunt) and a number of speakers in previous debates here this evening have said: 'But of course we do not have any real intention of selling the airline. We are just trying to set it up in such a commercial fashion that it will be competitive'. Indeed, the Minister said in his second reading speech:
The Government intends that the new company be fully commercial with the powers of the Government restricted to rights of shareholders to appoint directors.
If the facts are, as I put them to Parliament just now, that TAA is making a profit, that TAA is getting more traffic than it had in the past, that TAA is doing better than Ansett, why does the Government need to set it up in such a different fashion? I put it to the Parliament and to the Australian people that the reason is that in the long run this Government intends to try to sell TAA despite the fact that many of its own supporters say that it should not do it. Indeed, one of the Government's most influential members, the Deputy Prime Minister and Leader of the National Country Party (Mr Anthony), said at Echuca at the Country Party's Victorian branch conference that the Government must run TAA. It is very interesting that since 10 April 1981 he has not said that it must not sell it. One might ask: Why is the Government wanting to sell TAA? Why is it setting it up in such a fashion? It is quite obvious that it is because Mr Murdoch and his friends wish to buy the airline and wish to try to dominate the airline industry in Australia. A number of Government members are obviously in league with this plan-the gang of seven that voted against the Government this evening, the deregulators who wish to see the airline industry in Australia destroyed so that there can be control of the industry by Mr Murdoch and Sir Peter Abeles. That would be to the great detriment of the people of Australia. Indeed, the people who will be the great losers will be the people of Queensland and the outlying areas of some of the States.

interests which support it and then concocts so called philosophical arguments later. This Bill and the previous Bill, which are in blatant contradiction of each other, clearly expose the so-called philosophy as a sham..
Why is TAA to be made into a so-called public company? Why is the Australian National Airlines Commission to be disbanded? The only thing that even resembles an argument for this action is that TAA will be subject to fewer restrictions from the Government if it becomes a so-called public company. Yet, if it is necessary to remove such restrictions, this can be done by amending the regulations governing the Commission. It is not necessary to establish this public company for that purpose. If anyone denies this, let him refer to Hansard of 18 September of last year, when this same Government introduced legislation to achieve this goal of deregulating TAA while retaining it as a commission.
Last year the Government assured this House that TAA could be relieved of government controls while retaining its commission status. Why has the Government now changed that view? The reason will become evident to all Australians in the next few months. This Government is converting TAA to a public company beca~se that way it is easier to move to a situation of selling off its shares to private individuals and private companies. Thus one of the few remaining profitable public enterprises in this country is to become the private property of wealthy Australians and overseas interests. Taxpayers' investments in this company over many years are to be dispensed with at the whim of the Minister. What is serious here is that the Government is being dishonest in the way it is going about achieving the goal. The Minister informed us that the Government has decided to engage consultants to examine the practicality of selling some or all of the shares in TAA, including how this might be done in the light of the Government's domestic aviation policies. So we are to have private sector consultants to analyse and determine whether TAA is to remain publicly owned. That is like asking Mephistopheles to determine whether souls will go to heaven. The Government fools no one with this delaying tactic. Trans-Australia Airlines is as good as gone.
I address myself to a further point. The general philosophy of getting rid of productive and profitable public enterprises is an absurd economic thesis. It is a thesis which, if carried to its extremes, results in higher taxation because more public revenue has to be found. In the last few

years TAA has in fact contributed $35m in dividends to Consolidated Revenue. It has also contributed $423m in government charges and taxes.

KOONGARRA PROJECT AREA BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 28 May, on motion by MrWilsol1:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Ml' WEST (Cunningham) (12.16 a.m.)-The purpose of this Bill is once again to confuse the Northern Territory Aboriginals, by allowing uranium mining at Koongarra to proceed in the Kakadu National Park which has already been submitted for inclusion in the World Heritage List. This Bill amends the proclamation of the Governor-General of 5 April 1979 which was made under section 7 (2) of the National Parks and Wildlife Conservation Act 1975 by which an area was excluded from the Kakadu National Park far the Kaangarra mining lease area. Ranger and Jabiluka, the two other major uranium mines, which border the Kakadu National Park, have Australia's largest uranium reserves. Ranger, if it goes ahead, would produce an estimated 110,000 tonnes of yellowcake, and Jabiluka about 206,000 tonnes of yellowcake. Mining at the Ranger proposal would be open cut with an annual production initially planned of 3,000 tonnes of yellowcake with a capacity to increase to 6,000 tonnes. It currently employs 1,560 workers. The permanent jobs are estimated to number only between 250 and 400. Jabiluka plans to use the underground mining technique, with an annual production of 3,000 tonnes of yellowcake. Permanent jobs are expected to number about 880.
No other deposits approach the vastness of these reserves. The fact that these mines border the national park poses grave threats to the environmental safety of the park. Despite these threats at least one can say that these mines only border the Kakadu Park. Koongarra, on the other hand, with only 13,300 tonnes of mineable yellowcake, is geographically situated in the middle of the park. The open cut method for the No. 1 orebody is expected to be used in a rapid short term program lasting 18 months to two years. This is a considerable change from the original proposal which expected total production of Koongarra yellowcake to be about 28,000 tonnes. The original estimates aimed to produce 1,500 tonnes of yellowcake annually, but this estimate has now been dropped to 1,000 tonnes annually. Similarly, employment was expected to number

150 permanent jobs, but this has dropped to only 100 permanent jobs.
The whole history of this deposit reveals shonky wheeling and dealing. The wholly owned Canadian company, Noranda, originally leased the deposits in 1970. When the Kakadu Park was declared in April 1979 the deposits were excised from the park so that it actually became an enclave within the park. Just before the October election last year it was announced in this Parliament that Noranda had sold its interest in Koongarra to another wholly owned Canadian company, Denison. The timing of the announcement was deliberate, to avoid the inevitable uproar and debate about Australian resources being swapped around amongst foreign companies without Australian participation. Denison is still 100 per cent foreign owned.
What is happening with the Government's 80called foreign investment and Australian equity policy? The machinations have not stopped there. This Bill represents the worst of these manipulative and deceitful dealings and amounts to a betrayal of the Australian public. Honourable members may remember that the National Parks and Wildlife Conservation Act, as amended in 1978, provided for the declaration of the Kakadu National Park and for leasing of Aboriginal land, granted to traditional owners under the provision of the Aboriginal Land Rights (Northern Territory) Act, by the Director of the Australian National Parks and Wildlife Service to become the Kakadu National Park.
The disgraceful exclusion of certain areas from the park, including Ranger and Koongarra, for uranium mining purposes then occurred. Of course, at that time amendments to the Aboriginal Land Rights (Northern Territory) Act provided for the leasing of former unalienated Crown land then held in trust for the traditional ownersby the Kakadu Aboriginal Land Trust to the Director of the Australian National Parks and Wildlife Service. By November 1978 these legislative actions had resulted in the traditional owners leasing their newly acquired land to the Australian National Parks and Wildlife Service minus, of course, the Ranger and Koongarra uranium bearing areas.
Then we saw a succession of events. We saw stage one of the Kakadu National Park proclaimed. In April 1980 we saw the draft management plan for the Kakadu National Park released for public comment. By August last year the final management plan was tabled in this Parliament. Only in April of this year the plan of management came into effect. It required 20 days

PART IV-MINING INTERESTS AND MINING OPERATIONS
40. (I) A mining interest in respect of Aboriginal land shall not be granted unless~
(a) both the Minister and the Land Council for the area in which the land is situated have consented, in writing. to the making of the grant; or
(b) the Governor-General has, by Proclamation, declared that the national interest requires that the grant be made.
(2) Where the Minister and a Land Council, after considering proposals placed before them by an applicant for an exploration licence in respect of Aboriginal land, being proposals for the exploration for minerals on that land and the recovery of any minerals found as a result of the exploration, have; for the purposes of sub-section (I), consented to the grant of that licence, that sub-section does not apply to a later grant to that person of other mining interests in respect of that land that are in substantial accordance with those proposals.
(3) Where the holder of an exploration licence in respect of land applied, before 4 June 1976, for another mining interest in respect of that land, sub-section (1) does not apply to the grant of that other interest.
(4) Where, before 4 June 1976, a person was issued with a permit in respect of land under the Petroleum (Prospecting and Mining) Ordinance 1954 of the Northern Territory, as amended from time to time, sub-section (1) does not apply to the grant to that person of a lease under that Ordinance in respect of that land.
(5) Sub-section (I) does not apply to the grant to a person of a lease in respect of Aboriginal land under the Petroleum (Prospecting and Mining) Ordinance 1954 of the Northern Territory, as amended from time to time, if that person applied for the grant of such a lease in respect of that land before the land became Aboriginal land.
(6) If the land, or part of the land, described in Schedule 2, being the land known as the Ranger Project Area, becomes Aboriginal land, sub-section (1) does not apply in relation to that land, or that part of the land.
(7) Sub-section (I) does not apply in relation to the land described in Schedule 3, being the land known as the Eastern Areas on Groote Eylandt.
41. (I) The Atomic Energy Act 1953 or any other Act authorizing mining for minerals does not apply in relation to land that is Aboriginal land so as to authorize the entry or remaining of a person on the land or the doing of any act by a person on the land unless-
(a) the Governor-General has, by Proclamation, declared that both the Minister and the Land Council for the area in which the land is situated have consented to the application of that Act in relation to entry on that land; or

(b) the Governor-General has, by Proclamation, declared that the national interest requires the application of that Act in relation to entry on that land.
(2) If the land, or part of the land, deScribed in Schedule 2, being the land known as the Ranger Project Area, becomes Aboriginal land, sub-section (1) does not apply in relation to that land, or that part of the land.
Mr WEST-1 thank the House. Honourable members will understand that sections 40 and 41 of the Aboriginal Land Rights (Northern Territory) Act are the Aboriginal right of veto sections which require the consent of Aboriginal owners to all mining except for land for which petroleum leases were issued before the land became Aboriginal land, the Ranger uranium mining project area and the eastern area on Groote Eylandt. Of course, Koongarra is not included in any of these areas where the veto does not apply. So the Aboriginals have the power of veto anyway, as I have explained. There was no need for the Government to put into this Bill clause 2 sub-clause (2). It is simply a means of trying to persuade the Aboriginals that the Government is giving them something. But I am sure that they will not fall for it. It is history that the Aboriginals were not consulted until after the decision had been made; neither was the National Parks and Wildlife Service consulted.
I wish now to refer to a statement issued by the traditional owners of the Koongarra project on 2 or 3 May this year. To save me from reading out all the statement. I seek the leave of the House to incorporate this statement in Hansard.

The document read as follows---
TRADITIONAL OWNERS OF KOONGARRA PROJECT: PRESS RELEASE
On Friday I May, a large meeting of traditional owners and the Gagudgu Association Incorporated was held at Patonga in the Kakadu National Park. The meeting was held in response to a letter sent by the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, Senator Baume, to the Northern Land Council which advised that the Federal Government had decided to excise a further area from the Kakadu National Park in response from Denison (Australia) Pty Ltd. Denison for some time had been negotiating with the traditional owners of the area through the NLC for the right to mine Koongarra 20km south of the uranium mine at Jabiru. Denison declared that the existing mining lease was unacceptable for their purposes. Denison asked the Federal Government to excise a further area from Kakadu, thereby excluding any control over their mining lease by the ANPWS which presently manages Kakadu Park under a lease from the traditional owners.
On 28 April, the NLC received a letter from the Minister which advised that the Federal Government would implement Denison's request.
At no time were the traditional owners of the area, the Gagudja Association (the Aboriginal owners of the entire area of the Park) nor the NLC invited to give an opinion about the application of the new lease, although the NLC was aware that a submission was to be made to the Federal Government.

Koongarra Project Area Bill

3 and 4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3055

Indeed for the owners of the area the very first they heard of the lease question was the Denison application had been already implemented.
The day after receipt of the lctter by the NLC a meeting was held at Patonga to discuss the issue. The meeting lasted two days and was attended by the traditional owners of the mine site, representativcs of the Gagudja association, members of the NLC negotiating team and representatives of the NLC.
The traditional owners after considering various options decided to ask the NLC to write to the Minister and declare their opposition to the granting of the new lease. They demanded that the Minister revoke his decision until such time as the traditional owners had been fully consulted regarding the matter and until they had had the opportunity of having advice from their own advisors as to possible effects the excision would have on the management of the Kakadu Park.
They also resolved to immediately suspend all negotiations until further notice with Denison in respect of the Koongarra project.
They further demanded that Denison cease having any contact with the Aboriginal people of the area.
The traditional owners expressed their absolute disappointment with the Minister regardless of the rhetoric about consultation by himself, his office and those who went before them could again make decisions about what would happen about Aboriginal land.
As the traditional owners asked "How can it be that the Minister can give our land away to a mining company without asking what we want". This is not self determination.
This is the same as always.
White fellahs making their own decisions about what is good for Aborigines.

In short, T think the Aboriginal traditional owners will and should remember Noonkanbah.
The Opposition also opposes this legislation because of the obvious precedent it sets and the poor example it gives to future Federal and present State governments. What a great example to give to Mr Bjelke-Petersen in Queensland who now threatens Moreton Island with sand mining and the Great Barrier Reef with oil drilling! The Queensland Premier knows this Government will not stand up to him. That is why he has refused for so long even to name his Ministers-although he may have just done that-to attend the time wasting ministerial council meetings on the Great Barrier Reef so he can prevent any more of the reef being declared a marine park. What a wonderful bonus this Bill is to the enemies of national parks in the Northern Territory and Queensland. We do not tamely accept the Minister's statement that the new area will be less sensitive to damage; not at all. Concerning the revised open cut mining and rapid short term program, it states:
I quote the report on the project circulated by the Denison mining company:
A number of environmental advantages are involved, such as the reduction of the permanent workforce on site, reduction of radiation doses received by pitworkers; reduction of pit dewatering time and thus reduction of quantities of waters to be stored allowing reduced evaporation pond areas and flexibility to the water management scheme.
There we have all the horrors exposed, and the bland remarks by the company as to how to reduce them. How nice it is to know that the workers' doses of radiation will be less because of the reduced time scale and that the threat of water contamination is slightly reduced for the same reason! The report entitled 'Development of the Alligator Rivers Uranium Deposit', compiled by the Australian Atomic Energy Commission in January 1980, assessed the Koongarra area as being 'of high national park value'. The report states:
Any water pollution arising [rom the operations of Koongarra would be a potential threat to the very valuable wildlife of the Woolwonga wetlands. The proposal to build the tailings dam in a natural drainage system gives further ground for concern since it means that, over the long term, as the dam wall and other protective works deteriorated the tailings would probably be subject to erosion.
Do honourable members opposite realise that tailings from uranium mining can give off radon gas,

3056 REPRESENTATIVES 3 and 4 June 1981

Koongarra Project Area Bill

which is carcinogenic, for 100,000 years? Yet the people involved expect to store this material in clay dams in a wetlands area of the Northern Territory. How long will it stay there? Where is their responsibility to future generations? We cannot take any comfort from the statement in the Minister's speech that the most important feature of the new mining plan outlined in the environmental impact statement is that it provides for a system of water management which has a no release objective. He is talking about an area with one of the highest rainfalls in the world. Similarly, his assertions that the project has undergone comprehensive analysis in line with Environment Protection (Impact of Proposals) Act requirements are meaningless. Such so called analysis has never yet prevented dangerous projects from proceeding. Neither can we take comfort from the Denison mining company's report on the water management system being based on this famous no release objective. The objective is fine; the question is whether such an objective can be achieved. In the words of that company's report, the objective is:
There will be no intentional release of waters from the mining. milling and run-off waters from the disturbed areas of the project to the environment.
The problem remains, what if there is an unintentional release of contaminated waters? The protection offered to the environment is based entirely on the supposition that there will be no accidents and no unforeseen hazards. Of course, the answer to that assertion can be seen by everyone. We need only look as far as the Three Mile Island accident in the United States. The authorities still have not been successful in cleaning that up. Again, the report states:
The water management system does not rely on the establishment of water quality standards as there will be no release to the environment.
All this means is that there are no safeguards and no environmental protection in the case of the unexpected. Theoretical models provide no guarantee for reality. Koongarra, as I have said, is in one of the highest rainfall areas in the world. What happens if contaminated waters are released? Not only is Koongarra an enclave in the national park but also it is upsteam of the Woolwonga wildlife sanctuary. Koongarra is situated in the Nourlangie Creek catchment in a great and beautiful valley upstream from the sanctuary, in the second major catchment area in the national park. Any contamination in this area threatens, to quote the Fox report of the Ranger Uranium Environmental Inquiry, 'the most important refuge and habitat for aquatic wildlife in the Northern Territory'. Justice Fox opposed mining at Koongarra on the following grounds:

We would oppose in principle any mining development upstream ofit-
That is, the Woolwonga area-
until it had been demonstrated by experience that it could take place without environmental damage.
What sort of experience has been gained? None; there is no experience at all. I turn now in my concluding remarks to the economic and market effects of what is happening in Australia. Last year the United States produced over 11,000 tonnes of uranium, one and a half times domestic annual consumption, in excess of its needs for nuclear power generation. This is indicative of the overproduction throughout the world. In 1978 the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development estimated world surpluses between 1982 and 1985 of almost 24,000 tonnes each year, and overproduction will continue until the year 2000. World demand has not increased at the rate forecast in the mid-1970s. The Australian Atomic Energy Commission predicted in 1975 that world demand would be 107,500 tonnes in 1985 and 157,500 tonnes by 1990. Yet in 1980 the Commission revised the estimate downwards by almost 65 per cent to 39,000 tonnes in 1985 and 54,000 tonnes by 1990.
The reduced project demands coupled with a crash in the price of uranium from $43 a pound in mid-1979 to $25 a pound in January 1981 have put an economic cloud over many of these projects touted to proceed in Australia during the 1980s. Already it looks as though the Mary Kathleen project will be folding due to the drop in prices. If the nine mines which may proceed in the 1980s get the go ahead-I refer to Ranger, Jabiluka, Yeelirrie, Koongarra, Beverley, Honeymoon, Lake Way, Ben Lomond and Roxby Downs-Australia could be producing at least 14,200 tonnes a year, at a conservative estimate. Yet even the AAEC expects Australia to be able to capture only 20 per cent, or between 8,000 and 9,000 tonnes a year, of the world market by 1990. What will we do with the extra 6,000 tonnes of uranium produced in Australia each year? Will we stockpile it or simply flog it on the world market at bargain basement prices with the Australian public picking up the tab to subsidise the mines if necessary? Of the multiplicity of projects proposed, which ones will be approved; which ones will proceed? No doubt the States and the Northern Territory would allow them all to proceed but the companies may not be happy to proceed without guaranteed markets.
The Northern Territory Minister, Mr Tuxworth, stated a few days ago that if Jabiluka and Koongarra did not go ahead by 1985 they would not be goers at all. If Jabiluka cannot proceed,

Koongarra Project Area Bill

3 and 4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3057

what chance will projects such as Honeymoon, Beverley, Ben Lomond, et cetera have? These three aforementioned projects, as well as Koongarra, are also environmentally damaging as well as being commercially dubious. Beverley and Honeymoon use the environmentally dangerous method of in situ leaching of uranium ore, which may contaminate the water table and affect the quality of the water of the artesian basin.
Koongarra, as I mentioned earlier, is in the Nourlangie catchment area upstream of the most important wildlife refuge and habitat for aquatic wildfowl in the Northern Territory. As I said, Justice Fox specifically recommended that it not proceed.Irrespective of the environmental hazards of the various projects, their economics make them millstones around the neck of Australia. Since they will have problems finding markets, their uranium may be sold off at cut rate prices, with the Commonwealth providing subsidies to cover the costs.
The reduction in world demand for uranium can be attributed mainly to the problems of nuclear safety and a general turn away from nuclear power. The Japanese nuclear power industry is reeling from the disclosure that this year there have been four or five major leaks from the Tsuruga power station which were not reported by the company. Several tonnes of radioactive water leaked into the sea and caused abnormally high radioactivity in the seabed. Measurement of this heightened level of radioactivity led to the discovery of the spill and revealed the cover-up by the company. Officials believe that the ground under the plant could be contaminated and that 278 workers may have received excessive radiation. The Tsuruga reactor has been closed for six months, but its effects, like those of the more famous Three Mile Island plant, will be felt in the world's uranium market and will seriously affect the viability of new Australian projects coming on stream in the 1980s.
I conclude by pointing out that the Government is not pressing for Koongarra on economic or environmental grounds, but rather to maintain its face as agung ho 'development at all costs' government and to keep in step with like minded State governments. Justice Fox recommended against Koongarra, the Aboriginal traditional owners are opposed to it, the markets are not there, yet the Government is proposing to disregard provisions of its legislation to achieve boundary changes to allow Koongarra to proceed. This is a travesty of the parliamentary system and must be condemned. Koongarra should not proceed. The Opposition will stand by its anti-uranium

mining policy and will vote against this despicable legislation.
Mr TAMBLING (Northern Territory) (12.44 a.m.)-The Koongarra Project Area Bill is ofimmense importance to the Northern Territory. It is a progressive step for the Government to take in its uranium policies. Because the Bill relates to uranium mining it has engendered much emotional debate in the community-debate on issues that are far removed from what this Bill sets out to do. For that reason, I would like not only to place the Koongarra development in its context but also to dispel some of the more fanciful myths that have been peddled to belittle this legislation. This Bill has one objective-to alter the boundaries of the Koongarra project area. It does not give approval for mining to take place. It does not change the status of Aboriginal land. It does not supersede Commonwealth environmental legislation. I will deal with those matters later.
Exploration for uranium in the Northern Territory first commenced in 1947. The first important discovery was made in 1949 at Rum Jungle, 64 kilometres south of Darwin. This find initiated a short but intense prospecting boom that resulted in the discovery of 12 small but high grade uranium-gold deposits in the South Alligator River area 220 kilometres south east of Darwin. Treatment plants were established in both Rum Jungle and the south east Alligator Rivers region. The latter operated between 1959 and 1964 and produced 800 tonnes of uranium oxide. The Rum Jungle plant operated between 1954 and 1971 and produced 3,500 tonnes of U30 S' In 1967 the Government announced an export policy for uranium which was designed to encourage exploration for new uranium deposits. Subsequent exploration revealed significant new deposits at Nabarlek, Jabiru, Jabiluka and Koongarra. Those four sites contain over 330,000 tonnes of U30 S and there is potential for further discoveries in the region. Indeed, only last week Energy Resources of Australia raised its probable and possible reserves at its No.3 ore body at Ranger by 24 per cent, or 13,800 tonnes. To gain an idea of the importance of the region and the magnitude of these projects, suffice it to say that the total estimated capital expenditure in the uranium province on these developments is in excess of $1 ,000m.
In 1968 Noranda Australia Ltd began exploring for uranium in the Northern Territory as part of a wider program of mineral exploration it had begun several years earlier. In 1970 the company discovered in the Alligator Rivers region the uranium deposits now known as Koongarra. By mid-1971 investigations showed that the deposit

3058 REPRESENTATIVES 3 and 4 June 1981

Koongarra Project Area Bill

was a major energy resource and that development was technically feasible and economically viable. In May 1972 Noranda and other companies with exploration rights in the region joined the Commonwealth in commissioning the Alligator Rivers study-a series of investigations undertaken during 1972-73 which provided a valuable inventory of the environment of the region. In 1976 Noranda applied for a special minerallease of 12.5 square kilometres, which was considered appropriate for the then proposed project area. The following year the Minister for Environment, Housing and Community Development directed Noranda, pursuant to the administrative procedures under the Environmental Protection Act, to prepare and submit to him an environmental impact statement. This was published in December 1978. In its environmental impact statement the company presented a revised concept requiring the tailings and evaporation ponds to be located outside the lease application area. One of the reasons for this was the reservations expressed by Mr Justice Fox in the Ranger Uranium Environmental Inquiry about mining in this area.
It certainly intrigues me that opponents of the Koongarra development are obsessed with quoting Justice Fox's reservations as a means of defeating this proposal. They completely ignore that it is partially in response to Justice Fox's reservations that the proposal to alter the boundaries has come about. The proposal contained within the Bill is to alter the boundaries of an area which has already been excised from the park following the whole review process entailed in the Ranger inquiry. Therein lies the most important feature of the new mining plan. The new boundaries provide for a system of water management which has a no-release objective. Being a self-contained system, it would not affect the Nourlangie Creek catchment or the Woolwonga Wildlife Sanctuary. Such a proposal can hardly be described as 'environmental vandalism', as the honourable member for Cunningham (Mr West) would like us to believe. It should be pointed out that the revised project area is actually smaller than the area now excluded from the park. Furthermore, the area to be added to the park will include the area adjacent to the Nourlangie Rock-Mt Brockman massif. Not only is this area environmentally important, but also it has great traditional significance to the Aboriginal people of the area.
Critics of the Koongarra project are touting this proposal as a gift of Aboriginal land to a mining company. There is no question of this Government giving away Aboriginal land to mining

companies. It should be understood that, regardless of whether the project area is in or out of the national park, it remains Aboriginal land. Moreover, no lease can be granted over the area to Denison Mines Ltd until the Aboriginals have concluded an agreement with the company. It is worth stressing that this Bill contains a specjfic provision that the changes will not take place unless Aboriginal consent to the project is forthcoming. The only confusion in the whole area is that which has been induced by the honourable member for Cunningham. As I mentioned before, this Bill in no way supersedes environment legislation, nor does it bypass the foreign investment guidelines. If anyone has any doubts about this I refer him to a question answered in this House on 24 February by the Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony). He said:
. . . the object of the environment protection legislation had been met and complied with in relation to the Koongarra project . . .
On Australian ownership he said:
It will be necessary for 75 per cent equity participation.
On this matter where does the Australian Labor Party really stand? On 29 April this year the Northern Territory Opposition Leader, John Isaacs, issued an apology for a public statement on Koongarra, ostensibly opposing all forms of mining in the Kakadu National Park. It is the most wishy-washy statement that the Northern Territory Opposition Leader has come up with to date. He said:
It's the location we object to, not mining per se.
The ALP would rather write off the expected $1,OOOm capital investment in the region. That is the only conclusion I can draw from Mr Isaacs statement. He also said:
The exclusion of Kakadu from any form of mining development would have an insignificant effect on the predicted value of this boom.
He then apologised for such stupid ALP policies. He said:
. . . the economic impact of the decision not to mine would, fortunately, be less severe.
The ALP in the Northern Territory spends most of its time nowadays trying to dissociate itself from the embarrassing anti-uranium mining policies which it knows the electorate will not buy and which its federal colleagues have adopted. The Northern Territory ALP must really cherish its colleague, the honourable member for Cunningham, the alternative Minister for Environment when he says:
A Federal Labor government will repudiate any commitments made by a non-Labor government for mining, processing or export of uranium and we will demand a moratorium on uranium mining.

3 and 4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3059

The Koongarra Project Area Bill is a sensible piece of legislation and a major step forward for the uranium industry. I urge that it be passed without delay.

Mr HOLDING-It is regrettable that the honourable member for the Northern Territory (Mr Tambling) in defending this legislation has established himself primarily as an exponent of uranium mining. Not once in his speech was he concerned to deal with the environmental factors that this proposal involves. Not once did he concern himself with the rights of traditional owners. Of all the legislation that has emanated from the

Fraser Government this is the saddest and the most contemptible. It is contemptible because it contemplates a gross abuse of the legislative process. It is the saddest because it constitutes one more betrayal of Aboriginal hopes and aspirations.
This Bill contemplates the Parliament's not complying with the procedures of its own legislation at the request of the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment (Mr Wilson). The National Parks and Wildlife Conservation Act contemplates parks and reserves being established by the Governor-General by proclamation. Once proclaimed, any exclusion of any area shall not be made except by resolution passed by each House of the Parliament by a motion which must be before each House for 15 sitting days before being moved. It seems to me to be extraordinary that the Parliament in its wisdom has decided the course which must be followed by this House and the Senate in dealing with any additions or alterations to the Governor-General's proclamation in respect of the creation of a national park. It is extraordinary that from time to time the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and other Ministers say that trade unionists must comply with the law and that parliamentarians are pledged to uphold the laws made by the Parliament. Yet in this situation the very procedures which have been established by this House have not been adhered to, nor has there been any attempt to adhere to them.
This is a Bill by which the Minister asks the Parliament to deem that those procedures have been followed. What a novel concept that is. Every member of this House knows that the procedures established by the will of the Parliament have not been followed. If members of parliament are not prepared to comply with the spirit and, indeed, the letter of their own legislation, how can they command respect from the rest of the community? This legislation has all the worst defects of retrospective legislation. It has one more defect. Not only is it retrospective but also it asks the Parliament-it seems to me to come very close to being a contempt of the Parliament-to support what the Minister knows is a retrospective lie. He knows that the procedures established by the Parliament have not been followed. We all know that. So we have this device by which all the members here will say to the community at large: 'Although we know the procedures established by the Parliament for dealing with this legislation have not been followed and have been deliberately ignored what we will do is deem that they have been followed'. That is a very dangerous precedent.

3060 REPRESENTATIVES 3 and 4 June 1981

Koongarra Project Area Bill

I believe any parliament which adopts that approach on any form of legislation is bound ultimately to hold itself up to public ridicule and contempt. We cannot as parliamentarians require the average citizen to comply with the laws of the Parliament, we cannot ask the average COl'POTation, trade union, trade unionist or company director, to comply with laws which we have passed but, when it suits the Executive or the Minister, introduce a Bill and simply tear up Acts of the Parliament. What is the purpose of this? Not only does the Minister choose to legislate in this way; but also we have to ask ourselves why, when the procedures are so clearly established, does the Minister choose to legislate in this way. The answer is very simple. His purpose is simply to ram this legislation through both Houses before the political composition of the Senate changes to reflect more accurately the views of the Australian people. That in itself is a thoroughly bad principle.
The honourable member for the Northern Territory referred to the environmental impact statement produced on behalf of the Koongarra project. If he had looked at that November 1977 impact statement he would have seen at page 2-180 and page 2 208 a map which accords, in all respects, with the boundaries proposed by the Bill. That is to say, in November 1979 in respect of the submission and environmental impact statement, what was contemplated by the Koongarra people was the very outlines of the very map now before this Parliament. I find it extraordinary that in the nomination dated May 1980, which goes from this Parliament to the World Heritage List, the old boundaries are indicated on the map on page 12 of the accompanying submission. Of course, that is the old map. What an extraordinary situation! Koongarra in November 1979 used the map shown in the Bill but in the submission on behalf of the Government to get the Kakadu National Park listed in the World Heritage List we are using the map which is about to be changed if the Bill is passed. I believe that that also is a situation which needs some explanation.
J believe that the Government's actions in regard to this legislation must put in jeopardy the seri~,)Us consideration of the Australian application for the inclusion of Kakadu in the World Heritage List. It is indeed novel that while specifying the uniqueness of the environment-the habi-
tat of flora and fauna and the fact that this area contains one of the world's great prehistoric art collections-the Government produces legislation which must be unique-in order to extend
mining within that very area. Of course, if the honourable member for the Northern Territory

had been completely frank with the House he would have pointed out that the Koongarra submission concedes that if this proposal is passed three important art sites will be directly affected. They will be covered by dumped waste material. Another nine sites will be indirectly affected. It is a strange thing that the honourable member for the Northern Territory, who quoted from the environmental impact statement, misstated those very facts.
My colleague the honourable member for Reid (Mr Uren) will enlarge upon aspects of the Fox report. I will leave that matter to him. However, I will now turn to what I believe is the very serious treatment of the traditional owners. By legislative enactment this Parliament gave the traditional owners of the land their rights in fee simple. They are the owners of the land. Their rights are as enforceable in law as are the rights of the Prime Minister in respect of his property at Nareen.
Earlier this week the Parliament was told how the Minister for Communications (Mf Sinclair) was not only concerned to discuss with Mr Murdoch and the Packer interests important legislation dealing with communication but was also happy to give them the draft legislation. We were told that that discussion was continuing. Those people made suggestions to the Minister and the Minister made suggestions to them. Pieces of draft legislation were floating around. I suppose the Prime Minister would justify that action on the basis that it involved consultation with interested and affected parties. Can there be any more serious interference with the rights of an individual-particularly by a government which prides itself on preserving the rights of the individual and talks about the rights of private property-than a situation in which the rights of an owner of property are affected by legislation of this kind without any consultation? What is it that gives the Packer and Murdoch interests the right to an ongoing and very considered consultation and the capacity to affect legislation that the Parliament has not even seen? On the other hand traditional Aboriginal owners can have their rights to property affected by legislation without discussion, consultation or any involvement. I refer the House to the Press release of the traditional owners of the Koongarra project. To save time. I seek leave to have the statement incorporated in Hansard.
Leave granted.
The document read as follows-

Koongarra Project Area Bill

3 and 4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3061

PRESS RELEASE. BY TRADITIONAL OWNERS OF KOONGARRA PROJECT
On Friday 1st Maya large meeting of Traditional Owners and the Gagudgu Association Inc. was held at Patonga in the Kakadu National Park.
The meeting was held in response to a letter sent by the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs (Senator Baume) to the Northern Land Council which advised that the Federal Government had decided to excise a further area from the Kakadu Park in response to a request from Denison (Australia) Pty Ltd.
Denison have for some time been negotiating with the traditional owners of the area, through the Northern Land Council, for the right to mine uranium at Koongarra, which is 20km south of the uranium mine at Jabiru.
Denison declared that the existing mining lease was unsuitable for their proposed mine. Denison asked the Federal Government to excise a further area from the Kakadu Park, thereby excluding any control over their mining lease by the ANPWS which presently manages the Kakadu Park under a lease from the traditional Aboriginal owners.
On the 28th April, the Northern Land Council received a letter from the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs, Senator Baume which advised that the Federal Government would implement Denison's request.
At no time were the traditional owners of the area, the Kakadu Association (the Aboriginal owners of the entire area of the Park) nor the Northern Land Council invited to give an opinion about the application for the new lease, although the Northern Land Council were aware that a submission was to be made to the Federal Government.
Indeed, for the owners of the area, the very first they heard of the lease question was that Denison's application had been already implemented.
The day after receipt of the letter by the Northern Land Council, a meeting was held at Patonga to discuss the issue. The meeting lasted two days and was attended by the traditional owners of the mine site and surrounding areas, representatives of the Gagadju Association, the members of the Northern Land Council negotiating team and representatives of the Northern Land Council.
The traditional owners, after considering various options, decided to ask the Northern Land Council to write to' the Minister and declare their opposition to the granting of the new lease. They demanded that the Minister revoke his decision until such time as the traditional owners had been fully consulted regarding the matter and until they had the opportunity of having from their own advisors as to possible effects the excision would have on the management of the Kakadu Park.
They also resolved to immediately suspend all negotiations until further notice with Denison (Australia) Pty Ltd in respect of the Koongarra Project.
They further demanded that Denison cease having any contact with the Aboriginal people of the area.
The traditional owners expressed their absolute disappointment that the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs regardless of the rhetoric about consultation by himself, his officers and those who went before them could again make decisions about what would happen to Aboriginal land.
As the traditional owners ask 'How can it be that the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs can give our land away to a mining company without asking what we think' this is not self determination, this is the same as always, white fellahs making their own decisions about what is good for Aboriginals.
Further Enquiries: To be made through Gagadju Association, Mr Ross Hebblewhite. Phone 81 4228.

Mr HOLDING-I will quote from the very last sentence of the Press release. This is the voice of the traditional owners:
As the traditional owners ask 'How can it be that the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs can give our land away to a mining company without asking what we think' this is not self determination, this is the same as always, white fellas making their own decisions about what is good for Aboriginals.
In respect of the recommendations of the Fox report I wish only to make a comment about a matter which was not mentioned by the Minister in his second reading speech. It is not for this side of the House to argue the veracity of the Fox report. If we are not going to comply with those recommendations the onus is clearly upon the Government to say why the House should ignore those findings. That has not occurred. If this legislation is passed in this form and ramroded right through this Parliament and the Senate, what it does--
Mr Fisher-Don't say it again.
Mr HOLDING-I suspect that if this was happening to any of the honourable member's constituents such as the farmers he would be heard to complain. I am not going to give lip service or support-no one on this side of the House would give such support-to legislation which does not comply with the processes or the standards which the House has set itself and which interferes with the rights of owners of property. The Koongarra people are traditional Aboriginal owners. Although some members of the National Country Party might choose to treat them as second class citizens with second class rights, that is not the view of the Opposition. Of course, the passage of this legislation is designed to do one more thing. The honourable member for the Northern Territory clearly gave away the intentions of the Government. Does anybody seriously believe that the honourable member does not think that mining will proceed? That was the basis of his whole speech.
When this legislation goes through the Parliament all the pressure will be asserted on the traditional owners by the mining companies, the Department of Aboriginal Affairs and the Minister. They will say: 'You are going to have mining; like it or leave it. Although we will make public statements saying we will not do anything without your approval, the only right you really have is to approve mining'. That is the position that this Government takes. That is what this legislation is about.
The Opposition is totally opposed to this legislation because it does not comply with the processes which the House itself has established. It clearly involves a great breach of the legislative

process. We are opposed to the legislation because traditional Aboriginal legal land owners are having their rights eroded in a way which constitutes a public scandal. Finally, we are opposed to it because it is simply an attempt to pressure traditional owners to give their approval to uranium mining. I will finish on this note and quote what I believe are some words of nobility by that great Australian author, Xavier Herbert, which I believe, ought to be constantly quoted in this House when we are dealing with Aboriginal affairs. He says:
Until we give back to the blackman just a bit of the land that was his, and give it without provisos, without strings to snatch it back, without complete generosity of spirit in COIlcession for the evil we have done him-until we do that, we shall remain what we have always been so far, a people without integrity, not a nation, but a community of thieves.
Mr UREN (Reid) (1.11 a.m.)--At this early hour in the morning in the stealth with which we are putting through this important piece of legislation, it is important to consider two important aspects. First of an the Koongarra Project Area Bill deals with one of the major gems of our national heritage and a gem of world heritage. We are also dealing with a question of substance which is basic to one of the most serious problems, which, in fact, can end mankind. Uranium mining-Government members hate to be reminded of this-cannot be divorced from the interrelationship with the nuclear industry which, in due turn, is interrelated with nuclear weapons and in turn with nuclear war. What Government members always want to forget are the recommendations and findings of the Fox Ranger Uranium Environmental Inquiry report set out at page 185. The report states:
3. The nuclear power industry is unintentionally contributing to an increased risk of nuclear war. This is the most serious hazard associated with the industry.
It does not matter whatever one does; one cannot divorce the peaceful uses from the warlike uses of nuclear industry. That is one aspect. Another aspect of the nuclear power industry is that it is sick and stagnant in the northern hemisphere. Later I will show the House certain figures to prove the fact that the nuclear industry economically is stagnant and sick. Forecast growth is falling sadly. I will show the House reports of Australian atomic energy interests on that aspect. The real price of uranium oxide, in fact, is only half the price it was five years ago. These are just some of the aspects.
I want to emphasise and interrelate the serious aspects that we are now talking about in regard to a national park with the spread of nuclear weapons which the third finding and recommendation of the Fox report deals with. Of course, at

the time of the Fox inquiry's examination we knew that India had got the bomb by stealth. Now we know that Israel has got the bomb by stealth. We know that South Africa has got the bomb by stealth. We know that Pakistan is well on the way to getting the bomb by stealth. We know that during the recent conflict in the Middle East, Israel thought that Iraq was on the way to getting the bomb by stealth. Some of the major statesmen in the world believe that within a short term-a decade-anything up to 20 nations in the world could have the bomb. In these early hours, when the newspapers have been put to bed, when we are not on the air, we are discussing this legislation.
This Bill is a disgraceful and cynical piece of legishtion. Its purpose is to short-circuit the normal procedures for altering the boundaries of a national park. They are reasonable procedures, but this Government is sweeping them aside so that it can alter the boundaries of the Kakadu National Park to suit a 100 per cent foreign owned uranium mining company, Denison (Australia) Pty Ltd. The reason for the Government's manipulation are blatant and cynical. At the federal election last October nearly 52 per cent of the electorate voted for two parties-the Australian Labor Party and the Australian Democrats--which oppose the mining and export of uranium. Those parties, after 30 June, will have half the seats and therefore half the votes in the Senate. That is why this legislation is being pushed through in the stealth of the night.
Under the National Parks and Wildlife Conservation Act there has to be 60 days for public comment on proposed changes to national park boundaries. After that both Houses of Parliament have to support the change. This procedure is a reasonable safeguard to protect national parks which have been set aside for the benefit of generations to come. National parks should not be subject to short term interests and political manipulation by governments. This Government is manipulating in the interests of wealthy mining companies and foreign mining companies at that. That is why there should be 60 days for public comment and the support of both Houses of Parliament before any changes take place. The Government's action undermines those procedures. Its intention is to thwart the majority vote against uranium mining at the last election, which will be reflected in the Senate after 30 June. This Bill is an abuse of the parliamentary institution and an affront to the Australian people. It demonstrates this Government's scant regard for democracy. The Government certainly has no

Koongarra Project Area Bill

3 and 4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3063

understanding of the democratic procedures or environmental procedures.
The Government's fiddle becomes even more serious when one considers that it was this Government which nominated the Kakadu National Park to be part of the World Hertitage List. It is a national disgrace that this Government nominates the park as part of the World Heritage List and then in such a cavalier fashion brings this piece of legislation before the House. The Government's action opens up a Pandora's box for the future. Think about it. You are a Queenslander, Mr Deputy Speaker. Think about the implications of the same attitudes on the question of the Great Barrier Reef, of what can happen with the procedures that have come down under this piece of legislation. The Government has fiddled with the boundaries of national parks to suit the conveniences and expediencies of the day. It is a shameful and irresponsible precedent which poses a threat to our national parks everywhere.
The Bill highlights once again the absurdity of the Kakadu National Park as declared by this Government. The Government has left a series of mining reservations in the middle of the Kakadu National Park area out of the park. These are at Ranger, Jabiluka and Koongarra. Uranium mining is to take place at these locations. This destroys the whole concept of a national park. I stress that one cannot isolate areas in the middle of a complex and fragile ecosystem. These artificial boundaries in no way change the interrelationships of the ecological system. The uranium mines are a threat to the sensitive and beautiful wetlands which are downstream and within the national park. This is particularly true for the Koongarra project which is upstream of the Woolwonga Wildlife Sanctuary and in the South Alligator River catchment area. As the honourable member for the Northern Territory (Mr Tambling) knows, the interrelationship in those wetlands is very important because of the floods that occur, because of the Aboriginal people's relationship in having to fish in the way they do, particularly in the lagoons in the dry seasons. This is the saddest thing. This is why the second report of the Fox inquiry said that if we go ahead with mining we affect all the interrelationships which are set down in that second volume. This second volume of the Fox report has been torn up and thrown out because of the hypocrisy of this Government. The Government has by-passed and prostituted this document, which set down the guidelines if mining were to proceed.

With the Koongarra in operation as well as the Ranger project, both the major river systems of the region-the East Alligator and the South Alligator-would suffer the environmental effects of uranium mining. The Fox report clearly intended that if the mining were to proceed it should be restricted to the East Alligator catchment for the time being. It is envisaged that the South Alligator catchment be protected. The second Fox report also stated that the Woolwonga area is valuable ecologically and that we should oppose in principle any mining development upstream, at least until it has been demonstrated by experience that it can take place without environmental damage, and 1 stress the word 'experience'. I challenge Government members to tell me what experience they can point to as an example for saying that this legislation should go forward.
Again, I draw the attention of honourable members to the second Fox report, which stated that if the Government were to go ahead with uranium mining then quite clearly the development should be sequential. The report warned against the development of both areas. If development is carried out at Koongarra and at the same time at the Ranger project, two catchment areas will be affected. That shows the stupidity and blundering of this Government. The terribly sad things about the issue is that we do not know enough about this delicate environment. We were warned that we have to be aware of what is going on. We just cannot go ahead with these procedures. We are drawing on experience. The fact is that so many blunders have been made in the past. That is why this second report went into such detail, and that is why we are asking the Government to give consideration to this matter even at this late hour. Might I say that even the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser), at the time he announced the Government's uranium decision, recognised the environmental sensitivity of Koongarra. On 25 August 1977 he stated:
That deposit has particular environmental problems because of the sensitivity and fragility of the environment of the area and the location of the deposit upstream of a major wetlands area.
That was said by the Prime Minister on 25 August 1977. I ask the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment (Mr Wilson) to tell the House what has happened since the time the Prime Minister made his statement on 25 August 1977. What has changed? The fact is that nothing has changed. The Government is under pressure from the wealthy mining companies. That is the situation. This Government really represents corporate capital. It does not represent the Australian

3064 REPRESENTATIVES 3 and 4 June 1981

Koongarra Project Area Bill

people and our national heritage, it represents corporate capital particularly that of foreign corporations. I ask you, Mr Deputy Speaker, as a Queenslander, and no doubt you are proud of it, to make sure that you express your views within your own party room. Unless you do, they will do more of this. You will find that next the Government will be into exploiting the mineral aspects of the Great Barrier Reef.
As with so many of the Government's promises on uranium, it now seems that the interests of the mining companies will override environmental considerations. I think I have set the case clear on that situation. It is no good the Minister simply saying that there will be no problems because the company plans a 'no water release policy'. If there were a breach in the tailings dam at Koongarra the consequences for the wetlands in the South Alligator River could be very serious, and I give honourable members an example. There is a history of this sort of thing occurring in uranium mines. Only two years ago a modern tailings dam at the United States uranium mine at Church Rock in New Mexico burst, releasing radioactive water across Indian lands. The implications of this spill were so great that the water supply of southern California was contaminated. The consequences of such a spill occurring at Koongarra would be devastating for the Kakadu National Park. The Minister has said that his Department has examined the Koongarra environmental impact statement and the overall physical consequences of the proposal will fall within acceptable and manageable limits. That is a pretty vague statement which entails a considerable amount of judgment. Let them make the statement available for assessment and scrutiny by the Parliament. The statement has not been put before the Parliament or the public. Why does not the Minister make it available to the public? Let independent scientists examine it to see whether they agree with the Department's assessment. Let the public judge whether the limits of environmental damage are acceptable and manageable.

These are judgments which should be made explicitly and in an open fashion, not behind the closed doors of a government department. After all, it is a part of the world heritage with which we are dealing, as well as a part of our national heritage. That is what is involved.
The Minister has said that the Bill does not mean that the Government has approved the Koongarra project. That statement rings very hollow indeed. Why has the Government gone to such extraordinary lengths to thwart the will of the people if it has not made up its mind to give the go-ahead to this uranium project? It sounds like those hollow assurances of 1976, when the Fox inquiry was still under way. The Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony) came into this House day after day and said: 'We have not made up our minds yet'. The fact is that the Government, and particularly the Deputy Prime Minister, are doing everything in their power to get the Koongarra project under way. In doing so, once again it has thrown out the key recommendations of the Fox report and gone back to one of its undertakings of 1977, as expressed in the Prime Minister's statement.
It is a pity that the Government does not wake up to the real situation in the nuclear industry throughout the world. Up until the election of President Mitterrand, France was the only Western country where there had been any consistent growth in the nuclear industry. Now even that is in doubt. I am sure that the socialist government in France will eventually respond to people's questioning of and opposition to nuclear programs. Only last week President Mitterrand announced that a planned 6,000 megawatt nuclear power scheme in Brittany was to be cancelled. That is the pattern throughout the world. To give some indication of the cancellations of new orders for nuclear power stations which have occurred since 1972, I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard a table setting out the position.

Mr UREN-I thank the House. I will run briefly through the list. In 1979 there were 14 cancellations and in 1980 there were 16. If we look at Japan, we see that for the three-year period no new units were ordered. In West Germany no new units have been ordered since 1976. In France five units were ordered in 1980. In the UK four units were ordered, and in the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development countries a total of nine were ordered, of course, that number is just the British and French orders. REQUEST FOR DETAILED INFORMATION
