Telephone and Television Services in Remote Areas
(Question No. 1154)
Mr Campbell asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 8 Apri11981:
(I) Will the microwave link repeater stations that are to be located through the Kimberley region, Western Australia, be of assistance to persons living in remote situations in obtaining telephone and television services.
(2) In particular, is it planned to locate a repeater station near Turkey Creek, Western Australia.

(3) Will it be possible for the community there to utilise this repeater station for connection to television and STD telephone services.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The microwave link planned to be established between Port Hedland-Broome-Derby by the end of 1982 and between Derby-Halls Creek-Kununurra by the end of 1983 will provide a reliable trunk system which will support the provision and continued development of telephone services throughout the region.
(2) A repeater station on the Derby-Halls CreekKununurra microwave link will be located 10 km from Turkey Creek.
(3) The microwave link in general, and the Turkey Creek repeater in particular, will be utilised in the provision of STD telephone services to the community at Turkey Creek.
Further to the answers given above, my Department advises that, notwithstanding the installation of the microwave link to the Kimberley region, the proposed domestic communications satellite system may well prove to be the most effective means of providing adequate national television service to the North West of Western Australia generally and, in the context of this question, to the Turkey Creek area.
Television and Telephone Senices: Dampier Land Peninsula
(Question No. 1156)
Mr Campbell asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 8 April 1981:
(1) Has he received representations from the community of the Dampier Land Peninsula, Western Australia requesting television and telephone services for their area.
(2) Has he or his Department replied to these representations; if so, what was the nature of this reply.
(3) Is it possible to have television and telephone services extended to the Dampier Land Peninsula during the installation of the microwave link to the Kimberley region.
(4) Has a decision been made as to which route the microwave link into and through the Kimberley region will take.
(5) If so, will the route selected be of assistance to the people of the Dampier Land Peninsula in their aspirations for telephone and television services.
(6) If the decision has not yet been reached, will he ensure that Telecom Australia investigates the possibility of routing the microwave link to be of some assistance to the Dampier 1 qmt Peninsula.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) Yes. Replies have acknowledged the lack of adequate television reception and have explained the Government's objectives and problems in providing an adequate television service throughout Australia.
(3) The microwave link planned to be established between Port Hedland-Broome-Derby by the end of 1982 and between Derby-Halls Creek-Kununurra by the end of 1983 will provide a reliable trunk system which will support the provision and continued development of telephone services throughout the region, including the Dampier Land Peninsula.
With regard to the provision of television services to the Dampier Land Peninsula, my Department has been requested to consider the communities in that area for inclusion in a

Answers to Questions

future television planning program. However, the proposed domestic communications satellite system may well prove to be the most effective means of providing adequate national television service to those areas, installation of the microwave link to the Kimberley region notwithstanding.
(4) The microwave links between Port Hedland-Derby and Derby-Kununurra will generally follow the Great Northern Highway.
(5) and (6) See (3) above.
Telecom Particular Person Call Service
(Question No. 1157)
Mr Campbell asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 8 April 1981 :
(1) Has Telecom Australia recently altered its operation of the Particular Person Call service.
(2) If so, does this alteration include the dropping of the facility of the caller being able to ask the operator to reach the particular person called and, if that person is not available when the call is placed, to advise when available.
(3) Does this alteration discriminate against Kimberley residents who are not yet equipped with an STD service.
(4) Will he undertake to have Telecom reconsider its position on this matter if this alteration has come into effect.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1, 2, 3 and 4) Telecom Australia has advised that in the interests of improved efficiency and service to customers the operating procedures in relation to the Particular Person service were varied from February last by introducing a 'Try Again' service in place of the former 'Left Word' procedure. The 'Left Word' procedure relied on the person answering the called number advising the wanted person that the caller was wishing to make contact. Telecom was not able to ensure that the wanted person received this advice. With the 'Try Again' procedure Telecom retains responsibility for the connection of Particular Person unavailable calls.
When the wanted person is unavailable the operator will advise the caller, giving all available information. The caller may then-
inform the operator to keep trying the call until the wanted person is available. In this case the operator will, if possible, ascertain when the person will be available or will attempt the call again in 30 minutes.
speak to an alternative party at the same number. if it is known that the wanted person can be reached at another number anywhere in Australia, ask the operator to attempt connection to that number. advise the operator to hold the call until he wishes it to be tried again. cancel the call.
The service outlined is available to aU telephone customers and does not in any way discriminate against customers who do not have STD access.
Aid to China
(Question No. 1174)
Mr Howe asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 9 April 1981 :
(I) Is he able to state whether in China 190 million people have been affected by one of the worst famines in that country's history and that 25 million people are close to starvation.

(2) Is he also able to state whether a United Nations team which returned from China at the commencement of April 1981 reported that £stg300m and 1.5 million tons of food were needed to ease conditions in drought-ridden Hebei Province, south of Peking and flooded Hubei Provitlce, west of Shanghai.
(3) If so, has the Chinese Government appealed to the Australian Government for assistance.
(4) What was the Government's response to any appeal.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Accordingto a report issued by the United Nations Disaster Relief Organisation (UNDRO) which visited China from 12 to 31 January 1981 at the request onhe Chinese Government, the flood and drought disasters experienced in 1980 affected some 173 million people in seven provinces. However, the most serious effects of the flood and drought disaster were in Hubei and Hebei Provinces respectively, where a total of 43.2 million people were affected with over 20 million most seriously affected.
(2) The UNDRO report, which is based on detailed information provided by the Chinese authorities, did not seek a level of monetary commitment from international donors but did outline a number of requirements for Hubei and Hebei Provinces including food, clothing, quilts/covers, seeds, fertiliser, medicines and pesticides. The food requirements were listed as follows:
Rice-SOO,OOO metric tonnes; Wheat-600,000 metric tonnes; Maize/Sorghum-440,000 metric tonnes; Soya Beans-7S,000 metric tonnes; Cooking Oil-l0,000 metric bnnes; Milk Powder-2,000 metric tonnes.
I am unaware of any further United Nations team which returned from China at the beginning of Apri11981, although I am informed that a UNICEF representative made a visit to the disaster affected areas in April to monitor the distribution of assistance being provided by that organisation.
(3) The Chinese Government sought international aid through UNDRO which approached the Australian Government for assistance.
(4) Bearing in mind the humanitarian aspects of the disasters, the Australian "Government appt6ved the following response to the Chinese appeal:
0) the provision ofllledical supplies, valued at $100,000;
and
(ii) the provision of 10,000 tonnes of wheat currently valued at $1.Sm.
Both the Chinese authorities and UNDRO have been informed of Australia's offer, and arrangements are being made for the delivery of our assistance in accordance with the wishes of the Chincse Government.
Age Pensions in OECD Countries
(Question No. 1178)
Dr Klugman asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 8 April 1981:
(1) Is he able to state (a) how many persons are (i) in the work force and (ii) eligible for age pensions and (b) the ratio of those referred to in part (a) (i) to those referred to in part (a) (ii) in OECD countries; ifso, what are the details.
(2) Where estimates are available what is the ratio 'for the years 1990 and 2000 (assuming no change in eligibility for age pensions).

Answers to Questions

3 and 4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3071

Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) and (2) Information on the number of people eligible for the age pension in each OECD country is not readily available. The eligibility requirement for age pensions differs from country to country. In addition, apart from Australia and New Zealand, all OECD countries have contributory systems as an element in their age pension schemes under which entitlement is related to a person's contributions (or an employer's on their behalf) during their working life and in most cases to past earnings.
The OECD has produced a study ('Demographic Trends 1950-1990', OECD 1979) which gives an estimate of the labour force and number of persons 65 and over in each member country for 1975, and projections up to 1990-a copy of the study is available in the Parliamentary Library. Projections of the population aged 65 and over and the labour force for most countries including OECD members for the years 1975, 1980, 1985, 1990 and 2000 are contained in 'Labour Force Estimates and Projections, 1950-2000', International Labour Office, Geneva, second edition, 1977. It must be noted that these projections are subject to a number of reservations and limitations, and given differing eligibility criteria (including different age criteria between countries) it is not possible to assume that persons of a certain age and over will be those eligible for age pensions.

Department of Communications: Relocation of Staff in Canberra
(Question No. 1636)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) Did the Annual Report of his Department for 1979-80 state that the new organisation involved a greater proportion of the staff being relocated in Canberra for greater efficiency.
(2) If so, will he detail the savings and areas of greater efficiency resulting from the relocation of staff to Canberra.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) The comments in the Annual Report of my Department for 1979-80 referred to by the honourable member relate to a new organisation which came into effect after June 1980. This was the result of recommendations made in reviews of the Department's functions conducted in conjunction with

the Public Service Board. The new organisation changed a number of elements of the Department's structure and brought together in one location those elements which are involved in preparing policy advice to the Government on the various responsibilities within my portfolio. Prior to the reorganisation, these policy elements of the Central Office of the Department were fragmented between Sydney, Melbourne and Canberra. Co-location in Canberra has overcome areas of duplication of work and has resulted in more effective coordination and communication between the interrelated policy elements of the Department.

Telephone Calls by Staff: Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs
(Question No. 1884)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) Has his attention been drawn to a report in the West Australian of 18 July 1979, page 37 that London metropolitan police reduced its telephone bill by $985 000, or about 20 per cent by use of a monitoring device whJch recorded length, time and destination of telephone calls and the extension from which the call was made.
(2) Are personal telephone calls, particularly trunk and STD calls, made by staff of his Department controlled; if so, in what way.
(3) Has any consideration been given to the introduction of monitoring devices for telephone calls in his Department; if so, what was the outcome of the consideration.

3074 REPRESENTATIVES 3 and 4 June 1981

Answers to Questions

Mr Macphee-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Departmental instructions make it clear that phones are for official use. Local personal calls are to be kept to a minimum and use of phones for such calls is to be monitored by supervisors. All personal trunk calls are to be booked through the switch board and paid for on completion.
(2) In the Department's Central Office calls on each extension are separately recorded on a metering device. This information is regularly monitored and provides a control on the use of telephones. STD facilities are only made available to officers who have a need for frequent use of this facility.
In all other offices of the Department with the exception of Melbourne, the STD facility is only made available to senior officers. In Melbourne, the Department is not responsible for the PABX and STD access has not yet been controlled. It is understood that action is in train to implement STD barring on this PABX.
(3) The individual metering arrangements referred to above enable the Department to exercise control over the degree of use of telephones. Preliminary consideration was given to the use of additional monitoring devices but the department did not pursue the matter because of the technical limitations of the currently available equipment. Further STD barring is under consideration.
2,4-D and 2,4,5-T
(Question No. 1931)
Mr Howe asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 28 April 1981:
(1) Has his attention been drawn to a Swedish study published in the British Journal of Industrial Medicine, linking exposure to the herbicides 2,4-D and 2,4,5-T with substantially increased risk of developing cancer of the soft tissues.
(2) Does the study show that the increase in risk is seventeenfold; if so, is he prepared to ban the use of these highly toxic chemicals.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) This further contribution from the Swedish group does report a seventeenfold increase in the relative risk of softtissue sarcomata and exposure to 2,4,5-T. In addition, it also reports increased risks associated with a number of similar chemicals.
This study is clearly important and its results cannot be ignored. However, confirmation in other populatio.ns is urgently needed to help assess the possible causal significance of the reported association.
The Public Health Advisory Committee (PHAC) at its recent meeting on 9-10 April 1981 considered the report and agreed that an investigation of cliloroplienoxy compounds

should be undertaken in Australia. It has been proposed that a special working group, including experts in pathology, epidemiology, toxicology and oncology, should be convened to consider the implications of this Swedish study and other recently published reports.
No further comment is possible until this further expert assessment has been made of the study and its implications.
Borrowing by States
(Question No. 1968)
Mr Willis asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 28 April 1981:
(I) What has been the total amount of borrowing by each of the States under the infrastructure borrowing program in each year since the program began and what is the expected borrowing for 1980-81.
(2) How much of this borrowing by each State in each year has been from overseas, and what proportion of the total sum borrowed is this figure in each case.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The total amounts borrowed under the infrastructure program in 1978-79 and in 1979-80 and the estimated total amounts to be borrowed in 1980-81 by authorities in each State are published in Budget Paper No.7, Table 90, page 127.
(2) In 1978-79, overseas borrowings comprised $143m (or 96 per cent) of the $149m borrowed under the infrastructure program in that year by State authorities. The corresponding figures for 1979-80 are $322m (or 81 per cent) out of a total of $399m. State-by-State details are confidential to Loan Council. Information on the amounts borrowed by authorities in each State under the infrastructure borrowing program for 1980-81 and the proportion of the total borrowed overseas will be available after the end of the financial year and it is expected that it will be included in the 1981-82 Budget papers.

World Council ofIndigenous Peoples
(Question No. 2071)

I have no information as to whether indigenous peoples from Malaysia or the Soviet Union have at this point sought membership or expressed interest in doing so.

Exhibition of Ancient Treasures
(Question No. 547)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Enviroment, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:
Is the exhibition of ancient treasures dealing with the life and times of Alexander the Great (the Greek Exhibition) to be brought to Queensland.
Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Greek Government has agreed in principle to send to Australia an exhibition of ancient treasures dealing with the

Answers to Questions

3 and 4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3077

life and times of Alexander the Great. Timing of such an exhibition will be decided later following the end of the exhibition's current tour of the United States and after discussions with the Supreme Greek Archaeological Committee and other appropriate Greek Government services. On present commitments the exhibition will be in North America until 1983.
Apart from objects lent by Greek Museums, the exhibition will include objects lent from European and American public and private collections. The exhibition shows the superb craftsmanship and artistry of ancient Macedonia from the time of Alexander the Great (336-323 BC) and the Hellenistic era he introduced.
The International Cultural Corporation of Australia, a company recently set up with financial assistance from the Commonwealth Government to undertake important international cultural projects, will be invited to manage the exhibition and it would be the responsibility of that company to consider venues for the exhibition in consultation with the Greek authorities.
I am advised that large scale international art exhibitions require a high level of private sponsorship and although it is the policy of the International Cultural Corporation of Australia to encompass all States within its programme, implementation of such policies is determined mainly by the availability of funds. In bringing the Greek Exhibition to Australia the Corporation will be seeking private sponsorship to the order of $400,000.
I am further advised that another factor in determining where major exhibitions are to be shown is the availability of suitable venues. The conditions of loans specified by the countries concerned include stringent environmental control in display galleries and the best possible security surveillance equipment. In the past such conditions have limited the display of certain major collections. In this regard I am informed, however, that the Queensland Art Gallery, Brisbane, has mounted the Pompeii AD79 Exhibition and the Century of Modern Masters Exhibition. That Gallery will also mount several major international art exhibitions in the future including Aboriginal Australia, William Strutt and the Chinese Classical Paintings Exhibition.

Birds: Smuggling
(Question No. 989)
Mr Good luck asked the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, upon notice, on 1 April 1981 :
(1) What species of birds can be exported from Australia. (2) Is he able to say whether some birds which are destroyed as pests in Australia would create a lucrative market to overseas ornithologists. (3) Are many exotic birds smuggled out of Australia.
Mr Moore-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(1) The exportation of birds native to Australia is only permitted in terms of the Customs (Prohibited Exports) Regulations:
(i) to approved publicly operated zoos for zoological display purposes;
(ii) to approved scientific institutions for bona fidc research; and
(iii) as household pets subject to certain stringent conditions.
The conditions relating to the export of household pets are as follows:
(a) the exporter is departing from Australia with the intention of taking up permanent residence in another country;
(b) he has been resident in Australia for not less than three years prior to departures; and
(c) he has owned and kept the specimen as a household pet for not less than two years prior to departure.
The concession may be allowed once only, either to an individual householder or to one member of a family unit, and normally may be granted in respect of one specimen of a species ordinarily kept as a household pet.
(2) No, but Australia's export policy allows certain overseas scientific institutions to obtain from approved Australian institutions specimens of native birds for bona fide study and research purposes. These specimens are available to trained ornithologists for serious scientific research work.
The second Report of the House of Representatives Standing Committee on Environment and Conservation dealing with trafficking in Fauna in Australia made a recommendation in favour of government controlled exports of inter alia wild trapped common and pest species of birds. After consideration of this recommendation the Council of Nature Conservation Ministers (CONCOM), which comprises the States and Federal conservation Ministers, decided that no action should be taken to relax the existing policy.
(3) It is known that birds of species native to Australia, some of which by reason of their rarity or endangered status may be described as exotic, are available for sale in other countries. Where possible all information concerning such

Answers to Questions

3 and 4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3079

cases is investigated with a view to identifying the source of exportation and taking appropriate action.
Australia Post Courier Service: Advertising
(Question No. 1031)
Mr Groom asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 2 April 1981:
(1) What sum was spent by Australia Post in advertising the Australian Post Courier Service during (a) 1979-80, (b) the period 1 July 1980 to 31 March 1981 and (c) what is the estimated sum to be spent during 1980-81.
(2) When was Courier-Fax established and what is its purpose.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Australia Post advertises its services nationally and at State and local levels. The form of advertising can vary widely (eg, television advertising, press advertising, brochures) and cover more than one service.
While there is some difficulty in determining precise details of expenditure for a particular service such as Australia Post Courier, the following amounts have been calculated for the periods nominated:
1 July 1979-30 June 1980-$71,000 I July 1980-31 March 1981-$184,000 I July 1980-30 April 1981-$187,000.
In accordance with the decision arising from the Government's Review of Commonwealth Functions, that the Australia Post Courier service be abolished, all advertising in respect of this service ceased with the announcement of that decision. (2) The Courier-Fax service was introduced, in conjunction with Telecom Australia, on 30 July 1979. The service is designed to meet the needs of postal customers for a fast and reliable means of transmitting facsimile copies of documents.
In view of the abolition of the Australia Post Courier ser-- vice, the future of Courier-Fax is currently being reviewed.

Non-official Postmasters
(Question No. 1075)
Mr Wallis asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 7 April 1981 :
(1) When were the rates paid to non-official postmasters last reviewed by (a) Australia Post and (b) Telecom Australia where manual exchanges operate.
(2) What is the basis for establishing the rates paid to nOnofficial postmasters by Australia Post and Telecom Australia.
(3) Are these rates adjusted at regular intervals to compensate for the effects of inflation; if so, at what periods.

3080 REPRESENTATIVES 3 and 4 June 1981

Answers to Questions

Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) 30 June 1980.
(2) The Business Allowance (the major payment made to non-official postmasters and postmistresses) is determined by the level of postal, and postal agency work, conducted at a particular office and is based on the rate of payment to official staff performing similar duties.
Work performed by the non-official postmaster as an agent of Telecom Australia is included and paid as part of the Business Allowance.
Telecom Australia pays an additional after-hours exchange attendance allowance which is based on the pay rates of official staff.
Australia Post pays additional allowances for such duties as cleaning and after hours attendance and these are based also on the rate of payment to official staff. Other allowances for the provision of accommodation, heating and lighting for postal facilities, and performance of outdoor services (for example, clearance of street posting boxes), are based on negotiations with the individual non-official postmaster or postmistress.
(3) All allowances are reviewed annually and adjusted if necessary. In addition, the Business Allowance and the afterhours exchange attendance allowance are adjusted in line with National Wage Case decisions, the most recent of which was handed down on 8 May 1981.

Television and Film Violence
(Question No. 1185)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 8 April 1981:
(1) Has his attention been drawn to a recently published statement by a Queensland Uniting Church minister calling for a review of television and film violence, following the

Answers to Questions

3 and 4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3081

attempted assassination of the President of the United States of America, Mr Ronald Reagan.
(2) Has his attention also been drawn to the statement by a lecturer in Psychology at North Brisbane College of Advanced Education, Father Alan Soares, that violence in the community could probably be traced back to the portrayal of violence on television.
(3) What are the present procedures and guidelines for review of violent content in (a) television programs and (b) films.
(4) Does the Government hold the view that these guidelines are adequate.
(5) In what censoring priority does the Government place (a) explicit sexual content, (b) physical violence content, (c) dangerous and reckless driving content and (d) profanity in (i) television programs and (ii) films.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) Yes.
(3) All imported television program material including cinema films for use in television, and those Australian programs produced outside the control of television stations are examined and classified by the Film Censorship Board in terms of Television Program Standards determined by the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal. The standards contain provisions relating to the portrayal of violence in programs and set down a system of classifications ('G', 'PGR', and 'AO') based on the time of day of transmission and the expected composition of the audience at these times. Television stations are expected to observe these provisions of the Standards in the production and scheduling of Australian material.
In order to ensure that all stations, commercial, national and multi-cultural, maintain a consistent approach to program classification a Classification Consultative Committee has been established consisting of representatives of the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal, the Film Censorship Board, the Australian Broadcasting Commission, the Federation of Australian Commercial Television Stations and the Special Broadcasting Service. The committee is designing a uniform censorship classification system to cover all Australian and imported television material and this will be incorporated in amended Standards being devised by the Tribunal.
The classification of films for use in the cinema is the responsibility of the Film Censorship Board which is under the control of my colleague, Senator the Honourable Peter Durack QC., the Attorney-General.
(4) Yes.
(5) The Government has confidence in the work of the Film Censorship Board and the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal and does not set down priorities as to the view to be taken of various aspects of programming.

Travel Agencies
(Question No. 1627)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Industry and Commerce, upon notice, on 9 April 1981 :
(1) What Australian travel agencies have collapsed in the last 3 years.
(2) What action has been taken or is contemplated by the Government to protect Australians travelling or about to travel.
Sir Phillip Lynch--The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The following travel agencies operating in Australia are known to have collapsed during the last three years:

(A) Licensed Travel Agents (NSW)
(i) Ta Khattar (Consolidated Travel Services) (ii) M. and T. Fragakis (iii) AUS Student Travel Pty Ltd (iv) Kacar Entl'Orprises Pty Ltd (Contact Travel) (v) Adria Travel Agency Pty Ltd (vi) Milsons Point World Travel Pty Ltd (vii) Trail Finders Pty Ltd (viii) Comserve (No. 522) Pty Ltd (Paddington Inter-
national Travel)
(B) Unlicensed Operators (NSW)
(i) Travellers Facilities Ply Ltd (ii) M. Bartlett (Air Pacific) (iii) Port Royale Charters Pty Ltd (iv) See Australia First Pty Ltd
(C) Agents in other States/Territories
(i) Kings Travel (SA) (ii) Blue Sky Travel (ACT)
(D) Australian Offices of Overseas Operators
(i) Treasure Tours (ii) Transit Travel Pty Ltd (iii) Penn World Ltd (2) Regulation of the activities of travel agents operating in Australia falls within the responsibility of the States and Territories.
The only State/Territory to have legislated to regulate the activities of travel agents is New South Wales through its Travel Agents Registration Board. Under the New South Wales Travel Agents Act (1973) all persons, firms or corporations carrying on the business of a travel agent are required to be licensed. Members of the public have access to the travel agents compensation fund in the event of a licensed travel agent failing to account for monies entrusted to the licensee or his employee.
No other State or Territory is understood to be intending to introduce similar legislation at this time.
In 1978 the Commonwealth Government considered the introduction of legislation to be administered in conjunction with the States, to regulate the activities of travel agents operating in Australia, but as a result of objections raised by the States and industry and following advice from the Travel and Tourist Industry Advisory Council, decided n'ot to proceed with the legislation.
In deciding not to proceed with the legislation the Commonwealth had had regard to the degree of protection for the travelling public which already existed, including Commonwealth and State laws relating to protection of the consumer, the regulation of corporate bodies and crimes matters generally.
Commonwealth and State Consumer Affairs Ministers recently reviewed the desirability of licensing travel agents and decided that it should be left to each jurisdiction to take whichever action it felt appropriate.
The Australian Federation of Travel Agents is currently investigating the possibility of establishing a self-regulatory consumer protection scheme. The objective is to design, fund and administer a traveller's protection scheme which would provide the public/travellers with security in the case of financial collapse experienced by member retail travel agents, tour wholesalers and general sales agents.
Consular assistance from Australia'8 overseas missions and posts is of course available to Australians travelling abroad who find themselves stranded or in distress as a result of circumstances beyond their control including the collapse of travel agencies.

Lucas Heights Atomic Energy Establishment
(Question No. 2003)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister representing the Minister for National Development and Energy, upon notice, on 29 April 1981:
Were requirements set down by the Australian Atomic Energy Commission around the Lucas Heights establishment, NSW, drawn up with local conditions in mind only, or were they drawn up with reference to regulations set down by (a) the International Committee for Radiation Protection (b) the Environmental Protection Agency or (c) any other international agency governing safety requirements regarding nuclear reactors.
Mr Anthony-The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourabkmember's question:
The requirements adopted by the Australian Atomic Energy Commission (AAEC) for the research Establishment at Lucas Heights were drawn up with local conditions in mind, and incorporated advice from the United Kingdom. The requirements complied as appropriate with the recommendations of the International Committee for Radiation Protection and are compatible with the general safety recommendations published by the International Atomic Energy Agency.
The Environmental Protection Agency, which was established in 1970 subsequent to the formulation of the AAEC requirements, is a United States Government organisation having no direct relevance to nuclear requirements in Australia.

(5) Did the Commonwealth Government receive an offer in October 1980 of the sale of 2 aircraft specifically developed to fight bushfires; if so, what response was made to this offer.
Mr Malcolm Fraset-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) An offer was made on 23 September 1980 to provide on lease one Canadair CL 215 aircraft for 3 months at a cost in the region of$515,000.

The Canadair offer was put to the then Minister for Science and the Environment and he replied on 24 October noting that the CSIRO study was intended to obtain essential,.objective information to evaluate the role of aircraft in fighting bushfires and that this objective would be unlikely to be satisfied by ad hoc demonstrations on uncontrolkd fires.

(5) See the ans-.yer by the Minister for Transport on 13 May 1981 to Question on Notice 814 (House of Representatives Hansard, p. 2389).

Social Service Benefits
(Question No.1067)
Mr Milton asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 7 April 1981:
(1) Will the Minister define what is meant when the Minister refers to 'people in genuine need' in relation to proposed cuts in social service benefits.
(2) What criteria are needed to establish 85 per cent incapacity for persons claiming invalid pensions.
Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(l) I do not understand the reference to 'proposed cuts in social service benefits'. The term 'people in genuine need' refers to people who are unable to provide for themselves from their own resources.
(2) On 7 May 1981 revised guidelines in relation to medical evaluation for invalid pensions were issued to Commonwealth Medical Officers.
The revised guidelines are:
Medical Evaluation
(a) Eligibility
To be eligible for an invalid pension a person must be permanently incapacitated for work to the extent to not less than 85 per cent or must be permanently blind.
(b) Assessment of permanence
An incapacity is considered permanent if no fundamental or marked change for the better can be expected in a person's condition in the future.
(c) Assessment ofincapacity
Incapacity is defined by the degree of permanent incapacity of a person compared with the capacity for work which that person would have had, but for his incapacity.

The comparison is to be made with regard to all work that could reasonably be regarded by the assessor as being within the claimant's capacity.
Assessment of incapacity is made having regard to the degree of the claimant's impairment which, together with relevant facts about the applicant such as age, sex, education, lack of skills and personal disabilities, constitutes incapacity. It is important to recognise that it is permanent incapacity and not permanent impairment that is relevant for invalid pension purposes.
Permanent impairment-This is a purely medical condition. Permanent impairment is an anatomic or functional abnormality of loss after maximal medical rehabilitation has been achieved, which abnormality or loss the Commonwealth Medical Officer considers stable or non-progressive at the time evaluation is made. It is always a basic consideration in the evaluation ofpermanent incapacity.
Permanent incapacity-This is not a purely medical condition. A person is permanently incapacitated or under a permanent incapacity when his actual or presumed ability to engage in gainful employment is reduced or absent because of impairment which, in turn, mayor may not be combined with other factors.
While fluctuations in the labour market are to be disregarded in the assessment of the applicant's capacity for work, that capacity must be assessed in the light of work of kinds that (subject to such fluctuations) are available in Australia, regardless of whether or not they are available at places reasonably accessible to the applicant at his current place of residence. However, age, sex, education, lack of relevant skills and personal disabilities are factors to be taken into account, together with impairment, in determining the capacity of an individual applicant for work that is not so reasonably accessible.
The fact that a severely handicapped applicant might be able to get work at a time of full or over-full employment because of the scarcity of labour would not disentitle him if he was unlikely to get work at any

3088 REPRESENTATIVES 3 and 4 June 1981

Answers to Questions

reasonable foreseeable time given the general level of demand for labour in Australia.
Self-inflicted incapacity brought about for the purpose of obtaining a pension disqualifies a person from pension.
(d) Employment considerations
While it is not generally possible for a person engaged in employment to be considered as being permanently incapacitated for work all relevant factors should be considered where a person is engaging in some form of employment, including
the nature of employment (part time, sheltered, therapeutic, token);
whether employment is within the claimant's remaining capacity for work;
whether employment is compatible with the claimant's medical condition;
whether employment will continue.
The Department of Social Security may request a review of medical entitlement if a pensioner commences work.
(e) Other considerations
A person who has lost the use of both arms or legs may be considered to be permanently incapacitated, independent of their personal and employment circumstances, even when engaged in some limited employment. A similar assessment may be made if a person has suffered some other permanent impairment which is as broadly disruptive of his capacity for work as the loss of both arms or legs would be.
Youth Housing
(Question No. 1068)
Mr Scott asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 7 April 1981:
(I) Has the Minister's attention been drawll to a report on the problems of youth housing circulated to the media ill Adelaide on 23 March 1981 which reportedly represented the views of 30 organisations, including Commonwealth and State Government Departments and community service organisations such as Emergency Accommodation Services for Youth at the YWCA and Saint Vincent de Paul.
(2) Is it a fact that in South Australia there are an estimated 6,000 homeless young persons.
(3) Has the Government been approached by the South Australian Government for an increase in funding under the Homeless Persons Assistance Act.
(4) Will the Government consider adopting the recommendations contained in the report by the South Australian Government Working Party on Youth Housing, particularly recommendations 5 (i), 5 (ii) and 5 (iii) which respectively relate to raising the rate of unemployment benefit, providing supplementary assistance to young unemployed persons living independently and increasing the unemployment rate for persons 16 and 17 years of age to that of the single adult rate if they have been living independently for a suitable qualifying period.
Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) Yes. My attention has been drawn to a Press release with attached background material, dealing with the problems of youth housing, which was issued on 23 March 1981 by

a South Australian organisation called 'Shelter'. This is an organisation whose members are individuals concerned about the problems of homeless people. Many of the members are employees of welfare organisations involved in providing accommodation services, and some are employees of Government Departments. These latter are members of the organisation in their capacity as private citizens, and are not representatives of their employing Departments.
(2) A report called 'Survey of Youth Homelessness in Metropolitan Adelaide', December 1979, which was released by the Council for Homeless Persons (SA), estimated that there were 6,000 homeless young persons in Metropolitan Adelaide. Another report, entitled 'Youth Housing', which was published in June 1980 by the South Australian Council of Social Service, estimated that 9,000 young people seek housing assistance each year in Metropolitan Adelaide.
It is difficult, however, to estimate accurately the homeless youth popUlation. The estimates given above were calculated from the number of homeless young people who contacted some welfare accommodation agencies in a given survey period, usually one week. An overestimate of the population size will, of course, occur if a homeless young person approaches more than one accommodation agency during the survey period, whereas there will be an underestimate if a young person seeking temporary accommodation does not contact any welfare agency. In addition, the surveys did llot contact every possible agency which might have received requests for accommodation assistance.
The government nevertheless accepts that these estimates point to a problem on a scale which warrants serious concern. The Commonwealth has therefore offered assistance to the States to provide additional facilities for emergency accommodation for young people up to 18 years. This assistance is provided through the Youth Services Scheme and is jointly cost shared with each State Government which administers its Commonwealth funding component. Four South Australian projects providing emergency accommodation and support for youth currently receive funding under the Scheme.
(3) There is no record of an approach by the South Australian Government for an increase in funding under the Homeless Persons Assistance Act.
(4) The recommendations in the report of the South Australian Government Working Party on Youth Housing have been noted and will be taken into account by the Government in its current Review of Improved Opportunities for Youth and in the annual Budget context.

Recycling of Paper: Department of Employment and Youth Affairs
(Question No. 1581)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
Has any action been taken by his Department to re-cycle paper; if so, what action.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Surplus or obsolete forms, letter heads and other proformas are used by Departmental staff as note and scribble pads. Bulk paper, e.g. computer print-out, is despatched to paper and pulp mills within the States for re-cycling. Waste paper is collected daily by contract cleaners engaged by the Department of Administrative Services and is disposed of in accordance with contract provisions.
There are no formal arrangements for Offices of the Commonwealth Employment Service outside metropolitan areas for the re-cycling of paper. Offices would be expected to comply with local arrangements concerning disposal of waste paper.
Recycling of Paper: Department of Veterans' Affairs
(Question No. 1592)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
Has any action been taken by the Department of Veterans' Affairs to re-cycle paper; if so, what action.
Mr Thomson-The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
In leased premises, the removal of unclassified waste paper is undertaken by cleaners employed by the Department of Administrative Services and recycling is usually arranged by the owners in conjunction with the Department of Administrative Services. In departmentally-owned premises, including hospitals and other institutions, arrangements exist with charitable organisations and private contractors for waste paper to be collected for recycling.
Classified waste is normally burnt or shredded under Departmental control or supervision.
The Department of Veterans' Afi'airs is conscious of the current emphasis on the need to recycle waste materials and the trend for community involvement in such projects. In all areas where waste is produced, investigations are carried out into the possibility of recycling and the means available in the area concerned.

Department of the Capital Territory Representation at Meeting
(Question No. 1781)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) What was the cost to his Department of being represented at the annual meeting of the Committee of Heads of Australian Herbaria and the Royal Australian Institute of Parks and Recreation in 1979-80.
(2) What benefits resulted from this representation.

Answers to Questions

3 and 4 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 3093

Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The cost of the Department being represented at the annual meeting of the Committee of Heads of Australia Herbaria held in Alice Springs from 24 to 29.9.1979 was $526.80 and the cost of represen,tation at the Royal Australian Institute of Parks and Recreation held in Brisbane from 7 to 12.10.1979 was $1,910.85.
(2) The Department benefited from representation at the meeting of the Committee of Heads of Australian Herbaria through the discussion of scientific and management matters designed to improve and expand co-operative arrangements amongst State and Commonwealth herbaria. The effective operation of herbaria is dependent upon exchange of specimens and information between institutions. By meeting together the Heads of Herbaria are also able to resolve complex taxonomic issues which cannot be dealt with readily by correspondence.
The 52nd R.A.I.P.R. Conference held in Brisbane involved general and concurrent sessions on 'Parks, Recreation & Community'. It was attended by over 400 delegates from all Australian States and overseas representing industry and educational institutions; Municipal, State and Federal Government. The advantages were the presentation, discussion and exchange of knowledge of latest policies, approaches and techniques in the parks and recreation field.

Fisheries Surveys
(Question No. 2259)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 13 May 1981:
(1) Are fisheries surveys, including studies of the demersal fish and benthic invertebrates of 8ass Strait, demersal fish in Western Australia and pelagic fish off the Northern Territory, currently being undertaken by the research vessel Hai Kung from the National Taiwanese University staffed by 10 scientists of which only 2 are Australian.
(2) If so, (a) why is this research program reliant on a Taiwanese research vessel, research facilities and scientific manpower and (b) cannot this type of research program draw on Australian research facilities and manpower.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The Hai Kung was in Australian waters from 26 January to 17 April 1981 during which time there were no less than two Australians on board at any time. In total, 18 Australians took part in various aspects of the research including representatives from the CSIRO, Tasmania,Victoria, South Australia, Western Australia and the Northern Territory. In some areas as many as five Australians were involved in the research at one time.
(2) (a) and (b) Australia has research programs directed towards each of the topics mentioned, conducted by Commonwealth and State Fisheries research organisations and Museums. In 1979, the CSIRO Division of Fisheries Research was strengthened by chartering the 53m research vessel Soela and increasing the numbers of research staff in order, inter alia, to further study fisheries resources in olir northern and north western waters.

For many years Australian scientists have participated in co-operative research cruises with their overseas colleagues on an opportunity basis. Such co-operation strengthens links be-
tween scientists and provides an additional and cost effective means for Australia to broaden its understanding of the waters surrounding the continent.

Fisheries Research
(Question No. 2260)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 13 May 1981:
(1) Which Australian States are presently collaborating with the Commonwealth in respect of the fisheries research being carried out by the Hai KUlig, a research vessel from the National Taiwan University.
(2) What is the cost to the Commonwealth for funding this research effort.
(3) Will the research findings be available to Taiwanese fishing interests, for example, the Taiwanese Provincial Fisheries Bureau, or any other commercial fishing interest in Taiwan.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Tasmania, Victoria, South Australia, Western Australia and the Northern Territory.
(2) $12,102, comprised of $7,677 in living and overtime allowances for staff and $4,425 in travel costs.
(3) Yes. A report on the research findings produced by the National Taiwan University will be provided to the Department of Primary Industry. Records of the vessel's operations were maintained by Australian participants in the expedition. The results will be made available to the public and the Australian fishing industry through fisheries magazines and scientific journals.

Control of Plague Locusts
(Question No. 2272)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 14 May 1981:
(1) Is control of plague locusts now to be entirely a State responsibility (Hansard, 30 April 1981, page 1847).
(2) What plans does the Government have for the Commonwealth employee currently associated with the Plague Locust Commission.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Control of plague locusts has always been a State responsibility. By agreement with the States, the Australian Plague Locust Commission, which consists of six Commissioners (two from the Commonwealth and one each from the contributing States) and a Director assisted by permanent staff, engages in operations designed to combat outbreaks or potential outbreaks of the Australian plague locust in New South Wales, Victoria, South Australia and Queensland, which in the opinion of the Commission are likely to result in damage to ruralindustries in another State.
(2) The general approach and procedures concerning staffing relating to the implementation of Government decisions following the Review of Commonwealth Functions have been detailed by the Public Service Board. These include the situation where a function is to be transferred to the States. Any change in Commission staffing arrangements will be in accord with these guidelines.

Storage Capacity for Wool
(Question No. 2274)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Primary Industry, upon notice, on 14 May 1981:
(1) On what basis will minimum storage capacity for wool be determined (Ha/lsard, 30 April 1981, page 1847).
(2) What action will the Government take if the minimum capacity proves to be inadequate.
(3) What would be the cost of this action in comparison with maintaining existing storage capacity.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) I have been informed that the Australian Wool Corporation regards an eight months supply of wool as the minimum storage capacity required.
(2) In an emergency, by block stacking, this area could provide storage for over six million bales or about 18 months production of wool.
(3) Action foreshadowed in (2) would entail some additional costs but the maintenance of existing storage capacity may also require significant capital outlays on the repair and modernisation of some stores.

Use in Australia of Americallese
(Question No. 2319)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Administrative Services, upon notice, on 26 May 1981:
(1) Did the notice placed on the Press Gallery Notice Board in Parliament House by the Honours Secretariat on 12 May 1981 say that as a mark of respect to the late General Sir John Wilton, flags will be half-masted on all Commonwealth Buildings.
(2) Is half-masted a particular military expression; if so, did it originate from General Haig, the Secretary of State of the United States of America.
(3) Is it more accurate to say that flags will be flown at half-mast.
(4) What symbolic importance can be deduced from this apparent ad hoc adoption of Americanese.
Mr Newman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) No.
(3) No. The Oxford English Dictionary recognises 'halfmasted', and records an example of its usage in England in 1891.
(4) As the Oxford English Dictionary identifies the term in English usage, it is not, therefore 'Americanese'. The honourable member would seem to be hoist with his own petard.
Television Translators
Mr Sinclair--On 13 May 1981 (Hansard, page 2297) Mr Tuckey asked me as Minister for Communications the following question, without notice:
I refer the Minister of Communications to the recent amendments to the Broadcasting and Television Act which allow community groups to erect national television translators. Can the Minister advise the House of the number of applications that have been received as a result of this legislation? Can he further state when the residents of Howick and Condingup in the Esperance Shire might receive approval of their applications?
The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Expressions of interest relating to the establishment of translators which may be licensed to relay national (ABC) television programs have been received for seven such services.

The group which has expressed an interest in establishing a privately funded translator to relay national (ABC) television programs to Howick and Condingup in the Esperance Shire is presently considering the options open to it. The most expeditious of these options would appear to be donation of the transmission facility to the Government'so that it will become part of the National Broadcasting Service at the time programming commences. Under these circumstances it would not be necessary for the group to obtain a licence for the translator and the project could proceed with a minimum of delay.
Should the group decide to pursue the option of applying for a licence to operate the translator as a privately owned outlet of the National television service, it is unlikely that the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal would be able to grant a licence until well into 1982.
This delay is entirely usual and is a result of the administrative processes involved in preparation and distribution of a draft planning proposal, preparation of a final planning proposal, the calling of applications for a licence and the actuallicence inquiry by the Tribunal. In addition to the time taken for administrative proc.esses, there are statutory periods set aside to allow interested parties to comment on draft planning proposals and to allow applications for the licence to be submitted to the Tribunal.
Commonwealth Census
Mr Malcolm Fraser-On 31 March 1981 (Hansard, page 1092) Mr Les Johnson asked me a question, without notice, concerning the Commonwealth census.
The following is in answer to the honourable member's question:
A circular has been sent to all Commonwealth Departments and Authorities seeking their co-operation in the conduct of the 1981 Census, and advising that public servants will be allowed to accept employment as temporary Census field officers whilst on leave from their regular employment and to retain the additional payment for the Census work.
The Australian Bureau of Statistics has advertised widely for temporary field staff for the Census and it is open to anyone to apply. The ABS has also sought the assistance of the Commonwealth Employment Service in specifically drawing the attention of those registered with them to the availability of this work. However, despite the unemployment situation, in a number of localities response to the advertisement has been disappointing.
Although the ABS has obtained the approval of the Public Service Board to employ public servants for this work, preference will be given, as far as practicable, to the recruitment of suitable people who are not already in employment.
